10 min
Two Kinds of Possibility: Dhati and Waq'ee
Author defines two kinds of possibility with necessity conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between subjective possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) and occurrence possibility (al-imkan al-waq'ee) based on their relation to necessity.

Core Idea
Subjective possibility allows external necessity; occurrence possibility excludes all necessity.

Modal logic often treats possibility and necessity as opposites. But Islamic logicians refined this by distinguishing between what is possible in itself (dhati) and what is possible in actual occurrence (waq'ee). The difference hinges on whether the possibility is contradicted by external necessity. This lesson clarifies the two concepts and their definitions from the source text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of possibility
Locate the terms 'al-imkan al-dhati' and 'al-imkan al-waq'ee' in the Arabic. Note that the author introduces them in context of a problematic inference.
Also called 'isti'dadi' (dispositional) in al-Jurjani's quotation.
2
Extract the definition of subjective possibility
Subjective possibility means the opposite side is not necessary by essence, but could be necessary through another. In other words, the thing itself does not entail impossibility, but external factors may force it.
Example: a writer being illiterate is subjectively possible because literacy is not part of his essence, though being a writer requires literacy (external necessity).
3
Extract the definition of occurrence possibility
Occurrence possibility means the opposite side is not necessary by essence nor by another, and assuming occurrence of the matching side never leads to impossibility.
Example: an Iranian becoming Iraqi is possible because no intrinsic or extrinsic necessity prevents it.
4
Compare their scope
Subjective possibility is broader because it includes cases where external necessity applies. Occurrence possibility is stricter, requiring absolute freedom from necessity.
Al-Jurjani says subjective possibility is 'absolutely more general' than occurrence possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العام قد يصدق مع نقيض الخاص؛ فالإمكان المقابل للقسم الأول قد يصدق مع القسم الثاني من الضرورة [أولوية القسم الثاني من الضرورة والإمكان] فلو اعتبرنا في الضرورة الذاتية القسم الأول بطل استدلالنا بـ«الإمكان» على «أن الممكن لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال»؛ فإنّ كون الإنسان الكاتب مثلاً أمياً نظراً إلى ذاته أمر ممكن، مع أنه لو وقع يلزم المحال. أما إذا اعتبرنا القسم الثاني فيصح ذلك الاستدلال، لأنه إذا لم تكن ضرورة أصلاً لا : الذات ولا بغيرها، فلا يلزم من الوقوع محال. هذا الإمكان يستى بـ«الإمكان الوقوعي»، و الأول بـ«[الإمكان] الذاتي». فالأولى أن يختار القسم الثاني من الضرورة. ١. في المثال مناقشة كما ذكرنا لأن الكاتب أمّي في طفولته فلا يلزم من وقوع أميته محال. بل المثال المناسب كون الإيراني عراقياً أو الإصفهاني تبريزياً. نعم، إن فسرنا «الأمي» لا بـ«من لم يتعلم الكتابة بعده بل بـ«من لم يتعلم و لن يتعلم الكتابة، كان أمراً دائمياً وكفى في الإمكان الوقوعي. .. ما وجدنا اصطلاح «الإمكان الوقوعي» في آثار المناطقة قبل المصنف و الظاهر أنه أوّل من جعل هذا الاصطلاح، و أول من اقتبسه في من وجدنا هو الشريف الجرجاني في كتابه التعريفات: الإمكان الذاتي» هو ما لا يكون طرفه المخالف واجباً بالذات، وإن كان واجباً بالغير. الإمكان الاستعدادي» و يستى الإمكان الوقوعي أيضاً و هو ما لا يكون طرفه المخالف واجباً، لا بالذات و لا بالغير، و لو فرض وقوع الطرف الموافق لا يلزم المحال بوجه. و الأول أعم من الثاني مطلقاً. (الجرجاني، كتاب التعريفات، ص ۱۶) .. يبدو لنا أن الإمكان الوقوعي هو في مقابلة القسم الأول من الضرورة الذاتية؛ لكن المصنف يرى خلاف ما تراه.
The universal may be true with the contradictory of the particular; so the possibility corresponding to the first division may be true with the second division of necessity. [Preference of the second division of necessity and possibility] So if we consider the first division in essential necessity, our inference by 'possibility' that 'the possible from the assumption of its occurrence does not entail impossibility' becomes invalid; for that a human being who is a writer is illiterate by consideration of his essence is something possible, even though if it occurred it would entail impossibility. But if we consider the second division, that inference becomes valid, because if there is no necessity at all, neither essential nor by another, then no impossibility follows from occurrence. This possibility is called 'occurrence possibility', and the first is called '[subjective] possibility'. So it is better to choose the second division of necessity. 1. There is a discussion in the example as we mentioned because the writer is illiterate in his childhood, so no impossibility follows from the occurrence of his illiteracy. Rather, the appropriate example is an Iranian becoming an Iraqi or an Isfahani becoming a Tabrizi. Yes, if we interpret 'illiterate' not as 'one who has not learned writing after that' but as 'one who has not learned and will not learn writing', it becomes a perpetual matter and suffices for occurrence possibility. ... We did not find the term 'occurrence possibility' in the writings of logicians before the author, and it appears that he was the first to coin this term, and the first we found who quoted it is al-Sharif al-Jurjani in his book al-Ta'rifat: 'Subjective possibility is that whose opposite side is not necessarily true by essence, even if it is necessary by another. Dispositional possibility, also called occurrence possibility, is that whose opposite side is not necessarily true, neither by essence nor by another, and if the occurrence of the matching side is assumed, no impossibility follows in any way. And the first is absolutely more general than the second.' (al-Jurjani, al-Ta'rifat, p. 16) ... It appears to us that occurrence possibility corresponds to the first division of essential necessity; but the author holds the opposite view.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between possibility and necessity in logical modality. The author begins with a general principle: a universal statement can be true simultaneously with the contradictory of a particular. Then he applies this to two kinds of necessity and two kinds of possibility. He considers an inference: from possibility (that a thing is possible) we conclude that assuming its occurrence does not lead to impossibility. But this inference fails if we use the first division of essential necessity. The example: a human being who is a writer is possibly illiterate by essence, but if that occurs it would entail impossibility? Wait, the example is problematic because a writer can be illiterate in childhood—so no impossibility. He corrects the example to avoid this objection. He then distinguishes two kinds of possibility: subjective (dhati) and occurrence (waq'ee) also called dispositional (isti'dadi). He defines them via their relation to necessity: subjective: the opposite side is not necessary by essence (but could be necessary through another); occurrence: the opposite side is not necessary at all. He then cites al-Jurjani's definitions. The passage shows that the author (possibly the author of Qustas) is refining the concepts, noting that occurrence possibility corresponds to the first division of essential necessity, but the text's author disagrees with that correspondence. The teacher should note that this is a technical discussion of modal logic, distinguishing between a possibility that is merely not contradicting essence and one that is free from any external necessity that would prevent occurrence. The examples from geography (Iranian becoming Iraqi) illustrate that the change is not inherently contradictory, but the example of a writer being illiterate is flawed because a writer was once illiterate. So careful definition of terms is needed.

Key Terms
الإمكان الذاتي
الإمكان الوقوعي
الضرورة الذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which condition does the inference 'if something is possible, assuming it occurs does not imply impossibility' become valid?
A) Using subjective possibility
B) Using occurrence possibility
C) Using the first division of essential necessity
D) Considering the writer example

Key Takeaway
The distinction between subjective and occurrence possibility turns on whether external necessity is excluded; occurrence possibility is stricter and required for the standard inference that possibility implies no impossibility from occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement 'A human can fly by essence'. Determine whether this is subjective or occurrence possibility, and explain why.
?
Using al-Jurjani's definition, classify the possibility of Allah creating a stone too heavy to lift in terms of subjective and occurrence possibility.

Correcting the Problematic Example
Author corrects a problematic example for occurrence possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the example of a writer being illiterate fails as an instance of occurrence possibility and provide a correct example.

Core Idea
A flawed example can undermine logical distinction; the writer example fails because illiteracy was once true.

The author points out that the example of a human who is a writer being illiterate is not a good illustration of occurrence possibility. Why? Because illiteracy can occur at childhood without contradiction. Through this correction, the author sharpens the definition of occurrence possibility and emphasizes the need for examples that truly exclude any possibility of occurrence. This lesson examines the correction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic example
The author says: 'a human being who is a writer is illiterate by consideration of his essence is something possible, even though if it occurred it would entail impossibility.' But then notes there is a discussion: because a writer once was illiterate in childhood, so no impossibility follows from occurrence.
The contradiction arises only if 'illiterate' is taken as permanent. But if interpreted as temporary, the example fails.
2
Understand why it fails as occurrence possibility
Occurrence possibility requires that assuming the occurrence never leads to impossibility. But a writer being illiterate at some point actually occurred (childhood), so it is not a case where occurrence would be impossible. Hence it does not demonstrate occurrence possibility correctly.
The example works for subjective possibility because it is not inherently impossible, but for occurrence possibility, the writer's past shows it can occur.
3
Find the corrected examples
The author provides better examples: an Iranian becoming Iraqi or an Isfahani becoming Tabrizi. These are changes that are not part of essence and no external necessity prevents them; also they have not occurred simultaneously with the identity.
These examples involve change of nationality or locality, which are accidental and not necessary.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
If 'illiterate' is defined permanently as 'one who has not learned and will not learn writing', then it becomes a perpetual state that cannot change. In that case, assuming occurrence would involve contradiction with the definition of writer, so it would entail impossibility, making it a valid example for occurrence possibility.
This shows that careful definition of terms is crucial for logical examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the example 'a writer is illiterate' problematic for illustrating occurrence possibility?

Key Takeaway

?

?

Conditional Necessity and Temporal Possibility
Explains conditional necessity and temporal possibility with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proposition is true by temporal possibility and explain its relationship to conditional necessity.

Core Idea
Conditional necessity ties necessity to a description's continuance; its contradictory is temporal possibility.

This lesson unpacks the concept of conditional necessity, where necessity is linked to the persistence of a description. The corresponding possibility, called temporal possibility, does not entail actual possibility but only applies at a different time. An example from the text clarifies this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Define conditional necessity
Understand that conditional necessity means the necessity of a predicate for a subject is conditioned on the continuance of a certain description of the subject.
For example, 'Every writer is literate' is necessary only as long as he is a writer.
2
Identify the corresponding possibility
The possibility that corresponds to conditional necessity is temporal possibility: it is true that some individual could have the opposite predicate at some time when the description does not hold.
This is not real possibility of the actual state.
3
Apply the example from the text
The author gives the example 'Some writer is illiterate' as true by temporal possibility, because at a time when that person was not a writer, he could have been illiterate. This does not mean it is possible for a writer to be illiterate now.
The key is the time shift.
4
Contrast with regular possibility
Regular possibility (imkan 'amm) would allow the occurrence at the same time, but temporal possibility only requires a different time.
This distinction is crucial in modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الأول: في القضايا الضرورة المشروطة و الإمكان الحيني] الفصل الخامس: في الجهة ٢٨١ وكذا حكم المشروطة إذا اعتبر مدخل دوام الوصف في الضرورة، فإن الممكنة المقابلة لها لا تقتضي جواز وقوع الممكن، فإنه يصدق حينئذ بعض' الكاتب أمي بالإمكان الحيني ' لكذب قولنا: لا شيء من الكاتب بأقي' عذا الاعتبار مع امتناع الوقوع. اشبة في أخذ الوقت في الضروريات السالبة] [1] [أزلية الموضوع في الضرورة الأزلية و دوام الضرورة في الضرورة الذاتية ] ب ما قالوا على الإطلاق .١ إن الضرورة الأزلية ما تكون الذات أزلية .۲ و الذاتية ما تكون ثابتة ما دامت الذات موجودة. فيه نظر؛ فإنّ ذلك في الموجبة صحيح. و أما في السالبة: ا يصدق حينئذ: حينئذ يصدق ب. ۲. بعض - پ. «الإمكان الحيني» مأخوذ من «الحينية الممكنة» في اصطلاح الخونجي و هو نقيض المشروطة العامة'. راجع كشف الأسرار ص ۱۰۸ س ۱ و ص ۱۲۵ س ۱۶-۱۷. الكاتب: كاتب ب. ٥. منطق الملخص، ص ۱۵۱ ذات الموضوع إن كانت أزلية كان المحمول أيضاً أزليا، كقولنا الله عالم'. .٦ الذاتية ما تكون ثابتة ما دامت الذات موجودة : هذه العبارة تفسر بوجمحين: ١. الضرورة الذاتية ما تكون [الذات] ثابتة ما دامت الذات موجودة الضرورة الذاتية ما تكون الضرورة) ثابتة ما دامت الذات موجودة التفسير الأول يوافق العبارة السابقة: «الضرورة الأزلية ما تكون الذات أزلية، لكنه بدعمي لا يليق بأحد إظهاره لأن كل ذات ثابتة ما دامت الذات موجودة، سواء كانت هناك ضرورة أم لا، وكانت الضرورة أزلية أم ذاتية أم غيرهما. أما التفسير الثاني يوافق العبارات اللاحنة و أقرب كلام إلى عبارة المصنف في المتن وجدناه في مطالع الأنوار للأرموي: الضرورة الذاتية أي الحاصلة ما دامت ذات الموضوع موجودة. (مطالع الأنوار، في لوامع الأسرار في شرح مطالع الأنوار ص ۲۴۰).
Chapter One: On Propositions [Conditional Necessity and Temporal Possibility] Fifth Section: On Modality 281. And likewise the ruling of the conditional when the entry of the continuance of the description in necessity is considered, for the corresponding possibility does not entail the permissibility of the possible occurring, for then some 'the writer is illiterate' is true by temporal possibility, because of the falsity of our saying 'No writer is illiterate' in this consideration, along with the impossibility of occurrence. [Similar in taking time in negative necessities] [1] [Eternality of subject in eternal necessity and continuance of necessity in essential necessity] b. What they said absolutely: 1. Eternal necessity is that whose subject is eternal. 2. And essential necessity is that which is established as long as the subject exists. There is consideration in it; that is correct for the affirmative. But for the negative: a. then it is true: then b is true. 2. Some - p. 'Temporal possibility' is taken from 'possible temporality' in the terminology of al-Khunaji, and it is the contradictory of the universal conditional. Refer to Kashf al-Asrar p. 108 line 1 and p. 125 lines 16-17. The writer: writer b. 5. Mantiq al-Mulakhkhas, p. 151: If the subject's essence is eternal, then the predicate is also eternal, like our saying 'God is knowing'. 6. 'Essential necessity is that which is established as long as the subject exists': this phrase is interpreted in two ways: 1. Essential necessity is that whose subject stays established as long as the subject exists. 2. Essential necessity is that whose necessity stays established as long as the subject exists. The first interpretation agrees with the previous statement: 'Eternal necessity is that whose subject is eternal', but it is unworthy of anyone to show it because every subject is established as long as the subject exists, whether there is necessity or not, and whether the necessity is eternal or essential or otherwise. The second interpretation agrees with the later statements and the closest wording to the author's text we found in Matali' al-Anwar by al-Urmawi: 'Essential necessity is that which occurs as long as the subject's essence exists.' (Matali' al-Anwar, in Lawami' al-Asrar fi Sharh Matali' al-Anwar, p. 240).
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage, from the fifth section on modality, deals with conditional necessity and temporal possibility. It begins by stating that when the continuance of the description is taken into account in conditional necessity, the corresponding possibility does not imply that the possible actually can occur. For example, 'Some writer is illiterate' can be true by temporal possibility even though it is impossible for a writer to be illiterate at the same time (since a writer is defined by being literate). This is because the possibility applies at a different time. The author then moves to a critique of common definitions of eternal necessity and essential necessity. He reports that some define eternal necessity as that whose subject is eternal (e.g., God's attributes), and essential necessity as that which holds as long as the subject exists. But he finds this problematic for negative statements. He gives a quick argument using letters. Then a footnote explains that 'temporal possibility' originates from al-Khunaji and is the contradictory of the universal conditional. Finally, the text examines two interpretations of the definition of essential necessity: one that takes it as the subject existing throughout, and another as the necessity itself existing throughout. The first is rejected because it says nothing special about necessity. The second is preferred, citing al-Urmawi. This shows the precision required in defining modal terms in logic.

Key Terms
الضرورة المشروطة
الإمكان الحيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does temporal possibility (الإمكان الحيني) correspond to?
A) The contradictory of absolute necessity
B) The contradictory of conditional necessity
C) A type of temporal occurrence
D) The same as absolute possibility

Key Takeaway
Temporal possibility is the negation of conditional necessity, allowing the opposite predicate at a time when the condition does not hold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a mujtahid who is not a faqih at some point. Explain how 'some mujtahid is not a faqih' can be true by temporal possibility, and why this does not contradict the conditional necessity that 'every mujtahid is a faqih' (as long as he is a mujtahid).

5 min
Critique of Eternal and Essential Necessity Definitions
Critiques definitions of eternal and essential necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of essential necessity and explain why the first interpretation is flawed.

Core Idea
The definition of essential necessity must refer to the persistence of the necessity, not the subject, or it becomes trivial.

How should we define eternal necessity and essential necessity? This lesson examines common definitions and their shortcomings, especially for negative statements. A careful analysis reveals that the definition of essential necessity is ambiguous, and the correct interpretation requires the necessity itself to be permanent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions given
The text reports that some define eternal necessity as that whose subject is eternal, and essential necessity as that which holds as long as the subject exists.
These are common in modal logic discussions.
2
Apply to affirmative vs. negative statements
The author notes that these definitions work for affirmative statements but are problematic for negative statements. For example, in a negative proposition, the definition fails to capture necessity.
The author gives a quick argument using letters, but it is condensed.
3
Examine the two interpretations of essential necessity
The phrase 'established as long as the subject exists' can mean either (a) the subject is established throughout its existence, or (b) the necessity itself is established throughout the subject's existence.
Interpretation (a) is trivial because every subject exists as long as it exists.
4
Adopt the correct interpretation
The author prefers interpretation (b), which says the necessity endures as long as the subject exists. This aligns with later scholars like al-Urmawi.
This interpretation is more substantive and avoids triviality.

'Essential necessity is that which is established as long as the subject exists': this phrase is interpreted in two ways: 1. Essential necessity is that whose subject stays established as long as the subject exists. 2. Essential necessity is that whose necessity stays established as long as the subject exists. The first interpretation agrees with the previous statement: 'Eternal necessity is that whose subject is eternal', but it is unworthy of anyone to show it because every subject is established as long as the subject exists, whether there is necessity or not, and whether the necessity is eternal or essential or otherwise. The second interpretation agrees with the later statements and the closest wording to the author's text we found in Matali' al-Anwar by al-Urmawi: 'Essential necessity is that which occurs as long as the subject's essence exists.' Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage, from the fifth section on modality, deals with conditional necessity and temporal possibility. It begins by stating that when the continuance of the description is taken into account in conditional necessity, the corresponding possibility does not imply that the possible actually can occur. For example, 'Some writer is illiterate' can be true by temporal possibility even though it is impossible for a writer to be illiterate at the same time (since a writer is defined by being literate). This is because the possibility applies at a different time. The author then moves to a critique of common definitions of eternal necessity and essential necessity. He reports that some define eternal necessity as that whose subject is eternal (e.g., God's attributes), and essential necessity as that which holds as long as the subject exists. But he finds this problematic for negative statements. He gives a quick argument using letters. Then a footnote explains that 'temporal possibility' originates from al-Khunaji and is the contradictory of the universal conditional. Finally, the text examines two interpretations of the definition of essential necessity: one that takes it as the subject existing throughout, and another as the necessity itself existing throughout. The first is rejected because it says nothing special about necessity. The second is preferred, citing al-Urmawi. This shows the precision required in defining modal terms in logic.

Defining and Critiquing Azaliyya and Dhatiyya Necessity
Definition and critique of two types of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of necessity (azaliyya and dhatiyya) and analyze why their definitions fail when applied to negative propositions.

Core Idea
Definitions of necessity that work for affirmatives collapse for negatives with non-existent subjects.

In this passage, the author defines two key types of necessity used in logical propositions: eternal necessity (al-ḍarūra al-azaliyya) and essential necessity (al-ḍarūra al-dhātiyya). He then subjects these definitions to a critical test by applying them to negative propositions, revealing logical inconsistencies. This exercise sharpens our ability to test definitions and avoid hidden assumptions about subject existence.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two definitions
Read the first two sentences: 'الأزلية ما تكون الذات أزلية' and 'الذاتية ما تكون ثابتة ما دامت الذات موجودة'. Identify that azaliyya requires the subject's essence to be eternal, while dhatiyya requires the predicate to hold as long as the subject's essence exists.
These definitions are meant to capture different modes of necessity.
2
Test definitions on affirmative propositions
The author concedes that these definitions work for affirmative propositions. Confirm this: for an affirmative like 'Allah is eternal', both definitions are coherent.
Affirmative propositions assert existence, so the conditions are naturally satisfied.
3
Apply definitions to negative propositions
Now apply the same definitions to negative statements. For eternal necessity, if we require the subject's essence to be eternal, then the negative 'No partner of Allah exists' (using 'partner' as a non-existent subject) creates a problem: both the necessary (eternal) and possible versions can be false simultaneously, violating logical opposition.
The author illustrates this with the example of 'partner of Allah' (non-existent subject).
4
Analyze the essential necessity case
For essential necessity in the negative, if we require the necessity of negation as long as the essence exists, we must assume the subject exists even in a negative proposition. This conflicts with the standard logical doctrine that negatives do not require subject existence.
The author notes that this contradicts 'their doctrine' (the logicians own principles).
5
Synthesize the critique
Combine the findings: the definitions only work if the subject exists. For non-existent subjects, the definitions lead to contradictions or unintended assumptions. This shows that definitions must be carefully refined to cover all cases.
The possible proposition does not oppose the necessary in non-existent subjects, breaking the expected logical relationships.

إن الضرورة الأزلية ما تكون الذات أزلية. و الذاتية ما تكون ثابتة ما دامت الذات موجودة. فيه نظر؛ فإنّ ذلك في الموجبة صحيح. و أما في السالبة: فلو شرط في الأزلية «أزلية الذات»، لكانت الممكنة مقابلة لها، لجواز كذبهما معاً إذا كان الموضوع معدوماً، كقولنا: لا شيء من شريك البارئ بموجود بالضرورة الأزلية و بعض شريك البارئ موجود بالإمكان. و كذا لو اعتبر في السالبة الذاتية «ضرورة السلب ما دامت الذات»؛ فإن ذلك يوجب اعتبار وجود الموضوع في السالبة و ذلك ينافي مذهبهم. و أيضاً لا تقابلها الممكنة في المعدوم.
Indeed, the eternal necessity is that the essence is eternal. And the essential necessity is that which is fixed as long as the essence exists. There is a consideration: for that is correct in the affirmative. As for the negative: if in the eternal [necessity] one stipulates 'the eternality of the essence', then the possible would be its opposite, because both can be false when the subject is non-existent, like our saying: 'Nothing of the partner of Allah exists with eternal necessity' and 'Some partner of Allah exists with possibility'. And likewise if in the essential negative one considers 'the necessity of negation as long as the essence [exists]', then that necessitates considering the existence of the subject in the negative, and that contradicts their doctrine. Also, the possible does not oppose it in the non-existent.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage defines two types of necessity: eternal necessity (al-ḍarūra al-azaliyya) and essential necessity (al-ḍarūra al-dhātiyya), and then critiques their definitions when applied to negative propositions. The author points out that these definitions work for affirmative propositions but fail for negatives. For eternal necessity, if we condition the eternality of the subject, then the corresponding possible proposition would be its contradictory, but both can be false when the subject does not exist. For example, 'No partner of Allah exists' is false because the subject is non-existent, and 'Some partner of Allah exists' is also false. This contradicts the law of contradiction. For essential necessity, if we stipulate that the necessity of negation holds as long as the essence exists, then in negative propositions we must assume the subject exists, which goes against the common doctrine that negative propositions do not require the existence of the subject. Also, in the case of a non-existent subject, the possible proposition does not oppose it. The passage thus refines the definitions by showing their limitations.

Problems with Negative Modal Propositions and Subject Existence
Author discusses difficulties in negative modal propositions when subject is non-existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in each type of negative modal proposition when the subject is non-existent, and apply it to Islamic theological examples.

Core Idea
Negative modal propositions fail when the subject doesn't exist, violating contradiction.

This passage examines a critical problem in modal logic: defining negative necessities without assuming subject existence. The author shows that common definitions of eternal, essential, conditional, and temporal negatives all fail when applied to non-existent subjects like 'partner of God'. Understanding this issue is crucial for theological debates about divine attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of negative modal propositions
Find the definitions of al-saliba al-azaliyya, al-dhatiyya, and al-mashruta in the Arabic. What conditions do they set for the negation?
Also note the 'urfiyya and darura waqtiyya which follow similar patterns.
2
Apply each definition to a non-existent subject
Use the example 'partner of God' (sharik al-Bari). For each type, determine whether the negative proposition is true or false when the subject does not exist.
The author argues that both the necessary negative and its contradictory possible affirmative can be false.
3
Check the logical contradictories
For each negative, what would be its contradictory affirmative? Under the definition, can both the negative and its contradictory be false? If so, why is that a violation of the principle of contradiction?
Contradictories cannot both be false; this is a fundamental logical rule.
4
Summarize the common flaw
What do all these definitions require implicitly about the subject's existence? How does that contradict the standard logical position that negative propositions do not assume subject existence?
The author concludes these are problems with 'external and real consideration'.

Source Text

1. If one stipulates in the eternal [negative] 'eternity of the essence', then the possible [affirmative] would be its contradictory, because both could be false together if the subject is non-existent, as in our saying: 'Nothing of a partner of God is existent by eternal necessity' and 'some partner of God is existent by possibility'. 2. Likewise if one considers in the essential negative 'necessity of negation as long as the essence exists'; then that necessitates considering the existence of the subject in the negative, and that contradicts their doctrine. And also the possible does not contradict it in the case of the non-existent. And the same holds for the perpetual.
[2][Temporal description of the subject in the conditional necessity and general customary]
C. The conditional negative:
1. If one stipulates in it 'necessity of negation as long as the subject's essence is described by the description', then the state of negation has been conditioned by 'the subject being described by the attribute, and its being described is only possible when it exists. Thus it necessitates stipulating the existence of the subject in the negative in the state of negation.
.. And if one does not stipulate, then the conditional negative is not conditional.
And the same holds for the customary.
[3][Temporal duration of the subject in the temporal necessity]
And the time being of the times of the essence or the description in the affirmative is correct. As for the negative, it necessitates stipulating the existence of the subject in the negative, and what we mentioned follows.
And these doubts only apply to the external and real consideration. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage critically examines problems arising in negative modal propositions when the subject may be non-existent. The author first considers the 'eternal negative' (salba azaliyya) defined with 'eternity of the essence'. If we negate eternally that a non-existent subject (e.g., 'partner of God') exists, the corresponding possible affirmative ('some partner of God exists possibly') is also false because the subject is non-existent. Thus both the universal necessary negative and the particular possible affirmative can be false simultaneously, contradicting the logical rule that contradictories cannot both be false. The same issue occurs with the 'essential negative' (salba dhatiyya) defined as 'negation as long as the essence exists', because it implicitly requires the subject's existence, which contradicts the logicians' usual position that negative propositions do not assume subject existence. Then the author examines the 'conditional negative' (salba mashruta). If its condition is 'negation as long as the subject is described by the attribute', then the negation is conditioned on the subject being described, which in turn requires the subject's existence, thereby again requiring subject existence. If no such condition is stipulated, the proposition is not truly conditional. The same applies to the 'customary' ('urfiyya). Finally, in 'temporal necessity' (darura waqtiyya), the time can be attached to the essence or description in affirmatives, but in negatives it again forces subject existence. These critiques target the 'external' and 'real' considerations of modality, not merely verbal definitions. The discussion shows the technical difficulties logicians faced in defining negative modalities without assuming subject existence.

السالبة الذاتية
السالبة المشروطة
العرفية العامة
الضرورة الوقتية

B) Because the necessary negative and the possible affirmative would both be false, violating the principle of contradiction.
C) Because the subject 'partner of God' is impossible, so only the negative is true.
D) Because the possible affirmative cannot be formed for a non-existent subject.

Key Takeaway
The definitions of negative modal propositions that rely on the subject's essence, description, or time inadvertently require the subject's existence, leading to logical contradictions when the subject is non-existent.

The Six Simple Modal Propositions
Author lists and defines the six simple modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define each of the six simple modal propositions and distinguish them based on their modal conditions.

Core Idea
The six simple modalities are the building blocks of modal reasoning.

In this lesson, we explore the six simple modal propositions that form the foundation of modal logic in the Islamic tradition. These propositions classify judgments based on necessity, permanence, condition, actuality, and possibility. Understanding them is essential for grasping how logicians evaluate conversions, contradictions, and syllogisms involving modal statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six simple modalities
List the names: الضرورية المطلقة, المشروطة العامة, الدائمة, العرفية العامة, المطلقة العامة, الممكنة العامة.
They are simple because each contains a single relation (affirmative or negative).
2
Extract the defining condition for each
For each, note the key condition: essence existence (ضرورية), condition of description (مشروطة), permanence (دائمة), duration of attribute (عرفية), actual relation (مطلقة), non-necessity of opposite (ممكنة).
The condition determines when the predicate holds.
3
Classify each as affirmative or negative
Each modal proposition can be affirmative or negative, but the definitions are given in affirmative form.
4
Compare the modalities
Distinguish similar pairs: e.g., absolute necessary vs. perpetual (necessary implies impossibility of change, perpetual only covers actual duration).
General conditional vs. general customary: both depend on a description, but conditional implies necessity, customary implies common occurrence.
5
Apply the definitions to Islamic examples
For instance, 'Every human is necessarily rational' (ضرورية مطلقة), 'Every writer is moving fingers while writing' (عرفية عامة).
Use examples from kalam, fiqh, or usul.

Source Text

First chapter: On Propositions
Fifth section: On Modality
Concerning the propositions they consider in conversions, contradictions, syllogism, and others.
[The considered modal propositions] They are thirteen propositions:
[The six simple ones]
Six of them are simple, and they are those in which there is a single relation, either affirmative or negative. A. The absolute necessary: that in which the necessity of the relation of the predicate to the subject is judged as long as the essence exists. B. The general conditional: that in which the necessity of the relation of the predicate is judged on condition of the description of the subject. C. The perpetual: that in which the permanence of the relation of the predicate to the subject is judged as long as the essence exists. D. The general customary: that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists. E. The general absolute: that in which the actual relation is judged. F. The general possible: that in which the negation of absolute necessity from the opposite side of the judgment is judged.
[The seven composite ones]
And seven of them are composite, and they are those in which there are two relations together. A. The specific conditional: that in which the necessity of the relation of the predicate to the subject is judged as long as the description, 'not always' according to the essence.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage provides a systematic enumeration of the thirteen modal propositions (muwajjahaat) recognized in classical logic. The author first notes that these are the modalities used in conversions, contradictions, syllogisms, etc. Thirteen are considered: six simple (basita) and seven composite (murakkaba). Simple ones have a single relation (either affirmative or negative). They are: (1) al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa (absolute necessary): the predicate belongs to the subject necessarily as long as the subject's essence exists; (2) al-mashruta al-'amma (general conditional): the predicate is necessary on condition that the subject is described by a certain attribute; (3) al-da'ima (perpetual): the predicate holds permanently as long as the essence exists; (4) al-'urfiyya al-'amma (general customary): the predicate holds as long as the attribute exists; (5) al-mutlaqa al-'amma (general absolute): the predicate actually holds without modality; (6) al-mumkina al-'amma (general possible): it is possible in the sense that the opposite is not absolutely necessary. Composite ones combine two relations; the example given is al-mashruta al-khassa (specific conditional): the predicate is necessary as long as the description exists, but not always according to the essence. This classification builds on the work of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and is essential for understanding modal conversions and syllogisms.

The Seven Composite Modal Propositions
Author defines the seven composite modal propositions and gives one example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the concept of composite modal propositions and identify the specific conditional proposition with its unique condition.

Core Idea
Composite modals combine two relations; the specific conditional ties necessity to a description but not to the essence.

Having studied the simple modal propositions, we now turn to the composite ones. These propositions contain two relations together, capturing more nuanced modalities. The author gives one key example: the specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة), which combines necessity with a temporal limitation based on a description.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of composite modal propositions
Composite propositions have two relations (e.g., necessity and non-necessity) in the same judgment.
Seven composites are mentioned, but only one is detailed.
2
Identify the specific conditional
It is defined as: the predicate is necessary as long as the description exists, but not always according to the essence.
The condition 'not always according to the essence' distinguishes it from the general conditional.
3
Compare with the general conditional
The general conditional (مشروطة عامة) says the predicate is necessary as long as the description exists, without the 'not always according to essence' clause. The specific conditional adds that the necessity is only during the description.
For example, 'All humans are necessarily speaking as long as they speak' (specific) vs. 'All speakers are necessarily speaking' (general, but essence-based).
4
Consider why composites are needed
Simple modalities cannot capture judgments that are necessary under one condition but not under another.
Composites fill that gap.

Concerning the propositions they consider in conversions, contradictions, syllogism, and others.
[The considered modal propositions] They are thirteen propositions:
[The six simple ones]
Six of them are simple, and they are those in which there is a single relation, either affirmative or negative. A. F. The general possible: that in which the negation of absolute necessity from the opposite side of the judgment is judged.
[The seven composite ones]
And seven of them are composite, and they are those in which there are two relations together. A. The specific conditional: that in which the necessity of the relation of the predicate to the subject is judged as long as the description, 'not always' according to the essence.

The Thirteen Modal Propositions in Islamic Logic
Author enumerates the six simple and seven composite modal propositions with their definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish all thirteen modal propositions by their definitions regarding necessity, permanence, actuality, and temporal conditions.

Core Idea
The thirteen modals are the toolbox for expressing necessity and possibility in Islamic logic.

In this passage, the author presents the complete inventory of modal propositions considered in formal logic. These modals are essential for correctly analyzing syllogisms, conversions, and contradictions. Mastery of these thirteen types will allow you to precisely evaluate the modality of any proposition encountered in Islamic scholarly arguments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish simple vs composite
Simple modals have one relation (affirmative or negative); composite modals have two relations together.
The author explicitly marks this division.
2
Identify the key condition for each simple modal
For each simple modal, determine whether it involves necessity (ضرورة), permanence (دوام), actuality (فعلية), or possibility (إمكان), and note the temporal scope: as long as essence (ما دامت الذات) or as long as description (ما دام الوصف).
For example, الضرورية المطلقة is necessity as long as essence exists.
3
Analyze composite modals
Each composite modal combines a condition with a negation of perpetuity or necessity. For instance, المشروطة الخاصة affirms necessity during the description but not perpetually.
The negation is implied by 'لا دائماً' or 'لا بالضرورة'.
4
Memorize the names and definitions
Use the mnemonic of six simple and seven composite. Practice recalling each definition verbatim from the passage.
Refer to the Arabic text for exact phrasing.
5
Understand relationships among modals
The author mentions that modals relate by generality, specificity, and opposition. Grasping these relationships prepares you for conversion and contradiction rules.
This insight is crucial for later logical operations.

The considered modal propositions: they are thirteen propositions. Six of them are simple, which have a single relation, either affirmative or negative. A: Absolute Necessary: judged with necessity of the predicate to the subject as long as the essence exists. B: General Conditioned: judged with necessity of the predicate conditioned by the description of the subject. C: Perpetual: judged with permanence of the relation of the predicate to the subject as long as the essence exists. D: General Customary: judged with permanence of the relation as long as the description of the subject exists. E: General Absolute: judged with actual relation. And the General Possible: judged with negation of absolute necessity from the opposite side of the judgment. Seven are composite, which have both relations together. A: Specific Conditioned: judged with necessity of the predicate to the subject as long as the description exists, not perpetually with respect to essence. B: Specific Customary: judged with permanence of the relation of the predicate to the subject as long as the description exists, not perpetually with respect to essence. C: Temporal: judged with necessity of the predicate to the subject at a specific time, not perpetually with respect to essence. D: Diffused: judged with necessity of the relation at an unspecified time, not perpetually. E: Non-perpetual Existential: judged with actual relation, not perpetually. F: Non-necessary Existential: judged with actual relation, not necessarily. G: Specific Possible: judged with negation of absolute necessity from both sides. And by 'non-perpetual' is meant the General Absolute, and by 'non-necessary' the General Possible. And it is not hidden from the investigator the relation of some to others by generality, specificity, and opposition after grasping their meanings. And there may appear in conversions, contradictions, and conclusions of syllogisms propositions outside the thirteen, known in their places, God willing.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the thirteen modal propositions considered in logic. The author first distinguishes six simple modals, which contain a single relation (either affirmative or negative) regarding necessity, permanence, or possibility under certain conditions. For example, the Absolute Necessary (الضرورية المطلقة) affirms necessity as long as the subject's essence exists, while the General Conditioned (المشروطة العامة) conditions necessity on the subject's description. Then seven composite modals are given, which combine two relations: a condition and a negation of perpetuity. For instance, the Specific Conditioned (المشروطة الخاصة) affirms necessity only during the description but not perpetually with respect to essence. The author also notes the relations among these modals (generality, specificity, opposition) and reminds that additional modals may appear in logical operations. Understanding these modals is essential for correctly applying conversions, contradictions, and syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Modality for the Predicate vs. the Quantifier
Author explains that modal term can qualify predicate or quantifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a modal term qualifies the predicate relation or the quantifier in a given Arabic proposition.

Core Idea
Modal terms can qualify either the 'how' of predication or the 'how' of universality.

In Islamic logic, the modal term (jiha) does not always attach to the predicate's relation to the subject. It can also qualify the quantifier, changing the meaning of propositions. This short lesson clarifies the distinction with a concrete example, a crucial skill for interpreting modal syllogisms in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of modality
From the passage, note that modality can be for the predicate (jimat al-ḥaml) or for the quantifier (jimat al-sūr). The former qualifies the copula linking subject and predicate; the latter qualifies the universality or particularity.
2
Examine the example provided
In 'It is possible that every human is a writer' (يمكن أن يكون كل الناس كاتباً), the modal term 'possible' applies to the quantifier 'every'. This means each individual human can possibly be a writer.
If the modality applied to the predicate, it would mean 'it is possible that the statement 'every human is a writer' is true'.
3
Contrast with predicate modality
Construct a parallel proposition: 'Every human is necessarily a writer' (كل الناس كاتب بالضرورة). Here 'necessarily' qualifies the predicate relation, not the quantifier.
The shift in meaning shows why the distinction matters for correct inference.
4
Apply to a new example
Given 'It is possible that some animals are rational,' determine which component the modality qualifies. (Answer: quantifier 'some')
Practice reinforces the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع [جمة الحمل و جمة السور] كما تكون [جمة] للحمل، أي كيفية نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع، كما مرّ. فقد تكون جمة للسور، أي كيفية العموم و الخصوص، كما إذا قلنا: «يمكن أن يكون كل الناس كاتباً»؛ معناه أنّ كلّ واحد واحد من الناس كافتهم يمكن أن يكون كاتباً.
The Fourth Investigation: The Modality of the Predicate and the Modality of the Quantifier. Just as it [the modality] is for the predicate, i.e., the mode of the relation of the predicate to the subject, as has passed, so it may be for the quantifier, i.e., the mode of universality and particularity, as when we say: 'It is possible that every human is a writer'; its meaning is that every single human among all of them can be a writer.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the modal term (jiha) can qualify either the copula (the 'how' of the predicate's relation to the subject) or the quantifier (the 'how' of universality or particularity). The author gives an example: 'It is possible that every human is a writer.' Here the modality 'possible' applies to the universal quantifier 'every', meaning that for each individual human, it is possible that they are a writer. This distinction is important in logical analysis to avoid ambiguity. In Islamic logic, understanding jiha as either for the predicate or for the quantifier affects the interpretation of propositions, especially in modal syllogisms.

The Seven Modal Propositions: Definitions and Relationships
Author enumerates seven types of modal propositions and notes their relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the seven modal propositions defined in the passage, and explain how they relate to one another in terms of generality and specificity.

Core Idea
Modal propositions qualify the necessity, permanence, or time of a judgment — a key tool for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences.

This passage from the logical text enumerates seven types of modal propositions, each specifying a different mode of predication: whether it is perpetual, time-bound, conditional on a description, or merely possible. Understanding these distinctions is essential for analyzing syllogisms, conversions, and contradictions in Islamic logical and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven types
List the seven Arabic terms: العرفية الخاصة, الوقتية, المنتشرة, الوجودية اللادائمة, الوجودية اللاضرورية, الممكنة الخاصة. Note that the passage also references the 'general absolute' and 'general possible' as correlates.
These seven are part of a set of thirteen, the rest of which are not detailed here.
2
Analyze the definition of each type
For each type, extract the key modal qualifiers: (ب) as long as the description lasts, (ج) at a specific time, (د) at an unspecified time, (ه) actual but not perpetual, (و) actual but not necessary, (ز) negation of absolute necessity from both sides.
The definitions rely on temporal and conditional concepts.
3
Understand the relational terms
The passage notes that 'non-permanent' refers to the general absolute (اللادوام المطلقة العامة) and 'non-necessary' to the general possible (اللاضرورة الممكنة العامة). These relate the seven to the wider set of thirteen.
Relationships include generality, specificity, and mutual exclusivity.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider a fiqh ruling: 'Fasting is obligatory in Ramadan.' This is a temporal (وقتية) modal if the time is specified, or a particular customary if conditional on the description 'being a Muslim'. Practice categorizing similar statements.
Modal distinctions help avoid confusing conditional rulings with unconditional ones.

b) The particular customary [proposition]: that in which the continuity of the predicate's relation to the subject is judged as long as the description [lasts], not perpetually, according to the essence. c) The temporal: that in which the necessity of the predicate's relation to the subject is judged at a specific time, not perpetually, according to the essence. d) The diffused: that in which the necessity of the relation is judged at an unspecified time, not perpetually. e) The non-permanent existential: that in which the actual relation is judged, not perpetually. f) The non-necessary existential: that in which the actual relation is judged, not necessarily. g) The particular possible: that in which the absolute necessity is negated from both sides. Commentary
This passage defines and enumerates seven types of modal propositions, which are part of a set of thirteen. The author lists them with their definitions: the particular customary proposition asserts continuity of the predicate as long as a description (e.g., being a writer) holds, but not perpetually; the temporal proposition asserts necessity at a specific time; the diffused proposition asserts necessity at an unspecified time; the non-permanent existential proposition asserts actual existence but not perpetual; the non-necessary existential proposition asserts actuality without necessity; and the particular possible proposition negates absolute necessity from both affirmation and negation. The author then explains that the terms 'non-permanent' and 'non-necessary' refer to the general absolute and general possible, respectively. The passage then notes that the relationships among these thirteen types (generality, specificity, and mutual exclusivity) become clear once their meanings are understood. Finally, the author acknowledges that there may be additional propositions beyond these thirteen that appear in logical operations like conversion, contradiction, and syllogistic reasoning.

Key Terms
العرفية الخاصة

Distinguishing Modality of Predicate from Modality of Quantifier
Author explains the difference between jiha al-haml and jiha al-sawr with examples and a discussion from al-Shifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given modal proposition uses jiha al-haml or jiha al-sawr, and explain the difference using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The modality of the predicate concerns the subject-predicate relation; the modality of the quantifier concerns the scope of the subject.

In this passage, the author introduces a crucial distinction in modal logic: the modality that qualifies the predicate (jiha al-haml) versus the modality that qualifies the quantifier (jiha al-sawr). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting propositions in Islamic theological and jurisprudential discourse, such as statements about divine attributes or universal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modality types
Locate the definitions of jiha al-haml and jiha al-sawr in the passage. Notice that one qualifies the predicate's relation to the subject, the other qualifies the quantifier (the manner of universality/particularity).
2
Examine the example given
The author gives: 'It is possible that every human is a writer'. Parse its two interpretations: (a) each individual can be a writer (jiha al-haml), (b) it is possible that all humans collectively are writers (jiha al-sawr).
3
Compare the two interpretations
Note that the first interpretation (jiha al-haml) is obvious and not doubted, while the second (jiha al-sawr) is doubted by some because they deny the collective possibility. This shows the conceptual difference.
4
Apply to a negative proposition
The passage says the same applies to negative propositions. Think of 'It is necessary that no one is a thief' – distinguish modal predicate vs modal quantifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجهة: [المبحث ] الرابع [جمة الحمل و جمة السور] . كما تكون [جمة ] للحمل، أي كيفية نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع، كما مرّ، ٢. فقد تكون جمة للسور، أي كيفية العموم و الخصوص، كما إذا قلنا: يمكن أن يكون كل الناس كاتباً»؛ معناه أنّ كلّ واحد واحد من الناس كافتهم يمكن أن يكون كاتباً». و الفرق بينها و بين «الجية بحسب الحمل» أن «ثبوث المحمول للموضوع» و «سلته عنه» مغاير لـ «عموم أفراد الموضوع» و «خصوصها»؛ فكيفية أحدهما مغايرة لكيفية الآخر. تفرقة الشيخ بين اعتباري إمكان السور و إمكان الحمل] و فرق الشيخ في الشفاء: بأن الثاني لا يشك فيه جمهورُ الناس، فإن كل واحد يعلم أنه يجوز أن يكون كاتباً. و الأوّل قد يُشك فيه؛ فإن من الناس من يقول: «من المحال أن يتفق أن لا يوجد واحد من الناس إلا و أن يكون كاتباً». و أما الجزئيتان فتساويان في الظهور و الخفاء. و لكنه قد يعلم – مع ذلك – التغاير بينهما في المفهوم كما في الكلية. و كذا حكم السالبة. [ تفسير الخونجي لجهة السور بالعموم المجموعي ] و من الناس من زعم أن مراده بـ «العموم» «الكلُّ من حيث هو كل وتمثل بأنه يصدق قولنا: «كل من في البلد يُشبعه هذا الرغيف» بحسب الحمل و لا يصدق بحسب السور، لأن مجموع الناس لا يشبعه " هذا الرغيف.
The Modality: Fourth Section: Jiha al-Haml and Jiha al-Sawr. Just as there is a modality for the predicate, i.e., the manner of the predicate's relation to the subject, as previously, so there can be a modality for the quantifier, i.e., the manner of universality and particularity, as when we say: 'It is possible that every human is a writer'; its meaning is that each and every one of all humans can be a writer. The difference between it and the modality according to the predicate is that the affirmation of the predicate for the subject and its negation are different from the universality of the individuals of the subject and their particularity; so the manner of one is different from the manner of the other. The Sheikh's distinction between the two considerations of possibility of the quantifier and possibility of the predicate: The Sheikh distinguished in al-Shifa by [saying] that the second is not doubted by the majority of people, for everyone knows that it is permissible for him to be a writer. The first may be doubted, for some people say: 'It is impossible that it would happen that not one of the people exists except that he is a writer.' As for the two particulars, they are equal in clarity and obscurity. However, the difference between them in concept is known, just as in the universal. And the same rule applies to the negative. [Interpretation of al-Khunji of jiha al-sawr as collective universality] And some people claimed that by 'universality' he meant 'the whole insofar as it is a whole', and he exemplified by saying: 'Everyone in the town is satisfied by this loaf' is true according to the predicate modality but not according to the quantifier modality, because the totality of the people are not satisfied by this loaf.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between two types of modality: jiha al-haml (modality of the predicate) and jiha al-sawr (modality of the quantifier). The author explains that just as a proposition can have a modality indicating the manner of predication (affirmation or negation), it can also have a modality indicating the manner of universality or particularity. An example is given: 'It is possible that every human is a writer.' This can be interpreted in two ways: either the possibility qualifies the predicate (each individual can be a writer) or it qualifies the quantifier (it is possible that all humans collectively are writers). The difference is that the predicate's affirmation/negation is distinct from the quantifier's universality/particularity. The author then cites a discussion from al-Shifa: the second interpretation (possibility of the predicate) is generally not doubted, while the first (possibility of the quantifier) may be doubted because some deny that it is possible for all humans to be writers simultaneously. For particular propositions, both interpretations are equal in clarity, but the conceptual difference still exists. Finally, al-Khunji's view is referenced, where he interprets 'universality' as collective universality (the whole as a whole), illustrated by the example of a loaf of bread that can satisfy each individual but cannot satisfy the entire group collectively.

Key Terms
جهة الحمل
جهة السور
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it involves an impossibility.
B) Because common people doubt that all humans could be writers simultaneously.
C) Because the predicate is always possible.
D) Because the quantifier is always necessary.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of distinguishing modal predicate from modal quantifier to avoid ambiguity in deductive arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Khunji's Interpretation of Jiha al-Sawr as Collective Universality
Author explains the difference between jiha al-haml and jiha al-sawr with examples and a discussion from al-Shifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Khunji's interpretation of jiha al-sawr as collective universality and apply it to an example from Islamic law.

Core Idea
Al-Khunji reinterprets jiha al-sawr as the whole collectively, not distributively.

The passage concludes with a reference to al-Khunji, who interprets 'universality' in jiha al-sawr as collective universality (al-umum al-majmu'i). This interpretation is illustrated with a powerful example: a loaf of bread that can satisfy each individual but not the whole group. This lesson explores this nuanced shift and its logical implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Khunji's claim
He holds that by 'universality' (al-umum) the author meant 'the whole as a whole' (al-kull min haythu huwa kull), not the distributive sense.
2
Analyze the bread example
The proposition 'Everyone in the town is satisfied by this loaf' is true under jiha al-haml (each individually could be satisfied) but false under jiha al-sawr (the collective totality cannot be satisfied by one loaf). This shows the two modalities diverge.
3
Identify the logical consequence
If jiha al-sawr is interpreted collectively, then the truth conditions of modal propositions change. For example, 'It is possible that all humans are writers' would mean the collective set of humans being writers is possible, which is much stronger and less likely.
4
Apply to Islamic jurisprudence
Consider a ruling: 'It is permissible for all Muslims to break the fast under duress.' Under distributive jiha al-haml, each Muslim individually may break. Under collective jiha al-sawr, it would mean that it is possible for the entire Ummah (collectively) to break, which is different.

Source Text

Distinguishing Modality of Quantifier from Modality of Predication
Author distinguishes between modality attached to the quantifier and modality attached to the predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between jiha al-sawr and jiha al-haml and identify each in a given proposition.

Core Idea
Modality can qualify the quantifier or the predication – they are not the same.

This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction in modal logic: the modality that attaches to the quantifier (e.g., 'all' or 'some') is different from the modality that attaches to the predication itself. Understanding this distinction helps you avoid conflating the possibility of a universal claim with the possibility of a predicate applying to an individual. We will unpack the definitions and Ibn Sina's illustrative example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modalities in the passage
Read the Arabic text and locate the terms 'جهة السور' (modality of the quantifier) and 'جهة الحمل' (modality of predication). Note how the author sets them apart.
The passage begins by stating that affirmation/negation (جهة الحمل) is different from generality/particularity (جهة السور).
2
Understand the conceptual difference
The modality of predication concerns whether the predicate is affirmed or negated of the subject (e.g., possible, necessary, impossible). The modality of the quantifier concerns whether the scope of the subject (all or some) is affected by modality (e.g., is it possible that all individuals have the predicate?).
The author says: 'فكيفية أحدهما مغايرة لكيفية الآخر' – the modality of one is different from the modality of the other.
3
Apply Ibn Sina's example
Ibn Sina gives an example: Every person knows it is possible for himself to be a writer (modality of predication). But some may doubt whether it is possible that every human is a writer (modality of the quantifier). This shows the two modalities can have different certainty.
The example illustrates that the modality of predication (personal possibility) is obvious, while the modality of the quantifier (universal possibility) can be contested.
4
Check the case of particular and negative propositions
The passage notes that for particular propositions, both modalities may be equally clear or unclear, but the conceptual difference remains. The same applies to negative propositions.
The author says: 'و كذلك حكم السالبة' – and likewise the ruling of the negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الفرق بينها و بين «الجية بحسب الحمل» أن «ثبوت المحمول للموضوع» و «سلبه عنه» مغاير لـ «عموم أفراد الموضوع» و «خصوصها»؛ فكيفية أحدهما مغايرة لكيفية الآخر.
[تفرقة الشيخ بين اعتباري إمكان السور و إمكان الحمل]
و فرق الشيخ في الشفاء:
بأن الثاني لا يشك فيه جمهورُ الناس، فإن كل واحد يعلم أنه يجوز أن يكون كاتباً. و الأوّل قد يُشك فيه؛ فإن من الناس من يقول: «من المحال أن يتفق أن لا يوجد واحد من الناس إلا و أن يكون كاتباً».
و أما الجزئيتان فتساويان في الظهور و الخفاء. و لكنه قد يعلم – مع ذلك – التغاير بينهما في المفهوم كما في الكلية.
وكذا حكم السالبة.
And the difference between it [modality of the quantifier] and modality according to predication is that 'the affirmation of the predicate to the subject' and 'its negation from it' are different from 'the generality of the individuals of the subject' and 'their particularity'; so the modality of one is different from the modality of the other. The Shaykh's distinction between the two considerations of possibility of the quantifier and possibility of predication: And the Shaykh distinguished in al-Shifa: that the second is not doubted by the majority of people, for every individual knows that it is possible for him to be a writer. The first may be doubted; for among people there is one who says: 'It is impossible that it should happen that not a single one of the people exists who is a writer.' As for the two particular [propositions], they are equal in clarity and obscurity. But one may know, nevertheless, the difference between them in concept, as in the universal. And likewise the ruling of the negative.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines the crucial distinction between two types of modality in propositions: modality of the quantifier (jiha al-sawr) and modality of predication (jiha al-haml). The author begins by stating that the affirmation or negation of a predicate to a subject is conceptually distinct from the universality or particularity of the subject's individuals. Each has its own modality. He then cites the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) from al-Shifa, who illustrates the difference: the second (modality of predication) is obvious to everyone—every person knows it is possible that he be a writer. The first (modality of the quantifier) may be doubted, because someone might deny that it is possible for every human to be a writer simultaneously. For particular propositions, both types appear equally clear or obscure, but the conceptual difference remains, just as it does for universals. The same applies to negative propositions.

Key Terms
جهة السور
جهة الحمل
ثبوت المحمول
عموم أفراد الموضوع

Key Takeaway
The modality of the quantifier concerns the scope of the subject, while the modality of predication concerns the affirmation/negation of the predicate; they are conceptually distinct and can have different degrees of certainty.

Distinguishing Collective vs Distributive Generality
Author rejects the claim that the two modalities are identical using the loaf-of-bread example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between distributive and collective generality in quantified propositions and identify why conflating them leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
The mistake of conflating collective and distributive generality leads to a contradiction about particular propositions.

In this passage, the author attacks a mistaken view attributed to al-Khunaji that the quantifier modality ('every' in 'every A is B') refers to the collective whole rather than the distributive individuals. Using a concrete example, the author shows that this interpretation would make the truth conditions of particular propositions behave differently, contradicting the earlier accepted claim that the two modalities (predication and quantifier) yield the same truth value for particulars. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly analysing quantified statements in logic and Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Khunaji's claim
Find the claim that 'generality' in the quantifier is intended as 'the whole insofar as it is a whole' (al-kull min haythu huwa kull). This is a collective interpretation.
2
Parse the loaf example
Consider the statement 'Everyone in the city is satisfied by this loaf'. Under the distributive (predication) interpretation, it is true because each individual can be satisfied by a part of the loaf. Under the collective (quantifier) interpretation, it is false because the group as a whole cannot be satisfied by a single loaf.
3
Trace the contradiction
If the collective interpretation were correct for the quantifier, then the particular proposition 'Some person in the city is satisfied by this loaf' would also have different truth values under the two modalities: true for predication (one person is satisfied) but false for the quantifier (the group is not satisfied). However, al-Khunaji himself admitted that the two particular propositions are equivalent in truth value.
4
Recognize the logical flaw
The flaw is that al-Khunaji's position is internally inconsistent: he claims the quantifier modality is collective, yet he also holds that particular propositions are the same in both modalities. The author's refutation exposes this contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من الناس من زعم أن مراده بـ «العموم» «الكلُّ من حيث هو كل وتمثل بأنه يصدق قولنا: «كل من في البلد يُشبعه هذا الرغيف» بحسب الحمل و لا يصدق بحسب السور، لأن مجموع الناس لا يشبعه هذا الرغيف. و ذلك خطأ، و إلا فيكون حكم الجزئي أيضا كذلك. فحينئذ لا تكون الجزئيتان متساويتين مع أن هذا القائل صرح بذلك كما صرح الشيخ.
And some people claimed that what he intended by 'generality' is 'the whole insofar as it is a whole', and he gave an example: our statement 'Everyone in the city is satisfied by this loaf' is true according to predication but not true according to the quantifier, because the totality of people is not satisfied by this loaf. And that is an error; otherwise the ruling of the particular would also be that. Then the two particulars would not be equal despite the fact that this claimant asserted that they are equal, just as the Shaykh asserted.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents a mistaken view attributed to al-Khunaji and refutes it. The claim is that the modality of the quantifier refers to the collective whole (al-kull min haythu huwa kull), not the distributive generality. The example: 'Everyone in the city is satisfied by this loaf' is true if interpreted as predication (each individual is satisfied) but false if interpreted as quantifier (the totality is not satisfied, because one loaf cannot feed all). The author declares this an error, because if that interpretation were correct, particular propositions would also behave differently between the two modalities, contradicting the fact that the earlier text stated they are equal. The claimant himself admitted equality, so his own position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
الكل من حيث هو كل
العموم المجموعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in al-Khunaji's claim according to the author?
A) It contradicts the example of the loaf.
B) It implies particular propositions would not be equal in truth value between the two modalities.
C) It misinterprets the word 'generality'.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Conflating collective and distributive generality leads to logical inconsistency, especially concerning the equality of particular propositions across modalities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Natural Position of the Two Modalities and Mental Modalities
Author states natural placement of modalities and defines mental necessity/possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between quantifier modality and predicate modality, and between objective and mental necessity/possibility.

Core Idea
Modalities attach to quantifiers or predicates; mental possibility is the mind's hesitation, not external reality.

In the previous lessons, you studied the eight modal propositions. Now al-Quhistani explains where each modality naturally belongs: the quantifier modality pairs with the quantifier, the predicate modality with the copula. He introduces a crucial distinction: necessity and possibility can be either objective (according to the actual state of affairs) or mental (according to the mind's state of knowledge). Let's explore these points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modalities
Look at the Arabic: 'جهة السور' (quantifier modality) and 'جهة الحمل' (predicate modality). Notice their natural positions: the quantifier modality attaches to the quantifier word (e.g., 'possibly all'), while the predicate modality attaches to the copula (e.g., 'all are possibly').
2
Understand the rule of extension
The author says any change from these natural positions is by way of extension (توسع). This means deviations are allowed but require justification.
Extension here refers to metaphorical or rhetorical usage.
3
Distinguish objective vs. mental
The passage says necessity and possibility can be 'بحسب نفس الأمر' (according to the actual state of affairs) or 'بحسب الذهن' (according to the mind). The mental kind is called 'ضرورة ذهنية' and 'إمكان ذهني'.
Objective modality concerns how things actually are; mental modality concerns what the mind knows or judges.
4
Define mental possibility
Mental possibility is described as 'تردد الذهن' (the mind's hesitation) and is named 'الاحتمال' (probability). It refers to uncertainty, not actual possibility.
For example, when you say 'It is possible that Zayd is writing' because you are unsure, that is mental possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموضع الطبيعي للجهتين] و موضع جهة السور الطبيعي أن تقرن بالسور و موضع جهة الحمل الطبيعي أن تقرن بالرابطة. فلو غير عن ذلك لكان على سبيل التوسع. [المبحث] الخامس الضرورة و الإمكان الذهنيتان] الضرورة و الإمكان كما تكون بحسب نفس الأمر كما عرف، فقد تكونان بحسب الذهن و تستى «ضرورة ذهنية» و «إمكاناً ذهنياً»، و هو تردّد الذهن المسمّى بـ«الاحتمال».
[The natural position of the two modalities] And the natural position of the modality of the quantifier is that it is paired with the quantifier, and the natural position of the modality of predication is that it is paired with the copula. So if it were changed from that, it would be by way of extension. [The fifth topic: Mental necessity and possibility] Necessity and possibility just as they are according to the actual state of affairs, as known, may also be according to the mind, and are called 'mental necessity' and 'mental possibility', and it is the hesitation of the mind called 'probability'.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of modalities with two points. First, the natural grammatical position: the modality of the quantifier attaches to the quantifier word (e.g., 'possibly all'), while the modality of predication attaches to the copula (e.g., 'all are possibly'). Any deviation is metaphorical extension. Second, it introduces the fifth topic: necessity and possibility can be objective (bi-ḥasb nafs al-amr) or mental (bi-ḥasb al-dhihn). Mental possibility is the mind's hesitation, known as probability (iḥtimāl). For example, when we say 'It is possible that Zayd is writing' based on uncertainty, that is mental possibility, not objective modal status.

Key Takeaway
Mental possibility is the mind's hesitation, distinct from objective possibility; modalities have natural positions in propositions.
