Mental vs External Modalities: Necessity and Possibility
Author defines mental and external necessity and possibility, showing their relationships

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental and external necessity and possibility, and state which is more specific/general.

Core Idea
Mental necessity is a subset of external necessity; mental possibility is a superset of external possibility.

This lesson unpacks a fundamental distinction in modal logic: the difference between mental (logical) and external (real) necessity and possibility. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating propositions in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where necessity and possibility are key concepts for arguments about existence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs of modal terms
The passage contrasts 'mental necessity' (al-ḍarūra al-dhihniyya) with 'external necessity' (al-ḍarūra al-khārijiyya), and 'mental possibility' (al-imkān al-dhihnī) with 'external possibility' (al-imkān al-khārijī).
2
Understand the relationship for necessity
The author states that mental necessity is 'more specific' (akhṣṣ) than external necessity. This means every mentally necessary proposition is also externally necessary, but not vice versa. The reasoning: if the mind judges something as necessary purely by understanding its terms (self-evident truths), it must hold in reality; otherwise, certainty would be lost. However, theoretical truths (naẓariyyāt) may be externally necessary without being mentally necessary for everyone.
Example: 'The whole is greater than its part' is mentally necessary – once you understand the terms, you cannot deny it.
3
Understand the relationship for possibility
Mental possibility is 'more general' (a‘amm) than external possibility. That is, everything that is externally possible is mentally possible, but the mind may consider something possible that is actually impossible in external reality (e.g., imagining a mountain of gold). Mental possibility includes whatever the mind does not judge as impossible, while external possibility is constrained by reality.
Example: 'A mountain existing entirely of gold' is mentally possible but not externally possible under normal conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في الجهة و الضرورة الذهنية أخض من الخارجية، لأن كل ما جزم الذهن بنسبة محمولها إلى موضوعها بمجرد تصوّر طرفيها كان في نفس الأمر كذلك، وإلا ارتفع الأمان عن البديهيات. و لا ينعكس، كما في النظريات. و حينئذ يكون الإمكان الذهني أعم من الخارجي.
Chapter Five on Modality. Mental necessity is more specific than external necessity, because whenever the mind determines the relation of its predicate to its subject merely by conceptualizing its two extremes, it is in reality the same, otherwise certainty in self-evident truths would be lost. And it does not reverse, as in theoretical matters. And then mental possibility is more general than external possibility.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage defines and compares mental and external modalities. The author states that mental necessity (al-ḍarūra al-dhihniyya) is more specific (akhṣṣ) than external necessity (al-ḍarūra al-khārijiyya). The reasoning is that if the mind affirms a proposition's truth simply by understanding its terms, that proposition must hold in external reality; otherwise, no self-evident truth could be trusted. So mental necessity is a subset of external necessity. The converse does not hold, as in theoretical matters where external necessity may exist without mental necessity. Then the author adds that mental possibility (al-imkān al-dhihnī) is more general (a‘amm) than external possibility. This distinction is fundamental for understanding modal judgments in logic.

Key Terms
الضرورة الذهنية
الضرورة الخارجية
الإمكان الذهني
الإمكان الخارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Mental necessity is broader than external necessity.
B) Mental possibility is more specific than external possibility.
C) Mental necessity is more specific than external necessity.
D) External possibility is more general than mental possibility.

Key Takeaway
Mental necessity is a subset of external necessity, and mental possibility is a superset of external possibility; this distinction is essential for accurate modal reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When Does a Proposition Become Multiple? The Rule of Predicated Parts
Author states the rule for multiplicity of propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a single expression contains multiple propositions based on the rule of predicated parts.

Core Idea
A proposition multiplies when its subject or predicate contains multiple predicated parts.

In logic, a single sentence can hide multiple propositions. The key is whether the terms are 'predicated parts' or not. This rule is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam, where compound rulings must be properly decomposed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's subject and predicate
Break the sentence into subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول). Look for multiple terms in either part.
e.g., in 'Man and horse are animal', the subject 'Man and horse' has two terms.
2
Check if the terms are 'predicated parts'
Verify that each part could stand as a separate predicate or subject in its own proposition. Exclude cases like 'ten is seven and three' where the parts are not logically independent.
The author calls these 'الأجزاء المحمولة' (predicated parts).
3
Apply the rule of multiplicity
If the proposition has multiple predicated parts, it becomes multiple propositions. For example, 'Man is body and rational' splits into 'Man is body' and 'Man is rational'.
The rule preserves quantity and quality of the original except when parts of the subject are more general than the whole.
4
Handle the exception
If the multiplicity arises from subject parts, check if the part is more general than the whole. If so, the original universal proposition does not split into universal parts.
Example: 'Every rational-animal is man' has 'animal' as a part more general than 'rational-animal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس في وحدة القضية وتعدّدها. تعدّد أطراف القضية يستلزم تعدد القضية مهما تعدد معنى موضوع القضيّة أو محمولها كقولنا: الإنسان و الفرس حيوان أو الإنسان جسم و ناطق، أو تركب أحدهما من الأجزاء المحمولة، تعدّدت القضية. و التعدد تحفظ كمية الأصل وكيفيته و جمثه، إلا التعدد بحسب أجزاء الموضوع لأنه لا يحفظ الكلية لجواز كون الجزء أعم من الكل. و احترزنا بـ الأجزاء المحمولة عن مثل قولنا: العشرة سبعة وثلاثة و السبعة والثلاثة عشرة فإنه لا تعدّد فيه. تعددت القضية: هذا منقوض بأمثلة كثيرة: كما نقول كاذباً: بعض الحيوان إنسان فرس فإنه لا يساوي هذين القولين الصادقين: بعض الحيوان إنسان و بعض الحيوان فرس .. وكما نقول كاذباً: بعض الإنسان الفرس حيوان فإنه لا يساوي هذين القولين الصادقين: بعض الإنسان حيوان و بعض الفرس حيوان؛ وكذلك قولنا الصادق كل حيوان ناطق إنسان لا يساوي المجموع من قولنا الكاذب كل حيوان إنسان و قولنا الصادق كل ناطق إنسان. وكذلك قولنا الصادق: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان فرس فإنّه لا يساوي هذين القولين الكاذبين: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان و لا شيء من الحيوان بفرس. أم المصنف فقد يشير إشارة ناقصة إلى بعض هذه النقوض تبعاً للخونجي انظر الهامش التالي. كما في قولنا كل حيوان ناطق إنسان و لا شيء من الفرس بحيوان ناطق و لا يصدق إنسان أو لا شيء من الفرس بحيوان لأن الجزء أي الحيوان أعم من الكل أي الحيوان الناطق. راجع كشف الأسرار، ص ١١٦ س ٥-٩- فإنّه فسّر التعدّد بـ الاستلزام لا بـ التلازم.
Chapter Six on the Unity and Multiplicity of the Proposition. The multiplicity of the terms of a proposition necessitates the multiplicity of the proposition: whenever the meaning of the subject or predicate is multiple, as in our saying 'Man and horse are animal' or 'Man is body and rational', or one of them is composed of predicated parts, the proposition becomes multiple. And the multiplicity preserves the quantity and quality and... of the original, except multiplicity according to the parts of the subject because it does not preserve universality, since the part may be more general than the whole. And we restricted ourselves to 'predicated parts' to exclude such as our saying 'Ten is seven and three' and 'Seven and three are ten', for there is no multiplicity in it. 'The proposition becomes multiple': This is refuted by many examples: such as our saying falsely 'Some animal is a man-horse' which is not equivalent to these two true statements 'Some animal is a man' and 'Some animal is a horse'; and as we say falsely 'Some man-horse is animal' which is not equivalent to these two true statements 'Some man is animal' and 'Some horse is animal'; and likewise our true saying 'Every rational-animal is man' is not equivalent to the conjunction of our false saying 'Every animal is man' and our true saying 'Every rational is man'; and likewise our true saying 'No animal is a man-horse' is not equivalent to these two false statements 'No animal is a man' and 'No animal is a horse'. But the author may have given an incomplete indication of some of these refutations following al-Khūnajī; see the following footnote. As in our saying 'Every rational-animal is man' and 'No horse is a rational-animal' and it is not true 'No horse is man' or 'No horse is animal' because the part, i.e. 'animal', is more general than the whole, i.e. 'rational-animal'. Refer to Kashf al-Asrār, p. 116 lines 5-9, for he interpreted 'multiplicity' as 'entailment' not 'concomitance'.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions under which a proposition is considered multiple. The author first states the rule: multiplicity of the terms (subject or predicate) implies multiplicity of the proposition. For example, 'Man and horse are animal' is actually two propositions: 'Man is animal' and 'Horse is animal'. Similarly, 'Man is body and rational' is two: 'Man is body' and 'Man is rational'. However, the author adds an exception: multiplicity according to the parts of the subject does not preserve universality, because the part may be more general than the whole. For instance, if we have 'Every rational-animal is man', 'animal' is a part of the subject and is more general than 'rational-animal', so the proposition does not split into two universal statements. The author then excludes cases like 'Ten is seven and three' where there is no multiplicity because the parts are not predicated separately. The text then presents a series of objections that refute the initial rule. The objector shows that the rule fails for many examples: e.g., 'Some animal is a man-horse' (false) is not equivalent to the conjunction of two true statements; similarly for other examples. The objector notes that the author's rule is incomplete and refers to al-Khūnajī. Finally, there is a reference to Kashf al-Asrār for a different interpretation of multiplicity as entailment rather than concomitance.

Key Terms
تعدد القضية
الأجزاء المحمولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a proposition that does NOT become multiple despite having multiple terms?
A) 'Man and horse are animal'
B) 'Man is body and rational'
C) 'Ten is seven and three'
D) 'Some animal is a man-horse'

Key Takeaway
A proposition becomes multiple only when the terms are predicated parts, not mere numerical composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the number of propositions in the following expression from a fiqh text: 'Every intoxicant and every gambling is haram.' Use the rule of predicated parts to justify your answer.
?
Given the proposition 'The number 12 is 4 and 8', explain why it does not split into multiple propositions according to the author's restriction.

Refuting the Rule: Counterexamples to Multiplicity
Objections (nuqud) against the rule of multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct counterexamples (nuqud) that demonstrate the insufficiency of the simple multiplicity rule.

Core Idea
The rule of multiplicity fails when compound propositions are not truth-functionally equivalent to conjunctions of simple propositions.

After laying down the rule, the author immediately presents objections that show it is not universally true. These counterexamples are essential for a precise understanding of logical multiplicity and are a standard tool in jadal (scholarly debate).

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule under attack
Recall that the rule claims: if a proposition has multiple predicated parts, it becomes multiple propositions (i.e., equivalent to their conjunction).
The objector challenges this equivalence.
2
Analyze the first counterexample
Consider 'Some animal is a man-horse' (false). The objector says it is not equivalent to 'Some animal is a man' (true) AND 'Some animal is a horse' (true). The conjunction is true, but the compound is false, so they are not equivalent.
This shows multiplicity does not behave as per the rule.
3
Examine other counterexamples
Work through the remaining examples: 'Some man-horse is animal' (false) vs. 'Some man is animal' and 'Some horse is animal' (both true); 'Every rational-animal is man' (true) vs. 'Every animal is man' (false) and 'Every rational is man' (true); 'No animal is a man-horse' (true) vs. 'No animal is a man' (false) and 'No animal is a horse' (false).
In each, the compound proposition differs in truth-value from the conjunction of its supposed parts.
4
Understand the logical lesson
The counterexamples prove that multiplying terms does not always yield a conjunction of independent propositions. The truth of the whole cannot always be reduced to the truth of its parts.
This refines the rule: multiplicity requires not just separate terms but also logical independence and appropriate quantifiers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

10 min
When Propositions Multiply: Rule and Exception
Author defines when a proposition becomes multiple and states the exception for subject parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a statement represents one or multiple propositions, and identify cases where multiplicity does not preserve universality.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of terms multiplies a proposition unless the multiplicity is in subject parts, where universality may break.

This lesson unpacks the logical rule for propositional multiplicity. A proposition becomes multiple when its subject or predicate contains multiple independently predicable meanings. However, if the multiplicity arises from parts of the subject, the original quantity (especially universality) may not be preserved. We will explore examples and the reasoning behind this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Locate the subject (mawḍūʿ) and predicate (maḥmūl) in the proposition. For example, in 'Man and horse are animal', 'Man and horse' is the subject, 'animal' is the predicate.
2
Check for multiple independently predicable parts
Ask: Can each part of the subject or predicate be separately predicated of the other term? In 'Man and horse are animal', 'Man is animal' and 'Horse is animal' are both true, so the proposition is multiple.
3
Apply the exception for subject parts
If the multiplicity arises from parts of the subject (e.g., 'Every rational animal is a man'), splitting may not preserve truth because a part (animal) is more general than the whole (rational animal). The universal quantifier is lost.
4
Distinguish from non-predicable parts
Guard against cases like 'Ten is seven and three'. Here 'seven' and 'three' are not independently predicable of 'ten' (since 'ten is seven' is false). No multiplicity occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعدد أطراف القضية يستلزم تعدد القضية مهما تعدد معنى موضوع القضيّة أو محمولها كقولنا: الإنسان و الفرس حيوان» أو «الإنسان جسم و ناطق»، أو تركب أحدهما من الأجزاء المحمولة، تعدّدت القضية. و التعدد تحفظ كمية الأصل وكيفيته و جمثه، إلا التعدد بحسب أجزاء الموضوع لأنه لا يحفظ الكلية لجواز كون الجزء أعم من الكل. و احترزنا بـ «الأجزاء المحمولة» عن مثل قولنا: «العشرة سبعة وثلاثة» و «السبعة والثلاثة عشرة» فإنه لا تعدّد فيه.
The multiplicity of the terms of the proposition necessitates the multiplicity of the proposition: whenever the meaning of the subject or the predicate of the proposition is multiple, like our statement 'Man and horse are animal' or 'Man is body and rational', or one of them is composed of predicated parts, the proposition becomes multiple. And the multiplicity preserves the quantity and quality and aspect of the original, except for multiplicity according to the parts of the subject because it does not preserve universality due to the possibility that the part is more general than the whole. And we guarded by 'predicated parts' against such as our statement 'Ten is seven and three' and 'Seven and three are ten', for there is no multiplicity in it.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage defines when a single proposition becomes multiple. The key is multiplicity in the meaning of subject or predicate. Examples: 'Man and horse are animal' implies two propositions: 'Man is animal' and 'Horse is animal'. 'Man is body and rational' implies 'Man is body' and 'Man is rational'. The author then states a condition: such multiplicity preserves the original quantity (universal/particular), quality (affirmative/negative), and aspect, except when multiplicity arises from parts of the subject. Why? Because a part of the subject may be more general than the whole subject, breaking universality. For example, in 'Every animal rational is man', the part 'animal' is broader than 'animal rational', so the universal 'Every animal rational is man' cannot be split into 'Every animal is man' (false) and 'Every rational is man' (true) without loss. The author also excludes cases like 'Ten is seven and three' because 'seven' and 'three' are not independently predicable of 'ten'—'ten is seven' is false. So only when each part is independently predicable does multiplicity occur. In Islamic law, similar: 'Every intoxicant and gambling is prohibited' splits into two true propositions, but 'Every prohibition is either eating or drinking' does not, because the predicate parts are not independent.

Key Takeaway
A proposition multiplies if its terms have multiple independent meanings, but subject parts require care because they may not preserve universality.

Counterexamples to the Multiplicity Rule
Author presents counterexamples refuting the claim that multiple subject parts always cause proposition multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct counterexamples that refute the claim that a compound subject proposition is equivalent to the conjunction of its part-subject propositions.

Core Idea
A single counterexample can dismantle a universal logical rule.

Earlier, a rule was proposed: if a proposition's subject has multiple parts, the proposition is actually multiple propositions. This passage attacks that rule with four counterexamples from the logic of subject-predicate combinations. By analyzing these, you will learn how to test and refute logical generalizations using carefully constructed examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target rule
The rule under attack: any proposition with a compound subject (e.g., 'Some A is B') where the subject has parts should be equivalent to the sum of propositions with each part. Note that the rule claims logical equivalence, not just implication.
The rule is implicit; the counterexamples show it fails.
2
Examine the first counterexample
"Some animal is man horse" is false because no animal can be both man and horse. But its parts "Some animal is man" and "Some animal is horse" are both true. Since the compound is false while the parts are true, they cannot be equivalent.
This uses the falsity of the compound versus truth of the parts.
3
Examine the remaining counterexamples
The second example: "Some man the horse is animal" (false) versus true parts. The third: "Every animal rational is man" (true) versus false+true parts. The fourth: "No animal is man horse" (true) versus two false parts. Each shows non-equivalence.
Notice the pattern: the truth values of compound and sum differ.
4
Distinguish affirmative from negative cases
The author notes that the refutation works for affirmative propositions (counterexamples 1-3) but not for negative ones (example 4). Why? Because for negatives, the compound true with false parts is clear, but the rule may still hold under certain conditions? Actually, the author says it's 'not necessary' for negatives, meaning the objection is not as decisive.
This nuance is important: the rule may be partially salvageable for negatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا منقوض بأمثلة كثيرة: كما نقول كاذباً: «بعض الحيوان إنسان فرس فإنه لا يساوي هذين القولين الصادقين: «بعض الحيوان إنسان» و «بعض الحيوان فرس» .. وكما نقول كاذباً: «بعض الإنسان الفرس حيوان» فإنه لا يساوي هذين القولين الصادقين: «بعض الإنسان حيوان» و «بعض الفرس حيوان»؛ وكذلك قولنا الصادق كل حيوان ناطق إنسان لا يساوي المجموع من قولنا الكاذب كل حيوان إنسان و قولنا الصادق «كل ناطق إنسان» . وكذلك قولنا الصادق: «لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان فرس فإنّه لا يساوي هذين القولين الكاذبين: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان» و «لا شيء من الحيوان بفرس». هذا في الموجبة صحيح، و أما في السالبة، فغير لازم و ذلك ظاهر.
This is refuted by many examples: as we say falsely 'Some animal is man horse' for it does not equal these two true statements: 'Some animal is man' and 'Some animal is horse'. And as we say falsely 'Some man the horse is animal' for it does not equal these two true statements: 'Some man is animal' and 'Some horse is animal'. And similarly our true statement 'Every animal rational is man' does not equal the aggregate of our false statement 'Every animal is man' and our true statement 'Every rational is man'. And similarly our true statement 'No animal is man horse' for it does not equal these two false statements: 'No animal is man' and 'No animal is horse'. This is correct for the affirmative, but as for the negative, it is not necessary and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents counterexamples to the earlier claim that multiplicity of subject parts entails multiplicity of propositions. The original claim was that if the subject has multiple parts, the proposition becomes multiple. But here it is shown that sometimes the compound proposition is not equivalent to the sum of its parts. For instance, the false proposition 'Some animal is man horse' (i.e., some animal is both man and horse) is not equivalent to the two true propositions 'Some animal is man' and 'Some animal is horse' because the compound is false while the parts are true. Similarly, 'Some man the horse is animal' (some man is a horse animal?) is false while the parts are true. Also, a true compound like 'Every animal rational is man' does not equal the sum of the false 'Every animal is man' and the true 'Every rational is man'. This shows that truth conditions differ. The author then notes that this objection works for affirmative propositions but not for negative ones, because for negatives the compound true with false parts is clear. In Islamic logic, consider 'Every invalid act is either eating or drinking' – the parts may be false but compound true? Actually the examples show the nuance. The point is that the earlier rule is not universally valid.

Defending the Compound Predicate Rule via Reductio
Author defends original rule by showing truth of compound predicate does not require truth of its parts individually

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum argument against the claim that compound predicate truth implies part truth.

Core Idea
A compound predicate's truth does not require the truth of its individual parts; meaning shifts in composition.

In logical analysis, a common objection is that if a compound predicate is true, each part must also be true. The author refutes this using a reductio ad absurdum that leads to infinite regress, showing the change in meaning of parts when combined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that the truth of a compound predicate does not imply the truth of its parts individually, giving examples: 'The animal painted on the wall is a painted animal' is true but 'it is an animal' is false; 'Zayd is a physician' and 'Zayd is skilled' are true but 'Zayd is a skilled physician' is false.
This is a counterexample to the rule that truth of parts is necessary for compound truth.
2
Understand the author's response
The author accepts the observation but then shows the consequence if we accept the opposite inference (that compound implies parts). He uses a reductio: for a thing that is both animal and white, if 'animal white' is true, and since 'animal' is also true, we would have to affirm 'white animal white' as true, which generates an infinite regress.
This is a modus tollens: if the inference rule were valid, absurdity results, so the inference rule is invalid.
3
Analyze the core insight
The reason the examples work is that the meaning of the parts changes when combined. 'Animal' in 'painted animal' does not mean 'living animal' but 'image of an animal'. Similarly, 'physician' and 'skilled' in combination refer to a specific skill set.
This preserves the rule that a proposition's truth depends on the actual state of affairs, not on isolated parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لا يلزم من كون الشيء محمولاً جملة كونه محمولاً فرادى و لا بالعكس ا. فإنه يصدق على الحيوان المنقوش على الجدار أنه حيوان منقوش» و لا يصدق عليه أنه «حيوان». قلنا: نعم و أيضاً يصدق «زيد طبيب» إذا كان طبيباً غير ماهر و يصدق «زيد ماهر» إذا كان خياطاً ماهراً؛ ولا يصدق «زيد طبيب ماهر». و لأنه إذا صدق على الشيء «الحيوان» و «الأبيض»، فلو صدق عليه المجموع لصدّق عليه المجموع مع كل واحد منها، فيصدق عليه «الحيوان الأبيض الأبيض». و ذلك يصير مجموعاً آخر و هو يصدق مع كل واحد منها. و على هذا إلى غير النهاية: يُصم المجموع إلى المفردات حتى يصير مجموعاً آخر، وهلم جرا. و إنه هذيان. صدق المجموع دون الأفراد في المثالين إنما كان المخالفة معنى الأفراد حالة الاجتماع معناها حالة الإفراد. وكون القول «هذياناً» لا يُخرجه عن الصدق.
For if it is said: It is not necessary from a thing being a predicate as a whole that it be a predicate individually, nor the converse. For it is true of the animal painted on the wall that it is a 'painted animal' and it is not true of it that it is 'animal'. We say: Yes. And also it is true of Zayd that he is a 'physician' if he is an unskilled physician, and it is true of Zayd that he is 'skilled' if he is a skilled tailor; but it is not true that 'Zayd is a skilled physician'. And because if 'animal' and 'white' are true of a thing, then if the compound were true of it, then the compound would be true along with each one of them, so 'white animal white' would be true of it. And that becomes another compound and it is true with each one of them. And so on indefinitely: the compound accumulates with the individuals until it becomes another compound, and so forth. And that is nonsense. The truth of the compound without the individuals in the two examples was only due to the meaning of the individuals in the state of composition differing from their meaning in the state of isolation. And the statement being 'nonsense' does not remove it from truth.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: the relationship between a compound predicate and its parts. The objector claims that just because something is a predicate as a whole does not mean its parts are predicates individually, and vice versa. Example: 'The animal painted on the wall is a painted animal' is true, but 'it is an animal' is false (since it's a painting). Similarly, 'Zayd is a physician' (if he is a bad physician) and 'Zayd is skilled' (if he is a skilled tailor) are both true, but 'Zayd is a skilled physician' is false. So the truth of the compound does not require the truth of the parts. The author responds with a reductio ad absurdum: If we accept that from the truth of a compound with two parts we can infer the truth of each part, then consider a thing that is both animal and white. If the compound 'animal white' is true, then since 'animal' is also true, we would have 'white animal white' true, which generates an infinite regress of compounds, which is absurd. Therefore, the objection is based on a misunderstanding: the meaning of the parts changes when combined. In the examples, the part 'animal' in 'painted animal' does not mean the same as 'animal' alone; it is a different predicate. Similarly, 'physician' and 'skilled' in combination have a different meaning. So the original rule that multiplicity of subjects or predicates causes proposition multiplicity is not refuted by these examples. The author also notes that calling the infinite regress 'nonsense' does not affect its truth value; the regress is logically valid but leads to absurdity, which shows the premise is false. In Islamic logic, consider 'Every invalid act is a prohibited innovation' – the parts 'invalid act' and 'prohibited innovation' may have different senses combined.

Compound Predicates: When the Whole is True but Parts are False
Author rebuts the objection that compound predication implies individual predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify counterexamples where a compound predicate is true but one of its individual parts is false, and explain why this occurs.

Core Idea
Truth of a compound does not guarantee truth of its parts.

In this passage, the author tackles a common logical assumption: if a compound predicate (like 'painted animal') is true of a subject, then each part ('painted', 'animal') must also be true individually. He provides counterexamples showing that this is not always the case. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurate logical analysis, especially when dealing with definitions and descriptions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound predicate
Look for a predicate that combines two or more terms. For example, 'painted animal' in the passage.
The compound is the whole predicate considered as one unit.
2
Check the truth of the compound
Determine if the statement with the compound predicate is true (e.g., 'The animal painted on the wall is a painted animal' is true).
3
Test each part individually
See if each part can be predicated alone. In the example, 'The animal painted on the wall is an animal' is false because it is a painting, not a real animal.
A part may be false even if the compound is true.
4
Identify the reason for divergence
Note that the meaning of the part shifts when combined. The author says 'the meaning of the individuals in the state of composition differs from their meaning in the state of isolation.'
This semantic shift invalidates the automatic inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفع دخل: فإن قيل: لا يلزم من كون الشيء محمولاً جملة كونه محمولاً فرادى و لا بالعكس. فإنه يصدق على الحيوان المنقوش على الجدار أنه حيوان منقوش» و لا يصدق عليه أنه «حيوان». و أيضاً يصدق «زيد طبيب» إذا كان طبيباً غير ماهر و يصدق «زيد ماهر» إذا كان خياطاً ماهراً؛ ولا يصدق «زيد طبيب ماهر». و لأنه إذا صدق على الشيء «الحيوان» و «الأبيض»، فلو صدق عليه المجموع لصدّق عليه المجموع مع كل واحد منها، فيصدق عليه «الحيوان الأبيض الأبيض». و ذلك يصير مجموعاً آخر و هو يصدق مع كل واحد منها. و على هذا إلى غير النهاية: يُصم المجموع إلى المفردات حتى يصير مجموعاً آخر، وهلم جرا. و إنه هذيان. صدق المجموع دون الأفراد في المثالين إنما كان المخالفة معنى الأفراد حالة الاجتماع معناها حالة الإفراد. وكون القول «هذياناً» لا يُخرجه عن الصدق.

قد لا يصح حمل الشيء وحده و يصح حمل المجموع المركب منه و من غيره، و لا يصح سلب المجموع و يصح سلب الجزء كما في الأجزاء الغير المحمولة: فإنه لا يصدق «البيت سقف» و يصدق «سقف و جدار» لا يصدق سلب المجموع و يصدق سلب الجزء.

قد يصح حمل الشيء فرادى و لا يصح مع الغير و يصح سلب المجموع دون الجزء: فإنه يصدق قولنا: الإنسان حيوان و لا يصدق «حيوان صهال» و يُسلب هذا المجموع دون الجزء.

و أما أن الشيئين يحمل كل واحد منهما وحده و لا يحمل المجموع أو بالعكس فذلك ضروري البطلان.
Rebuttal of an objection: For it is said: From the fact that a thing is predicated as a compound, it does not necessarily follow that it is predicated individually, nor conversely. For the statement 'the animal painted on the wall' is true of it as 'a painted animal', but it is not true of it as 'animal'. Also, 'Zayd is a doctor' is true if he is an unskilled doctor, and 'Zayd is skilled' is true if he is a skilled tailor; but 'Zayd is a skilled doctor' is not true. And because if 'animal' and 'white' are true of a thing, then if the compound were true of it, the compound would be true together with each of them, so 'white animal white' would be true of it. And that becomes another compound, and it is true with each of them. And so on ad infinitum: the compound is joined to the atomic statements until it becomes another compound, and so forth. And it is nonsense. The truth of the compound without the individuals in the two examples is only because the meaning of the individuals in the state of composition differs from their meaning in the state of isolation. And that the statement is 'nonsense' does not remove it from truth.

Sometimes a thing is not valid to be predicated alone but it is valid to be predicated as a compound composed of it and another, and the negation of the compound is not valid while the negation of the part is valid, as in the non-predicative parts: for 'the house is a roof' is not true, but 'a roof and a wall' is true, and the negation of the compound is not true while the negation of the part is true.

Sometimes a thing is valid to be predicated individually but not with another, and the negation of the compound is valid but not the negation of the part: for our saying 'man is an animal' is true, but 'man is a neighing animal' is not true, and this compound is negated without negating the part.

As for two things each of which is valid to be predicated alone but the compound is not valid, or vice versa, that is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical discussion about the relation between a compound predicate and its individual parts when applied to a subject. The author addresses an objection that one might think that if a term is predicable as a compound (together with another term), it must also be predicable individually, and vice versa. The author refutes this by giving examples.

First, he gives the example of an animal painted on a wall: we can say 'it is a painted animal' but cannot say 'it is an animal' (since it is a mere painting). So the compound 'painted animal' is true, but 'animal' alone is false. Another example: Zayd is an unskilled doctor, so 'Zayd is a doctor' is true, and Zayd is a skilled tailor, so 'Zayd is skilled' is true, but 'Zayd is a skilled doctor' is false because his skill is in tailoring, not medicine. This shows that the compound can be true while one of its parts is false.

Then the author imagines a scenario: if we have two predicates, 'animal' and 'white', both true of something, then if the compound 'white animal' were true, we would have to say 'white animal white' ad infinitum, which would be nonsense. But he notes that even if the argument is nonsense, the conclusion (that the compound may be true without the individuals) is still true; the 'nonsense' lies in the infinite regress, not in the conclusion.

He then explains that the truth of the compound without the individuals is due to a change in meaning: the individual terms have different meanings when combined than when isolated.

Next, he categorizes three cases:
1. Sometimes a term cannot be predicated alone but can be part of a compound, and the negation of the compound fails while negation of the part holds. Example: 'the house is a roof' is false, but 'the house is a roof and a wall' is true (a house consists of both). Negating the compound (the house is not a roof and a wall) is false, but negating the part (the house is not a roof) is true.
2. Sometimes a term is predicable alone but not in a compound, and negation of the compound holds while negation of the part fails. Example: 'man is an animal' is true, but 'man is a neighing animal' is false; its negation is true, but the negation of 'animal' alone is false.
3. The claim that two things are each individually predicable but not together, or vice versa, is necessarily false—because if each is true separately, the conjunction must be true (by the rule of conjunction), and if the conjunction is false, at least one must be false. So this case is impossible.

Thus the passage clarifies the conditions under which compound predicates and their parts can be truthfully asserted or denied.

مجموع
فرادى
سلب
هذيان

B) 'Zayd is a skilled doctor' (true) and 'Zayd is a skilled tailor' (false).

A compound predicate can be true even if one of its parts is false when taken alone, due to a change in meaning.

The Three Logical Cases of Compound Predication
Author categorizes the possible truth relations between compound predicates and their parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any given example of compound predication into one of the three cases identified by the author.

Core Idea
Compound predication falls into three distinct logical patterns.

After refuting the general assumption, the author classifies the possible relationships between a compound predicate and its parts. He identifies three cases: (1) the part alone is false but the compound is true, (2) the part alone is true but the compound is false, and (3) the impossible scenario where both parts are individually true but the compound is false (or vice versa). Mastering these categories sharpens your logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and compound predicate
Take an example from the passage, like 'the house is a roof and a wall'. The subject is 'the house', the compound predicate is 'a roof and a wall'.
2
Test each part individually
Check 'the house is a roof' (false) and 'the house is a wall' (false). Note: here both parts are false, but the compound is true. That matches case 1.
Case 1: part(s) alone false, compound true.
3
Test the compound and its negation
Check negation: 'the house is not a roof and a wall' is false, while 'the house is not a roof' is true. This aligns with case 1 where negation of compound fails but negation of part holds.
4
Compare with case 2 example
Use 'man is a neighing animal': 'man is an animal' is true, but 'man is a neighing animal' is false. Negation of compound is true, negation of part is false.
Case 2: part true, compound false.
5
Recognize case 3 as impossible
The author states that if each part is true individually, the compound must be true; if the compound is false, at least one part must be false. This is a logical necessity.
Case 3: impossible scenario.

دفع دخل: فإن قيل: لا يلزم من كون الشيء محمولاً جملة كونه محمولاً فرادى و لا بالعكس. فإنه يصدق على الحيوان المنقوش على الجدار أنه حيوان منقوش» و لا يصدق عليه أنه «حيوان». و أيضاً يصدق «زيد طبيب» إذا كان طبيباً غير ماهر و يصدق «زيد ماهر» إذا كان خياطاً ماهراً؛ ولا يصدق «زيد طبيب ماهر». و لأنه إذا صدق على الشيء «الحيوان» و «الأبيض»، فلو صدق عليه المجموع لصدّق عليه المجموع مع كل واحد منها، فيصدق عليه «الحيوان الأبيض الأبيض». و ذلك يصير مجموعاً آخر و هو يصدق مع كل واحد منها. و على هذا إلى غير النهاية: يُصم المجموع إلى المفردات حتى يصير مجموعاً آخر، وهلم جرا. و إنه هذيان. صدق المجموع دون الأفراد في المثالين إنما كان المخالفة معنى الأفراد حالة الاجتماع معناها حالة الإفراد. وكون القول «هذياناً» لا يُخرجه عن الصدق.

قد لا يصح حمل الشيء وحده و يصح حمل المجموع المركب منه و من غيره، و لا يصح سلب المجموع و يصح سلب الجزء كما في الأجزاء الغير المحمولة: فإنه لا يصدق «البيت سقف» و يصدق «سقف و جدار» لا يصدق سلب المجموع و يصدق سلب الجزء.

قد يصح حمل الشيء فرادى و لا يصح مع الغير و يصح سلب المجموع دون الجزء: فإنه يصدق قولنا: الإنسان حيوان و لا يصدق «حيوان صهال» و يُسلب هذا المجموع دون الجزء.

و أما أن الشيئين يحمل كل واحد منهما وحده و لا يحمل المجموع أو بالعكس فذلك ضروري البطلان.
Rebuttal of an objection: For it is said: From the fact that a thing is predicated as a compound, it does not necessarily follow that it is predicated individually, nor conversely. For the statement 'the animal painted on the wall' is true of it as 'a painted animal', but it is not true of it as 'animal'. Also, 'Zayd is a doctor' is true if he is an unskilled doctor, and 'Zayd is skilled' is true if he is a skilled tailor; but 'Zayd is a skilled doctor' is not true. And because if 'animal' and 'white' are true of a thing, then if the compound were true of it, the compound would be true together with each of them, so 'white animal white' would be true of it. And that becomes another compound, and it is true with each of them. And so on ad infinitum: the compound is joined to the atomic statements until it becomes another compound, and so forth. And it is nonsense. The truth of the compound without the individuals in the two examples is only because the meaning of the individuals in the state of composition differs from their meaning in the state of isolation. And that the statement is 'nonsense' does not remove it from truth.

Sometimes a thing is not valid to be predicated alone but it is valid to be predicated as a compound composed of it and another, and the negation of the compound is not valid while the negation of the part is valid, as in the non-predicative parts: for 'the house is a roof' is not true, but 'a roof and a wall' is true, and the negation of the compound is not true while the negation of the part is true.

Sometimes a thing is valid to be predicated individually but not with another, and the negation of the compound is valid but not the negation of the part: for our saying 'man is an animal' is true, but 'man is a neighing animal' is not true, and this compound is negated without negating the part.

As for two things each of which is valid to be predicated alone but the compound is not valid, or vice versa, that is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical discussion about the relation between a compound predicate and its individual parts when applied to a subject. The author addresses an objection that one might think that if a term is predicable as a compound (together with another term), it must also be predicable individually, and vice versa. The author refutes this by giving examples.

First, he gives the example of an animal painted on a wall: we can say 'it is a painted animal' but cannot say 'it is an animal' (since it is a mere painting). So the compound 'painted animal' is true, but 'animal' alone is false. Another example: Zayd is an unskilled doctor, so 'Zayd is a doctor' is true, and Zayd is a skilled tailor, so 'Zayd is skilled' is true, but 'Zayd is a skilled doctor' is false because his skill is in tailoring, not medicine. This shows that the compound can be true while one of its parts is false.

Then the author imagines a scenario: if we have two predicates, 'animal' and 'white', both true of something, then if the compound 'white animal' were true, we would have to say 'white animal white' ad infinitum, which would be nonsense. But he notes that even if the argument is nonsense, the conclusion (that the compound may be true without the individuals) is still true; the 'nonsense' lies in the infinite regress, not in the conclusion.

He then explains that the truth of the compound without the individuals is due to a change in meaning: the individual terms have different meanings when combined than when isolated.

Next, he categorizes three cases:
1. Sometimes a term cannot be predicated alone but can be part of a compound, and the negation of the compound fails while negation of the part holds. Example: 'the house is a roof' is false, but 'the house is a roof and a wall' is true (a house consists of both). Negating the compound (the house is not a roof and a wall) is false, but negating the part (the house is not a roof) is true.
2. Sometimes a term is predicable alone but not in a compound, and negation of the compound holds while negation of the part fails. Example: 'man is an animal' is true, but 'man is a neighing animal' is false; its negation is true, but the negation of 'animal' alone is false.
3. The claim that two things are each individually predicable but not together, or vice versa, is necessarily false—because if each is true separately, the conjunction must be true (by the rule of conjunction), and if the conjunction is false, at least one must be false. So this case is impossible.

Thus the passage clarifies the conditions under which compound predicates and their parts can be truthfully asserted or denied.

Key Takeaway
There are three possible relations between a compound predicate and its parts; knowing them prevents logical mistakes.

Understanding Predication of Composites
Author enumerates cases of predication alone vs. composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two cases of predicating a thing alone versus as part of a composite, and apply the principle that it is impossible for both parts to be predicable singly but not together.

Core Idea
Predication of a composite does not follow from predication of its parts—context and combination matter.

In defining terms and constructing propositions in Islamic logic, it is crucial to understand when a predicate applies to a subject. This lesson examines two cases from the classical text: first, where a thing is not predicable alone but becomes predicable when combined with another; second, where a thing is predicable alone but not when combined. These distinctions are vital for accurate definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: (1) a thing not predicable alone but predicable as part of a composite, (2) a thing predicable alone but not when combined with another. Also note the corresponding negation patterns.
The passage uses Arabic terms 'حمل' (predication) and 'سلب' (negation).
2
Parse case 1 example
Examine the example: 'The house is a roof' is false, but 'The house is a roof and wall' is true. The composite predicate 'roof and wall' applies even though 'roof' alone does not. The negation of the composite is false, while negation of the part is true.
This occurs with 'الأجزاء الغير المحمولة' (parts that are not predicated alone).
3
Parse case 2 example
Examine the example: 'Man is an animal' is true, but 'Man is an animal neighing' is false. The single predicate 'animal' applies, but the composite 'animal neighing' does not. The composite is negated while the part is not.
The composite adds a differentiating property that does not hold for the subject.
4
Understand the final principle
The author concludes that it is necessarily false for two things each to be predicable singly but not together (or vice versa). That is, if each part is true of the subject, the conjunction must be true; if the conjunction is true, each part must be true. This is a logical principle about truth-functional conjunction.
This principle is 'ضروري البطلان' (necessarily false to claim otherwise).
5
Apply the distinction
Practice identifying which case applies to given statements. For example, 'The prayer is invalid' alone may be false, but 'The prayer is invalid due to lack of intention' may be true (case 1). Conversely, 'Zayd is a writer' is true, but 'Zayd is a writer and a poet' may be false if he is not a poet (case 2).
Use examples from Islamic sciences like fiqh or kalam.

Source Text

١. قد لا يصح حمل الشيء وحده و يصح حمل المجموع المركب منه و من غيره، و لا يصح سلب المجموع و يصح سلب الجزء كما في الأجزاء الغير المحمولة:
فإنّه لا يصدق «البيت سقف» و يصدق «سقف و جدار»
لا يصدق سلب المجموع و يصدق سلب الجزء.

ويُسلب هذا المجموع دون الجزء.
و أما أن الشيئين يحمل كل واحد منهما وحده و لا يحمل المجموع أو بالعكس فذلك ضروري البطلان
Chapter Six: On the unity and multiplicity of the proposition. 1. It may not be correct to predicate a thing alone, but it is correct to predicate the composite consisting of it and another, and it is not correct to negate the composite, but it is correct to negate the part, as in the parts that are not predicated: for 'The house is a roof' is not true, but 'roof and wall' is true; the negation of the composite is not true, but the negation of the part is true. It may be correct to predicate a thing singly and not correct with another, and it is correct to negate the composite without the part: for our statement 'Man is an animal' is true, but 'animal neighing' is not true, and this composite is negated without the part. As for two things each being predicated singly but not the composite, or the opposite, that is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage examines the conditions under which a predicate applies to a subject, focusing on the unity of the proposition. The author presents two cases. First, a thing may not be predicable alone but becomes predicable as part of a composite; for example, 'house' is not correctly called 'roof' alone, but 'roof and wall' is a valid predicate of house. The negation of the composite fails, but the negation of the part is true. Second, a thing may be predicable alone but not when combined with another; for instance, 'man' is correctly called 'animal' alone, but not 'animal neighing' (a composite of animal and a differentiating property). Here the composite can be negated while the part is not negated. The author concludes that it is necessarily false for two things each to be predicable singly but not together, or the reverse—meaning that such combinations are impossible because if each part is true of the subject, the conjunction must also be true, and if the conjunction is true, each part must be true. The passage follows a numbered example-based approach typical of logical works.

The Definition of Contradiction and the 'By Itself' Condition
Definition of contradiction and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify true contradictions by applying the definition and distinguishing cases that violate the 'by itself' condition.

Core Idea
True contradiction arises when two propositions directly oppose each other by themselves, not through an equivalent term.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational definition of contradiction in Islamic logic. The author gives a precise formulation that includes a critical proviso: the opposition must arise 'by itself' (li-dhātihī). This excludes cases where the appearance of contradiction is mediated by a term that is equivalent but not identical. Mastering this definition is essential for understanding the square of opposition and immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Identify the core components: (a) disagreement in affirmation/negation, (b) necessity of opposite truth values arising from the propositions themselves. Note the phrase 'by itself' as a key qualifier.
The definition is: 'the disagreement of two propositions in affirmation and negation such that it necessitates by itself the truth of one and the falsehood of the other.'
2
Understand the 'by itself' exclusion
The phrase 'by itself' excludes cases where the truth values are necessitated indirectly, e.g., through a term that is co-extensive but not identical to the predicate. Such opposition is not inherent.
3
Analyse the excluded example
The author gives: 'This is a man' and 'This is not rational'. 'Rational' is equivalent to 'man' in denotation but not identical in sense. The negation is not directly of the predicate 'man', so these are not contradictories.
The false opposition arises via negation of the equivalent (salb al-musāwī).
4
Grasp the direct negation condition
True contradiction requires that the negation be applied to the exact same affirmational relation (al-nisba al-ījābiyya) that was affirmed. The negative proposition must negate precisely what the affirmative affirmed.
This ensures the opposition is immediate and not mediated.
5
Recognize the eight unities
The author mentions that contradiction requires eight unities (e.g., subject, predicate, time, place, condition). These ensure that only the affirmation/negation differs, making the opposition direct. The list is not given here but will be elaborated later.
The eight unities are the conditions for the 'by itself' requirement.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في التناقض
[تعريف التناقض]
[المبحث الأول]
في تعريفه و شرائطه
التناقض هو «اختلاف القضيتين بالإيجاب و السلب بحيث يقتضي لذاته صدق إحداهما و كذب الأخرى». فقولنا «لذاته» احتراز عن الاختلاف الذي يقتضي ذلك، لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة سلب المساوي، كقولنا: «هذا إنسان» «هذا ليس بناطق». و هذا الاختلاف إنما يتحقق إذا كان السلب وارداً على النسبة الإيجابية التي حكيم عما في الموجبة بعينها. عدد الوحدات المعتبرة في التناقض و ذلك إنّما يحصل بثمانية وحدات.
Chapter Seven: On Contradiction. [Definition of Contradiction] [First Section] On its definition and conditions. Contradiction is 'the disagreement of two propositions in affirmation and negation such that it necessitates by itself the truth of one and the falsehood of the other.' Our saying 'by itself' is a precaution against the disagreement that necessitates that, but not by itself rather by means of the negation of the equivalent, like our saying: 'This is a man' 'This is not a nāṭiq (speaking/rational)'. And this disagreement only occurs if the negation is applied to the affirmational nexus that [the proposition] judged by what is in the affirmative proposition itself. The number of unities considered in contradiction is only obtained by eight unities.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage defines the logical concept of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and sets out its conditions. The author gives a precise definition: contradiction is the opposition of two propositions in affirmation and negation such that by virtue of their very essence (li-dhātihī) one is necessarily true and the other false. The phrase 'by itself' excludes cases where the opposition arises indirectly through the negation of an equivalent predicate, as in 'This is a man' and 'This is not rational' — these are not strictly contradictories because 'not rational' is not the direct negation of 'man' but rather a negation of a property that is co-extensive with man (i.e., rational animal). Such opposition does not arise from the nature of the propositions alone but from an external mediation. The author emphasizes that for true contradiction, the negation must directly oppose the exact same affirmational relation (al-nisba al-ījābiyya) contained in the affirmative proposition. Finally, he states that contradiction requires eight unities (waḥdāt) — referring to unity of subject, predicate, time, place, condition, etc. — though only the number is mentioned here, not the list. The passage is dense and foundational; students must memorize the definition and understand the proviso.

Key Terms

سلب المساوي
النسبة الإيجابية

Key Takeaway
True contradiction requires that two propositions differ only in affirmation/negation and that the opposition arises from their own content, not through an equivalent term.

Essential Conditions for Contradiction: Subject and Predicate
Author explains al-Razi's reduction of the conditions for contradiction to subject and predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two propositions are genuinely contradictory by checking unity of subject and predicate, and evaluate whether other conditions (time, place, relation, potentiality) are subsumed under the predicate.

Core Idea
True contradiction requires only unity of subject and predicate; all other conditions are aspects of the predicate.

In logic, a contradiction is not just any opposition—it requires precise conditions. Early logicians like Aristotle and al-Farabi listed multiple units (subject, predicate, time, place, relation, etc.). However, the great mutakallim and logician Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argued that these seemingly separate conditions are actually part of the predicate. In this lesson, we will explore al-Razi's critique and learn how to test for genuine contradiction using only unity of subject and predicate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions under consideration.
Take any pair of affirmative and negative propositions. For example, 'Wine is intoxicating actually' and 'Wine is not intoxicating potentially'.
The subject must be explicitly the same in both.
2
Check unity of subject.
Ensure the subject term (e.g., 'wine') is identical in both propositions. If the subject differs, there is no contradiction.
3
Check unity of predicate.
Examine the predicate carefully. Al-Razi shows that changes in time, place, relation, or mode (potential/actual) actually change the predicate itself. For example, 'sitting on the ground' is a different predicate from 'sitting on the sky'.
If the predicate is not identical, the propositions are not contradictory even if they seem opposed.
4
Apply al-Razi's reduction rule.
If a condition (time, place, relation, potentiality) is attached to the predicate, treat it as part of the predicate. Only when both subject and predicate are identical, and one is affirmed while the other is negated, do we have genuine contradiction.
This avoids false contradictions and ensures rigorous logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدد الوحدات المعتبرة في التناقض و ذلك إنّما يحصل بثمانية وحدات. القضيتين: قضيتين ب. ثمانية وحدات قد ذكر أرسطو أربعة من هذه الوحدات وحدة الموضوع والإضافة و الوضع و الزمان. (أرسطو، كتاب السفسطة الفصل الخامس 35-167228، منطق أرسطو ۱۹۸۰ ص ۸۱۵-۸۱۶). عدد الوحدات عند الفارابي] و اكتفى الفارابي بثلاث منها: قسطاس الأفكار في المنطق وحدة «الموضوع» و «المحمول» و «الزمان»، للعام الضروري بتحقق الاختلاف المذكور حينئذ. (الظاهر أن أول من نسب إلى الفارابي الاكتفاء بهذه الوحدات الثلاث أفضل الدين الخونجي (كشف الأسرار، ص (۱۲۲)؛ و لم نجد في آثار الفارابي ما يدل على اكتفائه بالموضوع المحمول و الزمان. و الذي وجدنا في آثاره هو الاكتفاء بوحدة الموضوع و المحمول و الشرط: و إنما تكونان متقابلتين بأن يكون [1] المعنى الموضوع في إحداهما هو بعينه المعنى الآخر الموضوع في الأخرى، [٢] و المعنى المحمول في إحداهما هو بعينه المحمول في الأخرى [٣] و بأن تكون الشريطة التي تشترط في إحداهما هو بعينه – أو التي سبيلها أن تشترط في إحداهما في اللفظ أو الضمير من زمان أو مكان أو جزء أو جمة أو حال أو غير ذلك هي بعينها - مشترطة أيضاً في الأخرى. (الفارابي، المنطقيات للفارابي، ج ۱ ص ۱۱۹) أو بالأولين مع وحدة السلب و الإيجاب: فإن هاهنا أشياء ينبغي أن يحتفظ بها في كل متقابلين: و هو: [1] أن يكون موضوع المتقابلين واحداً بعينه [٢] وكذلك محمولها [٣] ثم أن يكون السلب الواحد مقابلاً لإيجاب واحد، [۴] ثم أن يكون الإيجاب واحداً و السلب واحداً (الفارابي، المنطقيات للفارابي، ج ۲ ص ۷۸). و الحق أن الاكتفاء بوحدة الموضوع و المحمول و الزمان من آراء الإمام الرازي في منطق الملخص إلا أنه لم يجعل وحدة المكان من وحدة الزمان بل من وحدة المحمول: و عندي أن وحدة الموضوع و المحمول لا بد منه وإلا لم يتوارد السلب و الإيجاب على مورد واحد. فأما وحدة الإضافة فهي داخلة في وحدة المحمول لأنا إذا قلنا «زيد أبو عمرو»، «زيد ليس أبا خالد، فالمحمول في الأول أبوة عمرو، و في الثاني أبوّة خالد، و إحداهما غير الأخرى، فلم يكن المحمول واحداً في الموضعين. و كذا وحدة القوة و الفعل و إذا قلنا «الخمر مسكر» و عنينا به المسكر بالفعل و «الخمر ليس بمسكر و عنينا به كونه بحيث يقتضي الإسكار عند شرط مخصوص [أي المسكر بالقوة كان أحد المحمولين غير الآخر. وكذا وحدة المكان، فإنك إذا قلت زيد «جالس» أي على الأرض». «زيد ليس مجالس» أي «على السماء»، فالجلوس المحمول بالإيجاب في القضية الأولى غير المحمول بالسلب في الأخرى.
The number of units considered in contradiction. And that is only obtained by eight units. The two propositions: two propositions by? Eight units. Aristotle mentioned four of these units: unity of subject, relation, position, and time. (Aristotle, Sophistics, Chapter 5, 35-167228, Aristotle's Logic 1980 pp. 815-816). The number of units according to al-Farabi: and al-Farabi sufficed with three of them: Qistas al-Afkar fi al-Mantiq: unity of 'subject', 'predicate', and 'time', for the common person necessary for the realization of the mentioned difference at that time. (It appears that the first to attribute to al-Farabi the sufficiency of these three units is Afdal al-Din al-Khunaji (Kashf al-Asrar, p. 122); and we did not find in the works of al-Farabi anything indicating his sufficiency with subject, predicate, and time. What we found in his works is the sufficiency with unity of subject, predicate, and condition: and they are only opposed if [1] the subject meaning in one is exactly the subject meaning in the other, [2] and the predicate meaning in one is exactly the predicate in the other, [3] and that the condition that is conditioned in one is exactly – or which is supposed to be conditioned in one in speech or implication of time or place or part or whole or state or other – also conditioned in the other. (Al-Farabi, Mantiqiyyat al-Farabi, vol. 1 p. 119) Or with the first two along with unity of negation and affirmation: for here there are things that should be preserved in every pair of opposites: and that is: [1] that the subject of the two opposites is one and the same [2] and likewise their predicate [3] then that the one negation is opposite to one affirmation, [4] then that the affirmation is one and the negation is one. (Al-Farabi, Mantiqiyyat al-Farabi, vol. 2 p. 78). And the truth is that the sufficiency with unity of subject, predicate, and time is from the views of Imam al-Razi in Mantiq al-Mulakhkhas, except that he did not make unity of place from unity of time but from unity of predicate: and in my view, unity of subject and predicate are necessary, otherwise negation and affirmation would not apply to one locus. As for unity of relation, it is included in unity of predicate, because if we say 'Zayd is father of Amr', 'Zayd is not father of Khalid', the predicate in the first is fatherhood of Amr, and in the second fatherhood of Khalid, and one is not the other, so the predicate was not one in the two places. And likewise unity of potentiality and actuality: if we say 'Wine is intoxicating' and we mean by it the intoxicating in actuality, and 'Wine is not intoxicating' and we mean by it its being such that it necessitates intoxication at a specific condition [i.e., the intoxicating potentially], then one of the two predicates is not the other. And likewise unity of place, for if you say 'Zayd is sitting' i.e., on the ground, 'Zayd is not sitting' i.e., on the sky, then the sitting predicated affirmatively in the first proposition is not the predicate negated in the other.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage discusses the number of conditions (units) required for a genuine contradiction to occur. The author states that there are eight units in total. Aristotle mentioned four: unity of subject, relation, position, and time. The text then turns to al-Farabi's view, noting that he is often said to have sufficed with three (subject, predicate, time), but the author corrects this by citing al-Farabi's actual works. In the first citation, al-Farabi lists three conditions: unity of subject, predicate, and the condition (shart) which includes time, place, part, whole, state, etc. In a second citation, al-Farabi adds a fourth condition: unity of negation and affirmation, emphasizing that the negation must be one and the affirmation one. The author then clarifies that the view limiting to subject, predicate, and time actually belongs to Imam al-Razi, not al-Farabi. Al-Razi subsumes unity of place under unity of predicate, not time, and argues that only subject and predicate are indispensable. The author provides examples to show how other supposed units (like relation, potentiality/actuality, place) are actually variations in predicate, not separate conditions. For instance, if the predicate inherently specifies a relation (like fatherhood to a particular person) or a mode (actual vs. potential intoxication) or a location (sitting on the ground vs. sky), then changing that element changes the predicate, so there is no true contradiction. Therefore, the essential conditions are unity of subject and predicate; other factors are reducible to these.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are 'Zayd is sitting on the ground' and 'Zayd is not sitting on the sky' not a true contradiction?
A) The subject is different.
B) The predicate is different because 'sitting on the ground' and 'sitting on the sky' are not the same predicate.
C) The time is different.
D) The negation is not one.

Key Takeaway
For genuine contradiction, only unity of subject and predicate is essential; all other conditions are part of the predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Examine the following pair: 'Zayd is walking to the market' and 'Zayd is not walking to the mosque'. Are these contradictory according to al-Razi's criteria? Explain why or why not.
?
Task 2: In a fiqh debate, one scholar says: 'Riba is forbidden in all transactions of wheat' and another says: 'Riba is not forbidden in transactions of wheat if it is hand-to-hand'. Identify whether the predicate is the same. If not, rewrite the second proposition so that a genuine contradiction arises.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
The Three Required Unities for Contradiction
Author discusses al-Rāzī's reduction of contradiction conditions to three unities and subsumption of others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three unities (subject, predicate, time) required for contradiction and explain how other conditions (relation, potentiality, place, condition, part/whole) are subsumed under them according to al-Rāzī.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires same subject, same predicate, and same time—all else is subordinate.

The condition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) is central to logical reasoning, ensuring that two propositions cannot both be true or both false. While earlier logicians listed many conditions, al-Rāzī reduced them to three essential unities. In this passage, the author explains al-Rāzī's view and how other conditions like relation, potentiality, and place are subsumed under these three unities. Understanding this reduction helps you avoid false contradictions in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three unities
Read the passage and list the three unities al-Rāzī considers essential: subject (mawḍūʿ), predicate (maḥmūl), and time (zamān). Note that these must be exactly the same in both contradictory propositions.
2
Understand subsumption under predicate
The passage states that unity of relation (iḍāfa) and unity of potentiality vs actuality (quwwa wa fiʿl) are subsumed under unity of predicate. For example, 'Zayd is father of Amr' vs 'Zayd is not father of Khalid' have different predicates (fatherhood of Amr vs fatherhood of Khalid), so they are not contradictory. Similarly, 'Wine is intoxicating' (actually) vs 'Wine is not intoxicating' (potentially) have different predicates.
Subsumption means these conditions do not need separate enumeration; they are already covered by predicate unity.
3
Understand subsumption under subject
Unity of condition (sharṭ) and unity of part/whole (juzʾ wa kull) fall under unity of subject. For instance, 'The Negro is black' (all his parts) vs 'The Negro is not black' (all parts are not black) have different subjects (some parts vs all parts). Similarly, 'The white object is vision-dispersing' (as long as white) vs 'The white object is not vision-dispersing' (before white) have different subjects (body with whiteness vs body without).
4
Note the distinct status of time
Unity of time is not subsumed under unity of subject or predicate; it is separate and necessary. Unity of place is subsumed under unity of time, not vice versa. The passage argues that time and place are adverbial (ẓarfiyya) and external to the predicate, so they cannot be included in predicate unity without infinite regress.
This distinction will be explored in the next lesson.

عدد الوحدات عند الفارابي] و اكتفى الفارابي بثلاث منها: وحدة «الموضوع» و «المحمول» و «الزمان»، للعام الضروري بتحقق الاختلاف المذكور حينئذ. وحدة الموضوع و المحمول و الزمان: الظاهر أن أول من نسب إلى الفارابي الاكتفاء بهذه الوحدات الثلاث أفضل الدين الخونجي (كشف الأسرار، ص (۱۲۲)؛ و لم نجد في آثار الفارابي ما يدل على اكتفائه بالموضوع المحمول و الزمان. و الذي وجدنا في آثاره هو الاكتفاء بوحدة الموضوع و المحمول و الشرط: و إنما تكونان متقابلتين بأن يكون [1] المعنى الموضوع في إحداهما هو بعينه المعنى الآخر الموضوع في الأخرى، [٢] و المعنى المحمول في إحداهما هو بعينه المحمول في الأخرى [٣] و بأن تكون الشريطة التي تشترط في إحداهما هو بعينه – أو التي سبيلها أن تشترط في إحداهما في اللفظ أو الضمير من زمان أو مكان أو جزء أو جمة أو حال أو غير ذلك هي بعينها - مشترطة أيضاً في الأخرى. (الفارابي، المنطقيات للفارابي، ج ۱ ص ۱۱۹) أو بالأولين مع وحدة السلب و الإيجاب: فإن هاهنا أشياء ينبغي أن يحتفظ بها في كل متقابلين: و هو: [1] أن يكون موضوع المتقابلين واحداً بعينه [٢] وكذلك محمولها [٣] ثم أن يكون السلب الواحد مقابلاً لإيجاب واحد، [۴] ثم أن يكون الإيجاب واحداً و السلب واحداً (الفارابي، المنطقيات للفارابي، ج ۲ ص ۷۸). و الحق أن الاكتفاء بوحدة الموضوع و المحمول و الزمان من آراء الإمام الرازي في منطق الملخص إلا أنه لم يجعل وحدة المكان من وحدة الزمان بل من وحدة المحمول: و عندي أن وحدة الموضوع و المحمول لا بد منه وإلا لم يتوارد السلب و الإيجاب على مورد واحد. فأما وحدة الإضافة فهي داخلة في وحدة المحمول لأنا إذا قلنا «زيد أبو عمرو»، «زيد ليس أبا خالد، فالمحمول في الأول أبوة عمرو، و في الثاني أبوّة خالد، و إحداهما غير الأخرى، فلم يكن المحمول واحداً في الموضعين. و كذا وحدة القوة و الفعل و إذا قلنا «الخمر مسكر» و عنينا به المسكر بالفعل و «الخمر ليس بمسكر و عنينا به كونه بحيث يقتضي الإسكار عند شرط مخصوص [أي المسكر بالقوة كان أحد المحمولين غير الآخر. وكذا وحدة المكان، فإنك إذا قلت زيد «جالس» أي على الأرض». «زيد ليس مجالس» أي «على السماء»، فالجلوس المحمول بالإيجاب في القضية الأولى غير المحمول بالسلب في الأخرى. و أما: وحدة «الشرط» و «الجزء و الكل»، فتندرج تحت وحدة «الموضوع» و وحدة «الإضافة » و «القوة و الفعل» تحت وحدة «المحمول». و وحدة «المكان» تحت وحدة «الزمان»» لاختلاف هذه الثلاث باختلافها. و إنما لم يعتبر اندراج وحدة الزمان والمكان تحت وحدة المحمول - كما اعتبر قوم من المتأخرين - لأن تعلق الزمان والمكان بالقضية إنّما يكون بالظرفية، إذ لا بد لنسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع من أن يقع في زمان و مكان إن كان الموضوع زمانياً و مكانياً. و الظرفية إنما تتحقق بعد حصول النسبة المتأخرة عن المحمول، فيكون كل منهما خارجاً عن المحمول. فلو اعتبر دخولها في المحمول، يلزم أن يكون للزمان زمان آخر و للمكان مكان آخر. و ذلك محال بخلاف البواقي فإنّها من عوارض المحمول. وإذا كانا خارجين عن المحمول، فلا تندرج وحدتهما تحت وحدة المحمول. و إنما اعتبر وحدة الزمان دون المكان، لأن وحدة المكان تندرج تحت وحدة الزمان دون العكس. هذا تما تحير في تحقيقه جميع المتأخرين. و أنا وحدة الجزء و الكل، فهي عائدة إلى وحدة الموضوع، لإنك إذا قلت «الزنجي أسود» أي «كل أجزائه» و إذا قلت الزنجي ليس بأسود أي كل أجزائه ليس كذلك، فالموضوع في القضية الأولى بعض الأجزاء و في الثانية كلها. وكنا وحدة الشرط، فإنك إذا قلت الأبيض مفرق للبصر» أي «ما دام أبيض» و «الأبيض ليس بمفرق للبصر» أي قبل كونه أبيض». فالموضوع في القضية الأولى الجسم الموصوف بالبياض. و في الثانية الجسم الخالي عنه، فأحد الموضوعين غير الآخر. نعم، وحدة الزمان مغايرة لوحدة الموضوع و وحدة المحمول، و لا بد منه. (منطق الملخص ص ۱۷۷-۱۷۹)
[The number of unities according to al-Fārābī] And al-Fārābī sufficed with three of them: unity of 'subject', 'predicate', and 'time', for the necessary general [condition] by which the aforementioned difference is realized at that time. Unity of subject, predicate, and time: It appears that the first to attribute to al-Fārābī sufficing with these three unities is Afḍal al-Dīn al-Khūnajī (Kashf al-Asrār, p. 122); and we did not find in the works of al-Fārābī anything indicating his sufficing with subject, predicate, and time. What we found in his works is sufficing with unity of subject, predicate, and condition: And they are only contradictory by [1] the meaning subject in one of them being exactly the other meaning subject in the other, [2] and the meaning predicate in one of them being exactly the predicate in the other, [3] and the condition that is conditioned in one of them being exactly—or that which its way is to be conditioned in one of them in wording or implied from time or place or part or whole or state or other than that—exactly that same condition also conditioned in the other. (al-Fārābī, al-Manṭiqiyyāt, vol. 1 p. 119) Or with the first two along with unity of negation and affirmation: For here are things that should be preserved in every pair of contradictories: [1] that the subject of the two contradictories be exactly one, [2] and likewise their predicate, [3] then that one negation be opposite to one affirmation, [4] then that the affirmation be one and the negation one. (al-Fārābī, al-Manṭiqiyyāt, vol. 2 p. 78). And the truth is that sufficing with unity of subject, predicate, and time is from the views of Imām al-Rāzī in Mantiq al-Mulakhkhaṣ, except that he did not make unity of place from unity of time but from unity of predicate: And in my view, unity of subject and predicate are necessary, otherwise negation and affirmation would not apply to one locus. As for unity of relation, it is included under unity of predicate, because if we say 'Zayd is father of Amr', 'Zayd is not father of Khalid', the predicate in the first is fatherhood of Amr, and in the second fatherhood of Khalid, and one of them is not the other, so the predicate was not one in the two loci. And likewise unity of potentiality and actuality, if we say 'Wine is intoxicating' meaning by it actually intoxicating, and 'Wine is not intoxicating' meaning by it its being such as to necessitate intoxication at a specific condition [i.e., potentially intoxicating], then one of the two predicates is not the other. And likewise unity of place, for if you say 'Zayd is sitting' i.e., on the ground, 'Zayd is not sitting' i.e., in the sky, then the sitting predicated affirmatively in the first proposition is not the predicate in the other [negatively]. And as for: unity of 'condition' and 'part and whole', they fall under unity of 'subject'; and unity of 'relation' and 'potentiality and actuality' fall under unity of 'predicate'; and unity of 'place' falls under unity of 'time', because these three differ with their difference. And he did not consider subsuming unity of time and place under unity of predicate—as some later scholars considered—because the connection of time and place to the proposition is only by adverbality, since the relation of predicate to subject must occur in a time and place if the subject is temporal and spatial. And adverbality is only realized after the occurrence of the relation which is posterior to the predicate, so each of them is external to the predicate. If one considered them included in the predicate, it would necessitate that time has another time and place has another place. And that is impossible, unlike the others, for they are accidents of the predicate. And when they are external to the predicate, their unity is not subsumed under unity of predicate. And he considered unity of time without place, because unity of place is subsumed under unity of time, not the reverse. This is [the full] perplexity in its verification by all later scholars. And as for unity of part and whole, it goes back to unity of subject, because if you say 'The Negro is black' i.e., all his parts, and if you say 'The Negro is not black' i.e., all his parts are not like that, then the subject in the first proposition is some of the parts and in the second is all of them. And as for unity of condition, for if you say 'The white [object] is vision-dispersing' i.e., as long as it is white, and 'The white [object] is not vision-dispersing' i.e., before it becomes white, then the subject in the first proposition is the body described with whiteness, and in the second is the body devoid of it, so one of the two subjects is not the other. Yes, unity of time is distinct from unity of subject and unity of predicate, and it is necessary. (Mantiq al-Mulakhkhaṣ pp. 177-179)
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical analysis of the conditions required for two propositions to be contradictory (tanāquḍ). The author, likely from the tradition of Qustas, examines the number of unities (waḥdāt) that must be satisfied for contradiction. The discussion begins with al-Fārābī's position: he is reported to have sufficed with three unities—subject, predicate, and time—but the author notes that this attribution comes from al-Khūnajī and that al-Fārābī's own texts show he actually required unity of subject, predicate, and condition (sharṭ), where 'condition' encompasses time, place, part, whole, state, etc. Alternatively, al-Fārābī considered unity of subject, predicate, and the unity of negation and affirmation (i.e., one negation against one affirmation). The author then states that the view of sufficing with subject, predicate, and time is actually from al-Rāzī's Mantiq al-Mulakhkhaṣ. Al-Rāzī further clarified that unity of place is subsumed under unity of predicate, not time. The author argues that unity of subject and predicate are essential; otherwise, the negation and affirmation would not apply to the same locus (mawrid). He gives examples: for relation (iḍāfa), the predicate differs when saying 'Zayd is father of Amr' vs. 'Zayd is not father of Khalid', so the predicate is not one. Similarly for potentiality vs. actuality (quwwa wa fiʿl) in the example of wine: 'Wine is intoxicating' (actually) vs. 'Wine is not intoxicating' (potentially) have different predicates. For place, 'Zayd is sitting on the ground' vs. 'Zayd is not sitting in the sky' have different predicates. Then the author explains the subsumption relations: unity of condition (sharṭ) and unity of part/whole (juzʾ wa kull) fall under unity of subject; unity of relation and potentiality/actuality fall under unity of predicate; unity of place falls under unity of time. The author provides reasoning: the connection of time and place to a proposition is via adverbality (ẓarfiyya), which is posterior to the predicate, so they are external—thus subsuming them under predicate would lead to infinite regress (time having another time). Therefore, they are not subsumed under predicate. Unity of time is considered because place is subsumed under time, not vice versa. The passage ends with an admission that later scholars are perplexed by this issue. The examples are drawn from Islamic legal contexts (wine) and common sense (Zayd's paternity, blackness of a Negro). The overall argument is that the essential conditions for contradiction reduce to three: unity of subject, predicate, and time, with other conditions subsumed under these.
