تناقض (tanāquḍ)
وحدة الموضوع (waḥdat al-mawḍūʿ)
وحدة المحمول (waḥdat al-maḥmūl)
وحدة الزمان (waḥdat al-zamān)
الظرفية (al-ẓarfiyya)
وحدة الإضافة (waḥdat al-iḍāfa)
وحدة القوة والفعل (waḥdat al-quwwa wa al-fiʿl)
وحدة الشرط (waḥdat al-sharṭ)
وحدة الجزء والكل (waḥdat al-juzʾ wa al-kull)

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following pairs would NOT be a valid contradiction because of a failure in unity of predicate?
A) 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' (both at the same time and place).
B) 'Wine is intoxicating actually' and 'Wine is not intoxicating potentially'.
C) 'The world is temporally originated' and 'The world is not temporally originated'.
D) 'The Negro is black in all his parts' and 'The Negro is not black in any of his parts'.

Key Takeaway
The essential conditions for contradiction reduce to three unities (subject, predicate, time), with other conditions (relation, potentiality, condition, part/whole, place) subsumed under them, ensuring precise logical opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the pair 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created'. Check whether the conditions of subject, predicate, and time are met. Then consider 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created by Allah' – why is this not a contradiction according to al-Rāzī's conditions?
?
Find an example from Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh) where two rulings seem contradictory but actually differ in predicate due to unity of relation (e.g., 'This act is permissible for the traveler' vs 'This act is not permissible for the resident'). Identify which unity is missing.

Why Time and Place are Not Part of Predicate Unity
Author argues against subsuming time and place under predicate unity using the regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Rāzī's argument that time and place are external to the predicate (adverbial) and that subsuming them under predicate unity leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Time and place are adverbial, not predicate accidents — subsuming them would create regress.

In the previous lesson, we saw that al-Rāzī subsumed many conditions under unity of subject, predicate, and time. But why not subsume place under predicate as well? The passage provides a powerful argument: time and place are adverbial (ẓarfiyya), meaning they attach to the proposition after the predicate relation is complete. If we tried to include them in the predicate, we would face an infinite regress—time would need another time, place another place. Understanding this argument sharpens your ability to distinguish essential predicate features from external circumstances in any logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of adverbality (ẓarfiyya)
The passage states that the relation of predicate to subject necessarily occurs in a time and place (if the subject is temporal and spatial). However, this time and place are not part of the predicate itself; they are adverbial modifiers that come after the predicate relation is established. Think of them as circumstantial conditions external to the core meaning of the predicate.
In Arabic grammar, ẓarf (adverb) denotes time or place and is not considered part of the ism (noun) of the predicate.
2
Analyze the infinite regress argument
If time or place were included in the predicate, then the proposition 'Zayd is sitting at time t' would have a predicate that includes 'at time t'. But then the time t itself would be part of the predicate, and the proposition would itself be situated in a time (the time of the utterance). This would require a second time for the first time, leading to an infinite regress. The passage says: 'it would necessitate that time has another time and place has another place' which is impossible.
This is a classic argument in Islamic logic to show that time and place are external to the predicate proper.
3
Distinguish from accidents of the predicate
Unlike time and place, other conditions like relation (iḍāfa) and potentiality (quwwa) are real accidents of the predicate—they modify the predicate itself. For example, 'father of Amr' is a predicate that includes a relation; changing the relation changes the predicate. But time and place do not modify the predicate; they only situate the whole proposition.
The passage says: 'the others are accidents of the predicate' while time and place are external.
4
Understand why place is subsumed under time
The passage argues that unity of place falls under unity of time, not the reverse, because place changes with time. A change in place typically implies a change in time (e.g., moving from ground to sky takes time). Therefore, if time is unified, place is automatically unified; if time differs, place may also differ, but the critical condition is time. This is why al-Rāzī only mentions unity of time as a separate condition.
This subsumption is not arbitrary; it reflects the dependency of place on time in temporal-spatial propositions.

كما اعتبر قوم من المتأخرين - لأن تعلق الزمان والمكان بالقضية إنّما يكون بالظرفية، إذ لا بد لنسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع من أن يقع في زمان و مكان إن كان الموضوع زمانياً و مكانياً. و الظرفية إنما تتحقق بعد حصول النسبة المتأخرة عن المحمول، فيكون كل منهما خارجاً عن المحمول. فلو اعتبر دخولها في المحمول، يلزم أن يكون للزمان زمان آخر و للمكان مكان آخر. و ذلك محال بخلاف البواقي فإنّها من عوارض المحمول. وإذا كانا خارجين عن المحمول، فلا تندرج وحدتهما تحت وحدة المحمول. و إنما اعتبر وحدة الزمان دون المكان، لأن وحدة المكان تندرج تحت وحدة الزمان دون العكس. هذا تما تحير في تحقيقه جميع المتأخرين. و أنا وحدة الجزء و الكل، فهي عائدة إلى وحدة الموضوع، لإنك إذا قلت «الزنجي أسود» أي «كل أجزائه» و إذا قلت الزنجي ليس بأسود أي كل أجزائه ليس كذلك، فالموضوع في القضية الأولى بعض الأجزاء و في الثانية كلها. وكنا وحدة الشرط، فإنك إذا قلت الأبيض مفرق للبصر» أي «ما دام أبيض» و «الأبيض ليس بمفرق للبصر» أي قبل كونه أبيض». فالموضوع في القضية الأولى الجسم الموصوف بالبياض. و في الثانية الجسم الخالي عنه، فأحد الموضوعين غير الآخر. نعم، وحدة الزمان مغايرة لوحدة الموضوع و وحدة المحمول، و لا بد منه. (منطق الملخص ص ۱۷۷-۱۷۹)
[The number of unities according to al-Fārābī] And al-Fārābī sufficed with three of them: unity of 'subject', 'predicate', and 'time', for the necessary general [condition] by which the aforementioned difference is realized at that time. Unity of subject, predicate, and time: It appears that the first to attribute to al-Fārābī sufficing with these three unities is Afḍal al-Dīn al-Khūnajī (Kashf al-Asrār, p. 122); and we did not find in the works of al-Fārābī anything indicating his sufficing with subject, predicate, and time. What we found in his works is sufficing with unity of subject, predicate, and condition: And they are only contradictory by [1] the meaning subject in one of them being exactly the other meaning subject in the other, [2] and the meaning predicate in one of them being exactly the predicate in the other, [3] and the condition that is conditioned in one of them being exactly—or that which its way is to be conditioned in one of them in wording or implied from time or place or part or whole or state or other than that—exactly that same condition also conditioned in the other. (al-Fārābī, al-Manṭiqiyyāt, vol. 1 p. 119) Or with the first two along with unity of negation and affirmation: For here are things that should be preserved in every pair of contradictories: [1] that the subject of the two contradictories be exactly one, [2] and likewise their predicate, [3] then that one negation be opposite to one affirmation, [4] then that the affirmation be one and the negation one. (al-Fārābī, al-Manṭiqiyyāt, vol. 2 p. 78). And the truth is that sufficing with unity of subject, predicate, and time is from the views of Imām al-Rāzī in Mantiq al-Mulakhkhaṣ, except that he did not make unity of place from unity of time but from unity of predicate: And in my view, unity of subject and predicate are necessary, otherwise negation and affirmation would not apply to one locus. As for unity of relation, it is included under unity of predicate, because if we say 'Zayd is father of Amr', 'Zayd is not father of Khalid', the predicate in the first is fatherhood of Amr, and in the second fatherhood of Khalid, and one of them is not the other, so the predicate was not one in the two loci. And likewise unity of potentiality and actuality, if we say 'Wine is intoxicating' meaning by it actually intoxicating, and 'Wine is not intoxicating' meaning by it its being such as to necessitate intoxication at a specific condition [i.e., potentially intoxicating], then one of the two predicates is not the other. And likewise unity of place, for if you say 'Zayd is sitting' i.e., on the ground, 'Zayd is not sitting' i.e., in the sky, then the sitting predicated affirmatively in the first proposition is not the predicate in the other [negatively]. And as for: unity of 'condition' and 'part and whole', they fall under unity of 'subject'; and unity of 'relation' and 'potentiality and actuality' fall under unity of 'predicate'; and unity of 'place' falls under unity of 'time', because these three differ with their difference. And he did not consider subsuming unity of time and place under unity of predicate—as some later scholars considered—because the connection of time and place to the proposition is only by adverbality, since the relation of predicate to subject must occur in a time and place if the subject is temporal and spatial. And adverbality is only realized after the occurrence of the relation which is posterior to the predicate, so each of them is external to the predicate. If one considered them included in the predicate, it would necessitate that time has another time and place has another place. And that is impossible, unlike the others, for they are accidents of the predicate. And when they are external to the predicate, their unity is not subsumed under unity of predicate. And he considered unity of time without place, because unity of place is subsumed under unity of time, not the reverse. This is [the full] perplexity in its verification by all later scholars. And as for unity of part and whole, it goes back to unity of subject, because if you say 'The Negro is black' i.e., all his parts, and if you say 'The Negro is not black' i.e., all his parts are not like that, then the subject in the first proposition is some of the parts and in the second is all of them. And as for unity of condition, for if you say 'The white [object] is vision-dispersing' i.e., as long as it is white, and 'The white [object] is not vision-dispersing' i.e., before it becomes white, then the subject in the first proposition is the body described with whiteness, and in the second is the body devoid of it, so one of the two subjects is not the other. Yes, unity of time is distinct from unity of subject and unity of predicate, and it is necessary. (Mantiq al-Mulakhkhaṣ pp. 177-179)
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical analysis of the conditions required for two propositions to be contradictory (tanāquḍ). The author, likely from the tradition of Qustas, examines the number of unities (waḥdāt) that must be satisfied for contradiction. The discussion begins with al-Fārābī's position: he is reported to have sufficed with three unities—subject, predicate, and time—but the author notes that this attribution comes from al-Khūnajī and that al-Fārābī's own texts show he actually required unity of subject, predicate, and condition (sharṭ), where 'condition' encompasses time, place, part, whole, state, etc. Alternatively, al-Fārābī considered unity of subject, predicate, and the unity of negation and affirmation (i.e., one negation against one affirmation). The author then states that the view of sufficing with subject, predicate, and time is actually from al-Rāzī's Mantiq al-Mulakhkhaṣ. Al-Rāzī further clarified that unity of place is subsumed under unity of predicate, not time. The author argues that unity of subject and predicate are essential; otherwise, the negation and affirmation would not apply to the same locus (mawrid). He gives examples: for relation (iḍāfa), the predicate differs when saying 'Zayd is father of Amr' vs. 'Zayd is not father of Khalid', so the predicate is not one. Similarly for potentiality vs. actuality (quwwa wa fiʿl) in the example of wine: 'Wine is intoxicating' (actually) vs. 'Wine is not intoxicating' (potentially) have different predicates. For place, 'Zayd is sitting on the ground' vs. 'Zayd is not sitting in the sky' have different predicates. Then the author explains the subsumption relations: unity of condition (sharṭ) and unity of part/whole (juzʾ wa kull) fall under unity of subject; unity of relation and potentiality/actuality fall under unity of predicate; unity of place falls under unity of time. The author provides reasoning: the connection of time and place to a proposition is via adverbality (ẓarfiyya), which is posterior to the predicate, so they are external—thus subsuming them under predicate would lead to infinite regress (time having another time). Therefore, they are not subsumed under predicate. Unity of time is considered because place is subsumed under time, not vice versa. The passage ends with an admission that later scholars are perplexed by this issue. The examples are drawn from Islamic legal contexts (wine) and common sense (Zayd's paternity, blackness of a Negro). The overall argument is that the essential conditions for contradiction reduce to three: unity of subject, predicate, and time, with other conditions subsumed under these.

Key Terms
ظرفية (ẓarfiyya)
عوارض المحمول (ʿawāriḍ al-maḥmūl)
دور أو تسلسل (infinite regress)
وحدة الزمان (waḥdat al-zamān)
وحدة المكان (waḥdat al-makān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because they are adverbial and external; including them would require a second time/place (infinite regress).
C) Because al-Fārābī said so.
D) Because they are already covered under unity of subject.

Key Takeaway
Time and place are external adverbials to the proposition, not part of the predicate; subsuming them leads to infinite regress, so they must be treated as separate conditions for contradiction.

Conditions for Contradiction: Unity of Judgment, Modality, and Quantity
Author explains the conditions for contradiction, focusing on unity of judgment ratio and differences in modality and quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions required for two propositions to be true contradictories in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires more than opposite truth values – it demands unity of the judgmental relation.

In this passage, the author explains the precise conditions under which two propositions are genuinely contradictory. Far from mere opposition in truth value, true contradiction requires identity of the judgmental relation, and then considers differences in modality and quantity. This is crucial for understanding how logical arguments are tested for validity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core condition: Unity of the judgmental relation
The author states that negation must apply to the exact same relation that was affirmed. This means the subject, predicate, time, place, and condition must be identical. Without this, two propositions may differ in truth value but not be true contradictories.
This is the foundational condition; all others are subsidiary.
2
Identify the role of modality
The author explains that in non-perpetual matter (e.g., 'The moon is eclipsed'), the four perpetual propositions are false while non-perpetual ones are true. This shows that modality must be the same for contradiction; otherwise, both could be true or false.
Modality refers to the mode of predication (necessary, perpetual, etc.).
3
Analyze the quantity condition
In categorical propositions, difference in quantity can prevent contradiction: two particular propositions can both be true when the predicate is more general, while two universal propositions can both be false. Thus, quantity must be the same for contradiction.
Quantity: universal vs. particular.
4
Address the objection about subject unity
A hypothetical objector claims that two particulars do not contradict due to different subjects. The author responds that specifying the subject is an extra restriction; the discussion concerns absolute particularity. This reinforces that subject unity is already part of the judgmental relation condition.
Absolute particularity means the subject is considered as is, without additional specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قال يمكن رد الكل إلى وحدة النسبة الحكمية لاختلافها عند اختلافها، و يعني ما أن يكون السلب وارداً على النسبة التي ما حكم في الموجبة بعينها. [اختلاف الجهة و الكمية] و يعتبر أيضاً: اختلاف الجهة، لكذب الدوائم الأربع و صدق غير الدوائم - إيجاباً و سلباً – في مادة اللادوام. و في المحصورات اختلاف الكم أيضاً، يصدق الجزئيتين وكذب الكليتين فيما يكون المحكوم عليه أعم. فإن قلت: عدم التناقض بين الجزئيتين إنما كان لعدم اتحاد الموضوع. فلو جعل متحداً، تناقضتا. قلت: تعيين الموضوع قيد زائد على الجزئية والكلام في مطلقها.
And he said: It is possible to reduce everything to the unity of the judgmental relation because it differs when they differ. And what is meant is that negation applies to the relation that was judged in the affirmative proposition itself. [Difference of modality and quantity] And also considered: difference of modality, because of the falsity of the four perpetuals and the truth of the non-perpetuals – positively and negatively – in non-perpetual matter. And in categorical propositions, difference of quantity also: the two particular propositions are true and the two universal propositions are false when the predicate is more general. If you say: The lack of contradiction between the two particular propositions is only because of lack of unity of subject. If the subject is made identical, they would contradict. I say: Specification of the subject is an additional restriction beyond particularity, and the discussion is about absolute particularity.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for contradiction in propositions. The author begins by stating a view that all conditions can be reduced to the unity of the judgmental relation (النسبة الحكمية). The idea is that for two propositions to be true contradictories, the negation must apply to exactly the same relation that was affirmed in the original positive proposition. Then the author adds that one must also consider differences in modality (جهة) and quantity (كم). For modality, the example given is that the four perpetual propositions (الدوائم الأربع) are false, while non-perpetual ones are true, in non-perpetual matter such as the example of the moon eclipse. For quantity, in categorical propositions, two particular propositions can both be true while two universal propositions are false when the predicate is more general (الأعم). The author then addresses a potential objection: the reason two particular propositions don't contradict is that they have different subjects. If the subject were unified, they would contradict. The response is that specifying the subject is an extra restriction beyond mere particularity, and the discussion is about absolute particularity. This shows the nuance in the conditions for contradiction.

الدوائم الأربع

Key Takeaway
True logical contradiction requires identity of the judgmental relation, not merely opposite truth values, and is further constrained by modality and quantity.

Classification of Propositions: Categorical and Conditional
Author defines the two main types of propositions: categorical and conditional, then divides categorical into simple and compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as categorical or conditional, and identify whether a categorical proposition is simple or compound.

Core Idea
Every proposition is either categorical or conditional; categoricals are simple or compound.

In logic, the first step in analyzing an argument is to understand the types of propositions it contains. This lesson introduces the fundamental types: categorical (ḥamliyya) and conditional (sharṭiyya). These distinctions are essential for later work with syllogisms and other reasoning forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify proposition type
Look at the proposition: does it have a simple subject-predicate structure (categorical) or does it contain a condition like 'if...then...' (conditional)?
Categoricals affirm or deny something; conditionals express dependence.
2
If categorical, check simplicity
If categorical, count the subject and predicate. A simple categorical has one subject and one predicate. A compound categorical has multiple subjects or predicates.
Example: 'Zayd and 'Amr are students' is compound.
3
If conditional, note condition
If conditional, identify the antecedent (if-clause) and consequent (then-clause).
Conditionals are often used in legal rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية إما حملية أو شرطية. و الحملية إما بسيطة أو مركبة.
The proposition is either categorical or conditional. And the categorical is either simple or compound.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This is a foundational classification in logic. The author defines the proposition (qadiyya) as either categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya). A categorical proposition is one where something is affirmed or denied of a subject. A conditional proposition is one that expresses a condition. Then the categorical is further divided into simple (basita) and compound (murakkaba). Simple categoricals have one subject and predicate, while compound categoricals may have multiple parts. This classification is crucial for understanding different types of logical arguments.

Key Terms
حملية
شرطية
بسيطة
مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a conditional proposition?
A) 'Zayd is standing.'
B) 'If the sun rises, it is day.'
C) 'Every man is mortal.'
D) 'Some animals are not horses.'

Key Takeaway
The two primary types of propositions are categorical (subject-predicate) and conditional (if-then); categoricals are further divided into simple and compound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Contradictories of Simple Modal Propositions
Author lists the contradictory pairs for four types of simple modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any given simple modal proposition from the four types listed.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a modal proposition is another modal proposition that matches the modality in a specific way.

This passage lays out the exact contradictory pairs for four types of simple modal propositions. Understanding these rules is essential for immediate inference in logic, especially when testing arguments or constructing valid proofs in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal type
Determine which of the four simple modal types the proposition belongs to: absolute necessary, perpetual, general conditional, or general customary.
The proposition must be simple (not compound).
2
Match to the contradictory pair
Use the author's pairing: necessary ↔ general possible, perpetual ↔ absolute general, conditional ↔ temporal possible, customary ↔ temporal absolute.
Each pair is symmetric (the contradictory of the second is the first).
3
Apply the quality and quantity change
The contradictory must also oppose in quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). For example, the contradictory of a universal affirmative necessary is a particular negative possible.
The passage implies this but focuses on modality; standard square of opposition applies.
4
Verify the contradictory is simple
Check that the resulting proposition is still a simple modal proposition, not a compound one.
The author's first sentence emphasises this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما البسيطة فنقيضها أيضاً بسيطة. فنقيض الضرورية المطلقة «الممكنة العامة» وبالعكس؛ لأن الإمكان هو سلب الضرورة المخالفة. و نقيض الدائمة «المطلقة العامة» و بالعكس؛ لأن الثبوت دائماً يناقضه السلب بالفعل و بالعكس. و نقيض المشروطة العامة «الحينية الممكنة» المحكوم فيها بالثبوت أو السلب بالإمكان في بعض أوقات وصف الموضوع. و نقيض العرفية العامة «الحينية المطلقة» المحكوم فيها بالثبوت أو السلب بالفعل في بعض أوقات وصف الموضوع.
As for the simple [propositions], their contradictory is also simple. So the contradictory of the absolute necessary is the general possible, and vice versa, because possibility is the negation of the necessary of the opposite. And the contradictory of the perpetual is the absolute general, and vice versa, because perpetual affirmation is contradicted by actual negation and vice versa. And the contradictory of the general conditional is the temporal possible, wherein judgment is of affirmation or negation by possibility in some times of the description of the subject. And the contradictory of the general customary is the temporal absolute, wherein judgment is of affirmation or negation actually in some times of the description of the subject.

Commentary
This passage provides the rules for finding the contradictories of simple modal propositions. The author first states that the contradictory of a simple modal proposition is also simple (not compound). Then he lists four cases: (1) The contradictory of the absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة) is the general possible (الممكنة العامة). The reason is that possibility is the negation of the necessary of the opposite. (2) The contradictory of the perpetual (الدائمة) is the absolute general (المطلقة العامة). Perpetual affirmation is contradicted by actual negation. (3) The contradictory of the general conditional (المشروطة العامة) is the temporal possible (الحينية الممكنة). (4) The contradictory of the general customary (العرفية العامة) is the temporal absolute (الحينية المطلقة). This shows that the contradictory must match the modality in a specific way.

Contradictories of Compound Universal Modal Propositions
Author explains contradictions for compound universal modal propositions and begins the list for compound particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any compound universal modal proposition using the disjunctive concept (al-mafhūm al-muraddad).

Core Idea
A compound universal has no single contradictory but a disjunctive pair.

Compound modal propositions have two parts. When they are universal, their contradictory is not a simple proposition but a disjunctive concept formed from the contradictories of each part. This passage explains why and then lists seven types of compound universals with their disjunctive contradictories. Mastering this rule is essential for correctly negating complex logical statements in Islamic dialectics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the compound universal
Every compound modal proposition has two components. For example, the special conditional (المشروطة الخاصة) has a temporal condition and a simple proposition. Locate the two parts and their logical relationship (conjunction or disjunction).
The passage treats compound universals as those where the whole is universal in scope.
2
Find the contradictory of each part separately
Take each part and determine its individual contradictory. For instance, if a part is 'always true', its contradictory is 'not always true' or 'sometimes false'.
These contradictories are simple propositions.
3
Form the disjunctive concept
The contradictory of the compound universal is neither the contradictory of the first part nor the second alone, nor their conjunction. Instead, it is a disjunction of two possibilities: the opposite of one part or the agreeing (i.e., the contradictory of the other part). The author calls this 'المفهوم المردّد' (disjunctive concept).
This disjunction captures all cases where the original compound universal is false.
4
Apply the rule to each listed type
For each of the seven types (special conditional, special customary, temporal, extended, non-perpetual existential, non-necessary existential, special possible), the author gives the specific disjunctive contradictory. For example, for the special conditional: 'الحينية الممكنة المخالفة أو الدائمة الموافقة' (the temporal possible opposite or the perpetual agreeing). Note that the two disjuncts are always of the form 'opposite in one dimension' and 'agreeing in the other'.
The pattern: one disjunct uses 'مخالفة' (opposite) and the other uses 'موافقة' (agreeing), each with a different modality.
5
Verify the disjunction works as a logical contradictory
Check that the original compound universal and the disjunctive contradictory are exact opposites: they cannot be both true and cannot be both false. If the compound is true, the disjunction is false; if the compound is false (because one part is false), one of the disjuncts will be true.
This ensures the disjunction is the true contradictory, not just a contrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the compound [modal proposition], it is either universal or particular. And the universal's contradictory is the concept disjunctively between the contradictories of its two parts, because each of the two contradictories and their conjunction may be false along with the compound, since the compound becomes false by the falsity of one of its two parts. So neither of the two contradictories nor their conjunction is a contradictory for it. So the contradictory of the special conditional is the temporal possible opposite or the perpetual agreeing. And the contradictory of the special customary is the temporal absolute opposite or the perpetual agreeing. And the contradictory of the temporal is the temporal possible opposite or the perpetual agreeing. And the contradictory of the extended is the perpetual possible opposite or the perpetual agreeing. And the contradictory of the non-perpetual existential is the perpetual opposite or agreeing. And the contradictory of the non-necessary existential is the perpetual opposite or the necessary agreeing. And the contradictory of the special possible is the necessary opposite or agreeing. Commentary
This passage deals with compound modal propositions, which have two parts. The author explains that a compound universal proposition does not have a single contradictory; rather, its contradictory is a disjunctive concept formed from the contradictories of its two parts. The reason is that the compound universal is false if either part is false, so no simple contradictory can cover all cases. Then the author lists the contradictories for seven types of compound universal propositions: special conditional, special customary, temporal, extended, non-perpetual existential, non-necessary existential, and special possible. Each contradictory is expressed as a disjunction of two possibilities: either the opposite of one part or the agreeing of the other. The passage then starts the section on compound particular propositions but only gives the first item (necessary) and cuts off. This is likely due to the page break.

Limitation of al-Khūnajī's Method
Specifies that al-Khūnajī's method applies only to non-perpetual existential propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which type of modal proposition al-Khūnajī's reduction method applies to and explain the logical reason for its limitation.

Core Idea
Al-Khūnajī's method works only for non-perpetual existentials; otherwise the original may be false with both contradictories.

Al-Khūnajī's method is a powerful tool for reducing compound modal propositions to simple affirmatives, but it is not universally applicable. This lesson focuses on the precise scope of the method: it is valid only for non-perpetual existential propositions. Understanding this limitation prevents misapplication in logical analysis, especially in theological and cosmological arguments where modal propositions abound.

Guided Path
1
Recall the purpose of al-Khūnajī's method
Al-Khūnajī's method reduces a compound modal proposition (e.g., 'some A is B as long as it is A, not always') to a simple affirmative ('every A is B') by analyzing its contradictories. Review the basic structure of the method before examining its limitation.
2
Identify the restricted scope
The passage explicitly states: 'إنما يصح في الوجودية اللادائمة' – it is valid only in non-perpetual existential propositions. Note that 'non-perpetual existential' means a proposition asserting existence that is true at some times but not always.
Perpetual existentials (دائمة) are excluded, as are conditional propositions like العرفية الخاصة.
3
Examine the example given
The author provides an example from 'special conditional' (العرفية الخاصة): 'Some body is moving as long as it is a body, not always' and its supposed contradictories: 'Nothing of the body is moving when it is a body' and 'Every body is moving, moving always'. This illustrates that for non-perpetual existential propositions, the method works, but for others it can fail.
The example shows that the original proposition can be false while both contradictories are also false, which would make the reduction invalid.
4
Understand the reason for failure in other types
The passage says: 'لجواز كذب الأصل مع كل واحدة منها' – because the original can be false with each of them. If the original is false, its contradictory should be true, but in other proposition types, both the original and its apparent contradictories can be false, violating the logical principle that the method relies on.
This aligns with the concept that reduction methods require certain modal properties; here, the property is non-perpetuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختصاص طريقة الخونجي إلى الوجودية اللادائمة. و هو إنّما يصح في الوجودية اللادائمة. و أما في غيرها فلا لجواز كذب الأصل مع كل واحدة منها، مثلاً في العرفية الخاصة كقولنا: بعض الجسم متحرك ما دام جسماً لا دائماً و لا شيء من الجسم بمتحرك حين هو جسم، أو كل جسم متحرك، متحرك دائماً.
Specialization of al-Khūnajī's method to non-perpetual existential [propositions]. And it is valid only in non-perpetual existential [propositions]. As for others, no, because the original can be false with each of them, for example in special conditional [propositions] like our saying: Some body is moving as long as it is a body, not always, and nothing of the body is moving when it is a body, or every body is moving, moving always.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage restricts the application of al-Khūnajī's method (ṭarīqat al-Khūnajī) to a specific type of modal proposition: the non-perpetual existential (al-wujūdiyya al-lādāʾima). The method, which reduces compound modals to simple affirmatives, is not valid for other types because in those cases the original proposition can be false while both of its supposed contradictories are also false. The author gives an example from the 'special conditional' (al-ʿurfiyya al-khāṣṣa): 'Some body is moving as long as it is a body, not always' and its apparent contradictories 'Nothing of the body is moving when it is a body' and 'Every body is moving, moving always'. This illustrates that the method's validity is limited.

Key Terms
طريقة الخونجي (ṭarīqat al-Khūnajī): The method attributed to al-Khūnajī for reducing compound modal propositions to simple affirmative propositions, applied here only to non-perpetual existential propositions. [Note: In later texts, this method was attempted to be generalized, but the author here insists on its limitation.]
الوجودية اللادائمة (al-wujūdiyya al-lādāʾima): A proposition asserting existence that is not perpetual, i.e., true at some times but not always. [Note: This term is specific to modal logic discussions; it is stable in this context.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Non-existential propositions only.

Key Takeaway
Al-Khūnajī's method for reducing compound modal propositions is valid only for non-perpetual existential propositions; for others, the original may be false while both its contradictories are false, making the reduction invalid.

Reducing Compound Modals to Simple Affirmatives
Generalizes al-Khūnajī's method to all compound propositions and reduces them to simple affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any compound modal proposition to a simple affirmative and identify its contradictory.

Core Idea
Every compound modal reduces to a simple affirmative; its contradictory follows directly.

This passage extends al-Khūnajī's method to all compound propositions, universal or particular. It provides a systematic way to transform any complex modal statement into a simple affirmative, making it easier to handle contradictions. Understanding this reduction is essential for working with modal logic in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the compound is affirmative or negative. Look for the modal qualifier like 'not always' or 'not necessarily'.
Affirmative compounds have a positive modal qualifier; negative compounds have a negative one.
2
Apply the reduction rule for affirmatives
If affirmative: qualify the subject by the contradictory of the predicate, then assert the predicate as actual. For example, 'Every J is B not always' becomes 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually'.
3
Apply the reduction rule for negatives
If negative: qualify the subject by the predicate itself, then assert the contradictory of the predicate as actual. For example, 'No J is B actually not always' becomes 'Every J that is B actually is not B actually'.
4
Form the contradictory
Once the compound is reduced to a simple affirmative, its contradictory is a simple negative proposition with the opposite modality and quantity. For a universal affirmative, the contradictory is a particular negative; for a particular affirmative, it is a universal negative.
The contradictory always has opposite truth value to the compound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعميم طريقة الخونجي إلى المركبات الكلية. و إذا عرفت ذلك، فنقول: يمكننا أن تحصل قضية واحدة تساوي نقيض المركبة، كلية كانت المركبة أو جزئية. و ذلك لأن كل مركبة - كلية كانت أو جزئية، موجبة أو سالبة – ترجع إلى قضية واحدة موجبة: مثلا جمثها جمة الجزء الأول من المركبة يجعل الموضوع مقيداً بنقيض المحمول و المحمول محمول، إذا كانت المركبة موجبة، و يجعل الموضوع مقيداً بالمحمول، و يجعل المحمول نقيض المحمول، إذا كانت سالبة. قولنا: كل ج ب لا دائماً يرجع إلى قولنا: كلّ ج لا ب بالفعل، هو ب بالفعل و قولنا: لا شيء من ج ب بالفعل لا دائماً يرجع إلى قولنا: كل ج ب بالفعل، هو لا ب بالفعل. و إذا رجعت المركبة إلى موجبة واحدة بسيطة، فنقيض تلك الموجبة يساوي نقيض المركبة. فنقيض المركبة - سواء كانت المركبة موجبة أو سالبة - يكون أبدأ سالبة مناقضة للجزء الأول في الجهة و الكم.
Generalization of al-Khūnajī's method to universal compounds. And if you know that, we say: we can obtain one proposition that equals the contradictory of the compound, whether the compound is universal or particular. And that is because every compound – universal or particular, affirmative or negative – reduces to one affirmative proposition: for example, its sum is the sum of the first part of the compound: the subject is made qualified by the contradictory of the predicate and the predicate is the predicate, if the compound is affirmative; and the subject is made qualified by the predicate and the predicate is made the contradictory of the predicate, if it is negative. Our statement: 'Every J is B not always' reduces to our statement: 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually'. And our statement: 'Nothing of J is B actually not always' reduces to our statement: 'Every J that is B actually is not B actually'. And if the compound reduces to one simple affirmative, then the contradictory of that affirmative equals the contradictory of the compound. So the contradictory of the compound – whether the compound is affirmative or negative – is always a negative opposite to the first part in modality and quantity.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage generalizes al-Khūnajī's method to all compound propositions, including universals and particulars. The core idea: any compound modal proposition can be reduced to a single simple affirmative proposition. The rule for reduction: for an affirmative compound, take the subject and qualify it by the negation of the predicate; for a negative compound, qualify the subject by the predicate itself and then make the predicate its own negation. Examples are given: 'Every J is B not always' reduces to 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually', and 'No J is B actually not always' reduces to 'Every J that is B actually is not B actually'. Once reduced, the contradictory of the compound is simply the contradictory of this simple affirmative, which is a negative proposition with the same subject and predicate but opposite quantity and modality. This method streamlines the handling of contradictions in compound modals.

Key Terms
مركبة
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Every J that is not B actually is B actually.
C) Every J that is not B actually is not B actually.
D) Some J that is not B actually is B actually.

Key Takeaway
Any compound modal proposition reduces to a simple affirmative; its contradictory is then a simple negative with opposite modality and quantity.

Critique of Generalizing Khūnajī's Method to Universal Compounds
Criticizes the generalization of al-Khūnajī's method to universal compound propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the equivalence claimed for universal compound propositions fails and distinguish it from the valid case for particulars.

Core Idea
Universal conditional reversal fails; particular conjunctive reversal succeeds.

Al-Khūnajī's method attempted to reduce certain compound propositions to simple categoricals, but the author shows this only works for particulars, not universals. This lesson dissects the logical asymmetry between universal and particular compound propositions, using conditional and conjunctive equivalences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Recognize that the author is criticizing the generalization of Khūnajī's method to universal compounds. The method claims a reduction that the author argues is invalid.
The passage begins by stating the generalization is incorrect.
2
Unpack the universal reduction
Examine the two specific reductions: 'Every J is B not always' does not reduce to 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually', and similarly for the negative. The author uses conditionals to show non-equivalence.
Focus on the conditional forms: 'If this is J then it is B and D' and 'If this is J and D then it is B'.
3
Analyze the entailment
Notice that the first conditional ('If J then B and D') entails the second ('If J and D then B') but not conversely. This asymmetry means the categoricals are not equivalent, so the reduction fails.
The passage explicitly states: 'the first entails the second but not conversely'.
4
Compare with particulars
Contrast with the particular case: 'Some J is B and D' is equivalent to 'Some J that is D is B' because the conjunctive forms 'This is J and B and D' and 'This is J and D and B' are clearly equivalent.
The reversal works for conjunctions due to commutativity of 'and'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعميم المصنف لطريقة الخونجي إلى المركبات الكلية خطاً لأن: قوله: كل ج ب لا دائماً لا يرجع في الحقيقة إلى قوله: كل ج لا ب بالفعل، هو ب بالفعل. و قوله: لا شيء من ج ب بالفعل لا دائماً لا يرجع في الحقيقة إلى قوله: كل ج ب بالفعل، هو لا ب بالفعل. لأنّ كل ج هو ب و د لا يلازم كل ج د هو ب لأن هاتين الحمليتين معادلتان لهاتين الشرطيتين: إن كان هذا ج فهو ب و د و إن كان هذا ج و د فهو ب؛ و هاتان الشرطيتان لا تتلازمان لأن الأولى تستلزم الثانية من غير عكس. هذا بخلاف الجزئيات فإنّ بعض ج هو ب و د يلازم بعض ج د هو ب لأن هاتين الحمليتين معادلتان لهاتين العطفيتين: هذا ج و ب و د و هنا ج و د و ب؛ و هاتان العطفيتان تتلازمان بوضوح.
The author's generalization of al-Khūnajī's method to universal compounds is incorrect because: his statement 'Every J is B not always' does not actually reduce to his statement 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually'. And his statement 'Nothing of J is B actually not always' does not actually reduce to his statement 'Every J that is B actually is not B actually'. Because 'Every J is B and D' does not imply 'Every J that is D is B' because these two categoricals are equivalent to these two conditionals: 'If this is J then it is B and D' and 'If this is J and D then it is B'; and these two conditionals are not equivalent because the first entails the second but not conversely. This is unlike particulars, because 'Some J is B and D' does imply 'Some J that is D is B' because these two categoricals are equivalent to these two conjunctive statements: 'This is J and B and D' and 'This is J and D and B'; and these two conjunctives are clearly equivalent.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (taʿmīm al-muṣannif li-ṭarīqat al-Khūnajī ilā al-murakkabāt al-kulliyya khaṭaʾ) – the author argues that the generalization of al-Khūnajī's method to universal compounds is wrong. The reason: the reduction claimed for universals does not hold logically. For example, 'Every J is B not always' does not reduce to 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually'. This is because the author uses a more detailed analysis: the universal categorical 'Every J is B and D' implies the conditional 'If this is J then it is B and D', which entails 'If this is J and D then it is B', but not vice versa. So the equivalence fails. In contrast, for particular propositions, the equivalence holds because the conjunctive form is reversible. The critique shows a deeper understanding of the logical relationships between categorical and conditional propositions, highlighting that the generalization is invalid. In Islamic logic, this type of critique is common in commentaries and super-commentaries (ḥāshiya) to refine earlier positions.

Key Terms
لا يلازم
شرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the critique, why is the reduction invalid for universal compounds?
A) Because 'Every J is B and D' implies 'Every J that is D is B' but not conversely.
B) Because the subject and predicate are reversed.
C) Because the method only works for particular propositions.
D) Because the reduction changes the modality.

Key Takeaway
Universal compound reductions fail due to irreversible conditional entailment, while particular compound reductions succeed due to reversible conjunctive equivalence.

Reducing Compound Modal Propositions
Method for reducing compound modal propositions to simple affirmatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any compound modal proposition to a simple affirmative and identify its contradictory.

Core Idea
Compound modals reduce to simple affirmatives by restricting the subject—making contradiction straightforward.

Compound modal propositions, like 'Every J is B not always', seem complex but can be transformed into a single simple affirmative. This reduction clarifies their logical structure and makes finding contradictions easy. In this lesson, we will learn the step-by-step method from the text and see how it applies to Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound proposition
Take a compound modal proposition like 'Every J is B not always'. Note its quantity (universal/particular), quality (affirmative/negative), and the modal qualifier ('not always').
The compound always contains a modal or temporal qualifier that makes it compound.
2
Determine the reduction rule based on quality
If the compound is affirmative, the subject becomes restricted by the negation of the predicate (e.g., 'J that is not B'). If negative, the subject is restricted by the predicate itself, and the predicate is negated.
For affirmative compounds: subject restricted by naqid al-mahmul. For negative: subject restricted by mahmul, mahmul becomes naqid.
3
Apply the reduction to the example
'Every J is B not always' is affirmative, so it becomes 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually'. 'No J is B actually not always' is negative, so it becomes 'Every J that is B actually is not B actually'.
The result is always a simple affirmative (universal or particular depending on original quantity).
4
Find the contradictory of the compound
First reduce to simple affirmative, then take its contradictory (which is a negative proposition with the same modality and quantity opposite to the original's first part). That contradictory is the contradictory of the original compound.
This shortcut avoids handling the compound directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ارجاع الموجهات المركبة إلى الموجبات البسيطة] و ذلك لأن كل مركبة - كلية كانت أو جزئية، موجبة أو سالبة – ترجع إلى قضية واحدة موجبة: مثلا . يجعل الجزء الأول من المركبة موضوعاً مقيداً بنقيض المحمول و المحمول محمول، إذا كانت المركبة موجبة. و يجعل الموضوع مقيداً بالمحمول، و يجعل المحمول نقيض المحمول، إذا كانت سالبة. ١. قولنا: «كل ج ب لا دائماً» يرجع إلى قولنا: «كلّ ج لا ب بالفعل، هو ب بالفعل» و قولنا: «لا شيء من ج ب بالفعل لا دائماً» يرجع إلى قولنا: «كل ج ب بالفعل، هو لا ب بالفعل». و إذا رجعت المركبة إلى موجبة واحدة بسيطة، فنقيض تلك الموجبة يساوي نقيض المركبة. فنقيض المركبة - سواء كانت المركبة موجبة أو سالبة - يكون أبداً سالبة مناقضة للجزء الأول في الجهة و الكم.
Reduction of compound modal propositions to simple affirmatives. And that is because every compound - whether universal or particular, affirmative or negative - reduces to a single affirmative proposition. For example: the first part of the compound is made a subject restricted by the negation of the predicate, and the predicate is the predicate, if the compound is affirmative. And the subject is made restricted by the predicate, and the predicate is made the negation of the predicate, if it is negative. 1. Our statement 'Every J is B not always' reduces to our statement 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually'. And our statement 'No J is B actually not always' reduces to our statement 'Every J that is B actually is not B actually'. And when the compound reduces to a single simple affirmative, then the contradictory of that affirmative equals the contradictory of the compound. Thus the contradictory of the compound - whether the compound is affirmative or negative - is always a negative contradictory to the first part in modality and quantity.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage defines a method for reducing compound modal propositions (those with temporal or modal qualifiers like 'not always') to simple affirmative propositions. The author explains that every compound, regardless of quantity (universal/particular) or quality (affirmative/negative), can be recast as a single affirmative. The procedure: for affirmative compounds, the subject is restricted by the negation of the predicate (i.e., 'J that is not B'), and the predicate remains the same. For negative compounds, the subject is restricted by the predicate (i.e., 'J that is B'), and the predicate is replaced by its negation. Two examples are given: 'Every J is B not always' becomes 'Every J that is not B actually is B actually'; 'No J is B actually not always' becomes 'Every J that is B actually is not B actually'. After reduction, the contradictory (naqid) of the resulting simple affirmative is exactly the contradictory of the original compound, so one can find the contradictory of a compound by first reducing it. The author emphasizes that the contradictory is always a negative proposition that opposes the first part in modality (modal force) and quantity (universal/particular). This technique is foundational for handling modal logic in the Aristotelian tradition.

Key Takeaway
Compound modal propositions reduce to simple affirmatives by restricting the subject, and their contradictory is the contradictory of the reduced form.

Finding the Contradictory of Existential Non-Permanent Propositions
Examples and general rule for contradictory of existential non-permanent propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the general rule to find the contradictory of any existential non-permanent compound proposition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound modal proposition requires reduction and a precise rule for subject restriction.

This passage gives concrete examples of how to find the contradictory (naqid) of existential non-permanent propositions (wujudiyya la da'ima). These are compound modal propositions that assert existence but not permanence. The author provides a clear step-by-step method and a general principle (dabit) that you can apply to any such proposition. Understanding this process is essential for correctly negating statements in logical arguments, especially in Islamic sciences where temporal qualifications are common.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the quality of the proposition
Determine whether the original compound proposition is affirmative (positive) or negative. This is the first fork in the rule.
The passage treats 'Every J is B not always' as affirmative, and 'No J is B actually not always' as negative.
2
2. Reduce the compound if needed
If the proposition includes temporal qualifiers like 'ma dam J' (as long as it is J), translate it into a simpler form by moving the temporal condition into the subject restriction. For example, 'Some J is B as long as it is J not always' becomes 'Some J that is not B is B as long as it is J'.
This reduction ensures the proposition fits the pattern for applying the rule.
3
3. Apply the general rule for affirmatives or negatives
If the original is affirmative, construct a proposition whose subject is the original subject restricted by the negation of the predicate, and its predicate is the same as the original predicate. If negative, construct a proposition whose subject is the original subject restricted by the predicate, and its predicate is the negation of the original predicate.
The resulting proposition must also contradict the original in modality and quantity, often shifting from universal to particular or vice versa.
4
4. Verify the result is a valid contradictory
Check that the resulting proposition cannot be true at the same time as the original and cannot both be false. The examples show that the contradictories are exact opposites in truth conditions.
For instance, the contradictory of 'Every J is B not always' is 'It is not that some J that is not B actually is B always'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the contradictory of our statement 'No J is B actually not always' equals our statement 'It is not that some J that is B actually is not B always'. Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples of how to find the contradictory (naqid) of compound modal propositions that are existential non-permanent (wujudiyya la da'ima). It begins by showing that the contradictory of 'Every J is B not always' is 'It is not that some J that is not B actually is B always' – note the shift from universal to particular and from affirmative to negative. The contradictory of 'No J is B actually not always' is similarly 'It is not that some J that is B actually is not B always'. Two more examples deal with propositions qualified by 'as long as it is J not always'. For instance, 'Some J is B as long as it is J not always' is reduced to 'Some J that is not B is B as long as it is J', and its contradictory is 'No J that is not B is B when it is J'. The pattern follows the earlier reduction method: the compound is first reduced to a simple affirmative, then its contradictory is taken, and the result is a negative proposition with the subject restricted accordingly. Finally, the author states a general rule (al-dabit al-kulli) that summarizes the procedure: for an affirmative compound, construct a proposition whose subject is the original subject restricted by the negation of the predicate, and the predicate is the same; for a negative compound, the subject is restricted by the predicate and the predicate is negated. This rule ensures the contradictory opposes the original in modality and quantity. A marginal note indicates that Qutb al-Din al-Razi misunderstood this method for universals, referring to his commentary on Mataliʿ al-Anwar.

Contradiction of Conditionals: The Unity Condition
Author extends contradiction rules to conditionals with unity condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of conditional connections and apply the unity condition to determine whether two conditionals are contradictories.

Core Idea
Conditionals contradict only if they share the same type of connection.

The rules for contradicting conditional propositions largely mirror those for categoricals, but with a crucial addition. The two propositions must agree in the mode of connection—whether it is one of necessary implication (luzūm), incompatibility ('inād), or mere agreement (ittifāq). This ensures that we are comparing apples to apples. Without this condition, we might mistakenly treat two conditionals with different logical forces as contradictories when they are not.

Guided Path
1
Recall categorical contradiction rules
For categorical propositions, contradiction requires opposite truth value, same subject and predicate, and same quantity and quality. These are the baseline conditions.
This lesson extends these to conditionals.
2
Identify the three conditional connection types
Luzūm (implication) means the antecedent necessitates the consequent. 'Inād (incompatibility) means both cannot be true together. Ittifāq (agreement) means both are true together or both false together.
These are the three types of 'connective' in Arabic logic.
3
State the added condition
When finding a contradictory for a conditional, you must match the connection type. A conditional of luzūm can only contradict another conditional of luzūm, not one of 'inād or ittifāq.
Unity in the connection is required.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider: 'If Zaid is human, then he is mortal' (luzūm). Its contradictory could be 'If Zaid is human, then he is not mortal' (still luzūm) — but only if the truth values align. More precisely, the contradictory must be a proposition that is false when the first is true and vice versa, while preserving the connection type.
The example illustrates matching connection types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و أما الشرطيات فتناقضها كتناقض الحمليات في الشرائط، إلا أنه يشترط فيها الاتحاد في اللزوم والعناد والاتفاق.
As for conditionals, their contradiction is like the contradiction of categoricals in conditions, except that in them unity is required in implication (luzūm), incompatibility ('inād), and agreement (ittifāq).
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage extends the rules of contradiction from categorical propositions (ḥamliyyāt) to conditional propositions (sharṭiyyāt). The author states that the same general conditions apply, but with an additional requirement that when constructing a contradictory for a conditional, the two propositions must agree in the type of connection: whether it is a conditional of implication (luzūm), incompatibility ('inād), or mere agreement (ittifāq). This ensures logical consistency.

اللزوم

الاتفاق

Conditionals require that their contradictories share the same type of connection—implication, incompatibility, or agreement—alongside the standard conditions.

Existential Import and the Problem of Contradiction in External Propositions
Author raises a problem with contradictories when subject is non-existent in external negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical problem of existential import in external propositions and determine when contradictory pairs can both be false.

Core Idea
External negatives require subject existence; empty subjects break the square of opposition.

You are already familiar with the square of opposition, which tells us that contradictory propositions cannot both be false. But what happens when the subject of an external proposition does not exist? This passage exposes a hidden condition: if external negatives require subject existence, then when the subject is empty, both the universal affirmative and its contradictory particular negative become false. This challenges the universality of the square and reveals the importance of existential import in logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand external propositions and existential import
External propositions (al-qaḍāyā al-khārijiyya) only consider actually existent individuals of the subject. This means they have existential import: the subject must exist for the proposition to be about something.
Contrast with ḥaqīqiyya propositions, which include possible as well as actual individuals.
2
Recall the contradictory pairs in the square of opposition
In the square, two propositions are contradictories if they cannot both be true and cannot both be false. For categoricals: universal affirmative (A) contradicts particular negative (O); universal negative (E) contradicts particular affirmative (I).
Normally, exactly one of a contradictory pair is true.
3
Apply the existential condition to a contradictory pair
For external propositions, the truth of a negative proposition also requires the subject to exist. So both the universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every S is P') and its contradictory particular negative ('Some S is not P') require S to exist for them to be true. If S does not exist, both are false: the universal affirmative is false because there is no S to have the property, and the particular negative is false because there is no S that lacks the property.
4
Identify the violation of the law of contradiction
Since both can be false when S does not exist, the relationship is no longer contradictory. This shows that the square of opposition only holds when existential import is uniform across all propositions. The author points to this problem without resolving it here.
This problem is often dealt with by distinguishing external from non-external readings or by limiting the square to propositions with existential commitment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و هاهنا بحث: و هو أنه: ١. إن شرط في السالبة الخارجية وجود الموضوع، فيجوز ارتفاع الموجبة الكلية مع السالبة الجزئية، و السالبة الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية، لو كان الموضوع معدوماً.
And here is a discussion: namely that: 1. If existence of the subject is required in the external negative, then it is possible for the universal affirmative and the particular negative to both be false, and the universal negative and particular affirmative to both be false, if the subject is non-existent.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage introduces a problem (baḥth) regarding contradiction in external propositions (al-qaḍāyā al-khārijiyya). The issue arises when we consider external negative propositions (like 'No J is B' externally) that presuppose the existence of the subject. If the subject does not exist, then both the universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every J is B') and the particular negative ('Some J is not B') could be false simultaneously, violating the law of contradiction which requires exactly one to be true. Similarly, the universal negative and particular affirmative could both be false. This is a known problem in logic regarding existential import, and the author points it out without resolving it here.

Key Terms
الخارجية
السالبة الخارجية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Some jinn is visible and some jinn is not visible
D) No jinn is visible and not every jinn is visible

Key Takeaway
External negatives require subject existence; when the subject does not exist, contradictory pairs can both be false, challenging the universal applicability of the square of opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Negation of Conditional Propositions: The Three Unities
States the rules for contradicting conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the additional conditions for contradicting a conditional proposition and apply them to a given conditional from Islamic theology or jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Negating a conditional requires preserving implication, opposition, and agreement.

In categorical logic, contradicting a proposition requires unity in subject, predicate, time, place, etc. But when you negate a conditional statement—like 'If the world is created, it has a creator'—you must also keep the same relation of implication (luzum), opposition ('inad), and contextual agreement (ittifaq). This lesson unpacks these three extra conditions.

Guided Path
1
Recall standard contradiction conditions
For categorical propositions, contradiction requires sameness of subject, predicate, time, place, and relation. For conditionals, the antecedent and consequent take the place of subject and predicate; these same conditions still apply.
2
Identify the three extra conditions
The text adds three: al-ittihad fi al-luzum (unity in implication: the if-then link must be identical), al-ittihad fi al-‘inad (unity in opposition: the consequent must be denied exactly as it appears in the original), and al-ittihad fi al-ittifaq (unity in agreement: all contextual factors like time and place must match).
Al-‘inad applies especially to disjunctive conditionals, but here it refers to the overall opposition between antecedent and negated consequent.
3
Construct the contradictory for a kalam example
Take 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator.' Its contradictory is 'The world is temporally originated and it does not have a creator.' Check that it preserves the same antecedent, denies the consequent, and keeps the same context (same world, same origination, same time).
Notice that the contradictory of a conditional is a conjunctive proposition, not another conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقيض الشرطيات. و أما الشرطيات فتناقضها كتناقض الحمليات في الشرائط، إلا أنه يشترط فيها الاتحاد في اللزوم والعناد والاتفاق.
The negation of conditionals. As for conditional propositions, their contradiction is like the contradiction of categorical propositions in conditions, except that in them unity in implication, opposition, and agreement is required.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage defines how to form the contradictory (naqid) of conditional propositions (al-shartiyyat). It states that the conditions for contradiction are the same as for categorical (hamli) propositions, but with additional requirements: the contradictory must be united with the original in terms of implication (luzum), opposition ('inad), and agreement (ittifaq). For example, if the original is 'If it rains, the ground is wet,' its contradictory cannot be simply 'It rains and the ground is not wet' unless the same implication, opposition, and agreement are preserved. In Islamic logic, conditionals like 'If the world is originated, it has a creator' require careful matching of terms.

Key Terms
الشروط
اللزوم
العناد
الاتفاق

Distinction Between Naqid and Musawi al-Naqid
Clarifies the difference between a true contradictory and a proposition that merely equals it in logical effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the strict contradictory (naqīḍ) and its logical equivalent (musāwī al-naqīḍ) in propositional logic and explain why logicians prefer the equivalent.

Core Idea
True contradictory is bare negation; logicians use an equivalent for convenience.

In Islamic logic, a proposition's true contradictory is simply its negation—nothing more. But when constructing proofs, logicians often use a different proposition that is logically equivalent to that negation, because bare negation can be hard to handle in conversions and syllogisms. This lesson clarifies that distinction and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of naqid
The text defines naqid as 'رفعه فقط من غير قيد و اعتبار آخر' (bare negation without any restriction). Understand that this applies to both terms and whole propositions.
2
Recognize the practical problem
Logicians need the contradictory for proofs, but bare negation is difficult to work with in operations like conversion (aks) and syllogisms (aqyisa).
3
Understand the substitute
They use a proposition that equals (musawi) the contradictory—it is not the bare negation, but it has the same truth conditions for logical operations. For example, for 'Every A is B', the naqid is 'Not every A is B' and the musawi is 'Some A is not B'.
4
Apply the distinction
When you encounter a claimed 'contradictory' in a logical text, check whether it is the strict naqid or the operational musawi. This helps you assess the validity of later steps.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: الفرق بين النقيض و مساوي النقيض. اعلم أن نقيض الشيء هو رفعه فقط من غير قيد و اعتبار آخر، سواء كان الشيء قضية أو مفرداً. فما ذكروا في نقيض القضايا ليس شيء منها نقيضاً، بل مساوياً للنقيض. و ذلك لأنهم لما احتاجوا في البراهين إلى الاستدلال بنقيض القضايا على مطالبهم فحصلوا لكل قضيّة قضية تساوي نقيضها ليصح عليها طريان الأحكام في العكوس و الأقيسة و غيرها لصعوبة طريانها على الرفع المجرد.
A note: The difference between the contradictory and the equal of the contradictory. Know that the contradictory of a thing is its negation alone without any other restriction or consideration, whether the thing is a proposition or a simple term. So what they mention as the contradictory of propositions—none of them is a contradictory, but rather an equal of the contradictory. That is because when they needed in proofs to infer by the contradictory of propositions for their demands, they obtained for each proposition a proposition that equals its contradictory so that the application of rulings in conversions, syllogisms, and others becomes valid, due to the difficulty of applying them to the bare negation.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between the strict contradictory (naqid) of a proposition—its pure negation—and what logicians commonly use, which is actually a proposition that 'equals' the contradictory (musawi al-naqid). The author explains that the true contradictory of 'Every A is B' would be 'Not every A is B' (a bare negation), but logicians find it easier to use 'Some A is not B' (the particular negative) because it has the same truth conditions for logical operations like conversion and syllogism. In Islamic logical practice, this substitution is standard and does not affect the validity of arguments. For example, in the proof that the world is created, the contradictory of 'The world is eternal' is simply 'The world is not eternal,' but logicians may use 'The world is temporally originated' as an equivalent for argumentation.

Key Terms

مساوي النقيض
العكوس

Key Takeaway
The true contradictory is bare negation; logicians often use a logically equivalent proposition for practical convenience in proofs.

Problem of Contradiction in External Propositions
Raises a difficulty about the conditions for contradiction when the subject term refers to external existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two horns of the dilemma regarding external propositions and explain why each horn violates a law of contradiction.

Core Idea
External propositions about nonexistent subjects expose a flaw in standard contradiction conditions.

This passage presents a deep logical problem that arises when we consider propositions about subjects that have no external existence, such as 'partner of God'. The standard conditions for contradiction seem to fail in such cases. Let us examine the dilemma and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting conditions
The passage presents two alternative conditions for the negative external proposition: either existence of the subject is required or it is not. Recognize that these are mutually exclusive options.
2
Analyze the first horn: existence required for negative
If existence is required, then when the subject is nonexistent (e.g., 'partner of God'), the universal affirmative and the particular negative can both be false. This violates the law of excluded middle (one must be true).
The universal affirmative is false because there is no subject, and the particular negative is false because there is no subject to negate.
3
Analyze the second horn: existence not required
If existence is not required, then the subject of the negative becomes more general (including nonexistents), so the universal affirmative and the particular negative can both be true. This violates the law of non-contradiction (both cannot be true).
The universal affirmative is vacuously true (no counterexample), and the particular negative is also true because some nonexistent subject does not have the predicate.
4
Determine the consequences for the laws of contradiction
Each horn undermines one of the two fundamental laws of formal logic: the first horn violates the law of excluded middle, the second violates the law of non-contradiction. Thus, the standard definition of contradiction fails for external propositions with nonexistent subjects.
5
Relate to Islamic theological examples
Apply the dilemma to statements about impossible entities like 'a second god' or 'an eternal created being'. Recognize that Islamic theologians (mutakallimun) must be careful with such propositions to avoid logical inconsistencies in arguments about tawhid.
Later logicians resolved this by distinguishing external, true, and mental propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و هاهنا بحث: و هو أنه: ١. إن شرط في السالبة الخارجية وجود الموضوع ، فيجوز ارتفاع الموجبة الكلية مع السالبة الجزئية، و السالبة الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية، لو كان الموضوع معدوماً. ٢. و إن لم يُشترط ، يلزم أن يكون موضوع السالبة أعم من موضوع الموجبة، الجواز السلب عن المعدوم حالة العدم، فيجوز صدق الموجبة الكلية مع السالبة الجزئية، فينتفي التناقض.
And here there is an investigation: It is that: 1. If existence of the subject is required in the external negative, then it is possible for the universal affirmative and the particular negative to both be false, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative, if the subject is nonexistent. 2. And if it is not required, then the subject of the negative would be more general than the subject of the affirmative, due to the permissibility of negating about the nonexistent in the state of nonexistence, so it becomes possible for the universal affirmative and the particular negative to be true together, so contradiction is negated.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents a problem (shakk) concerning external propositions (al-qadaya al-kharijiyya), which are about actually existing individuals. The issue is about the condition of subject existence in the negative proposition. If existence is required for the negative, then when the subject is nonexistent (e.g., 'Every partner of God is knowing'), both the universal affirmative and the particular negative could be false—violating the law of excluded middle. If existence is not required, then the subject of the negative becomes more general (including nonexistents), so the universal affirmative and particular negative could both be true—violating the law of non-contradiction. In Islamic theology, this problem arises with impossible subjects like 'partner of God', which have no external existence. The author exposes a flaw in the standard theory of contradiction when applied to external propositions.

Key Terms

السالبة الخارجية
ارتفاع

Key Takeaway
The contradiction conditions for external propositions require careful handling of subject existence; failing to do so undermines the laws of logic.
