The Dilemma of Contradiction in Haqiqiyya Propositions
Extends the previous problem to propositions about possible individuals, with a similar dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dilemma of contradiction in haqiqiyya propositions and determine whether the condition of possibility is required in the negative.

Core Idea
The law of contradiction can break when the subject's individuals are merely possible.

In the previous lesson, we saw how external propositions about non-existent individuals can undermine contradiction. The same problem arises for haqiqiyya propositions, which deal with possible individuals. This passage shows that the condition of possibility creates a dilemma: either both affirmative and negative can be false, or both can be true. Understanding this helps us see the limits of logical principles when applied to non-actual domains.

Guided Path
1
Understand haqiqiyya propositions
Recall that haqiqiyya propositions are about individuals that are possible in existence, regardless of actual existence. The subject's individuals must be possible (imkan).
Example: 'Every human is rational' is haqiqiyya if 'human' includes possible humans.
2
Scenario A: Condition required in negative
If the condition of possibility is also required for the negative proposition, then when the subject is impossible (e.g., 'partner of God'), both the affirmative and negative can be false because neither applies to any possible individual.
Example: 'Every partner of God is knowing' and 'Some partner of God is not knowing' both false.
3
Scenario B: Condition not required in negative
If possibility is not required for the negative, then the subject of the negative is broader (includes impossible individuals), so the negative could be true while the affirmative is false, or both could be true in some cases.
This mirrors the earlier problem in external propositions.
4
Recognize the dilemma
In either case, the law of contradiction fails: both propositions can be false together (Scenario A) or both can be true together (Scenario B). This shows that contradiction is not absolute for haqiqiyya propositions.
This is a critical refinement of standard logical theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك في الحقيقية - إن شرط الإمكان في أفراد الموضوع - فإنه: ١. إن شرط ذلك في السالبة أيضاً، فيجوز ارتفاعها فيها يمتنع الموضوع، كقولنا: «كل شريك البارئ عالم» «بعض شريك البارئ ليس بعالم». ٢. و إن لم يشترط كان موضوع السالبة أعم من موضوع الموجبة، و يلزم ما مر في الخارجية.
And similarly in the true proposition—if possibility of the individuals of the subject is required—then: 1. If that is required in the negative also, then it is possible for them to be false when the subject is impossible, like our saying: 'Every partner of God is knowing,' 'Some partner of God is not knowing.' 2. And if it is not required, the subject of the negative would be more general than the subject of the affirmative, and what passed in the external applies.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage raises an analogous problem for 'true' propositions (al-qadaya al-haqiqiyya), which are about possible individuals (i.e., individuals that could exist). The condition here is possibility (imkan) of the subject's individuals. If possibility is required in the negative, then when the subject is impossible (like 'partner of God'), both affirmative and negative can be false (since neither applies to a nonexistent possible). If not required, the negative's subject is broader, allowing both true—again breaking contradiction. The example 'Every partner of God is knowing' and its contradictory 'Some partner of God is not knowing' both are false because there is no possible partner of God. This challenges the universality of the law of contradiction.

Key Terms
القضايا الحقيقية
الإمكان
شريك البارئ

Key Takeaway

Why Adding Unity of Subject Fails
Responds to an objection about unity of subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why introducing 'unity of subject' as a condition for contradiction breaks the absoluteness of logical rules and invalidates standard conversions.

Core Idea
Restricting propositions destroys logical universality.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection: why not simply require that the subject term be identical in both contradictory propositions? While it seems like a neat solution, the author shows that this extra restriction would undermine the very foundations of logical reasoning. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector suggests that requiring unity of subject (ittihad al-mawdu) resolves the earlier doubts about contradictory propositions in logic. According to the objector, if we ensure the same subject concept is used, the contradiction becomes valid.
This seems plausible but introduces an extra condition.
2
Understand the author's counter
The author (shaykh) replies that this unity is an extra restriction (qayd) on the concept of the proposition, and the contradictory of a restricted proposition is not the contradictory of the absolute proposition.
The restriction changes the logical form itself.
3
Trace the consequences
If restrictions are allowed, many standard logical rules (such as conversion, al-aks) would be invalidated because those rules assume absoluteness. Moreover, we could mistakenly allow contradiction between two particular propositions, which is logically invalid.
Particular propositions (juz'iyyat) cannot be true contradictories.
4
Draw the conclusion
Thus, observing absoluteness (itlaq) is necessary in logical judgments. The original doubts remain because the proposed solution fails without destroying logic itself.
The author reaffirms that logical rules are universal and cannot be arbitrarily restricted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: لا يشترط وجود الموضوع في السالبة الخارجية و لا إمكانه في الحقيقية. لكن يُعتبر اتحاد الموضوع، وحينئذ يسقط ما ذكرتم. قلت: هذا قيد زائد على مفهوم القضية و نقيض المقيد لا يكون نقيض المطلق. و أبحاث المنطق جارية على مطلقها؛ لأنه لو جوزوا القيد لبطل كثير من الأحكام في العكوس وغيرها و لجوزوا التناقض بين الجزئيتين. و ليس كذلك. فعلم أنّ رعاية الإطلاق واجبة في أحكام المنطق فتتوجه الشكوك.
For if you say: Existence of the subject is not required in the external negative nor its possibility in the true (proposition). But the unity of the subject is considered, and then what you mentioned falls away. I say: This is an additional restriction on the concept of the proposition, and the contradictory of the restricted is not the contradictory of the absolute. And the investigations of logic proceed on their absoluteness; because if they allowed the restriction, many of the rulings in conversions and others would be invalidated, and they would allow contradiction between two particular propositions. And that is not the case. Thus it is known that observing absoluteness is necessary in the judgments of logic, so the doubts remain.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (objection and answer). The objector says: In external and haqiqiyya propositions, we do not require existence or possibility, but we require 'unity of subject' (ittihad al-mawdu‘) meaning the same concept is used in both affirmative and negative. That would solve the problem because the subject is identical. The author (as the shaykh) replies: That unity is extra restriction on the concept of the proposition; the contradictory of a restricted proposition is not the same as the contradictory of the absolute. Logical rules are supposed to apply absolutely; if we add restrictions, many standard rules of conversion and syllogism (like obversion, conversion) would break, and we could even allow contradictions between two particular propositions (which is invalid). Therefore, the absolute (muthlaq) must be preserved, and the original doubts remain valid. This highlights a key principle: logical rules are universal and cannot be saved by ad hoc restrictions.

Key Terms
اتحاد الموضوع
المقيد
المطلق
الجزئيتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it would make logical rules too strict

C) Because it is impossible to achieve
D) Because it only applies to conditional propositions

Key Takeaway
Logical rules must apply to absolute propositions; adding restrictions like unity of subject destroys their universality and cannot rescue the system from doubts.

Classification of Conditional Propositions
Author defines conditional propositions (shartiyya) as muttasila and munfasila with positive and negative forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional proposition into its correct type (muttasila or munfasila with subtypes) based on the pattern of truth or falsity.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions split into connected (if-then) and disjoined (either-or), with three subtypes of disjunction.

In this passage, the author defines the two main families of conditional propositions. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing conditional syllogisms later, especially in Islamic legal reasoning where conditional statements appear in contracts, obligations, and theological arguments. By parsing the types, you will be able to recognize the logical structure behind many Qur'anic and hadith-based conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
The author states that a conditional proposition (shartiyya) is either connected (muttasila) or disjoined (munfasila). Note that this division is exhaustive: every conditional falls into one of these two categories.
Connected conditionals are often expressed with 'if...then...', while disjoined conditionals are expressed with 'either...or...'.
2
Distinguish affirmative and negative for connected conditionals
A connected conditional may affirm the connection (e.g., 'If A then B') or negate it (e.g., 'If A then not B'). Identify which is the affirmative and which is the negative form.
The affirmative asserts that when the antecedent holds, the consequent holds; the negative asserts that when the antecedent holds, the consequent does not hold.
3
Understand the three kinds of disjoined conditionals
The disjoined conditional asserts incompatibility (tabayun) between two propositions. The author lists three ways this incompatibility can occur: (1) in both existence and non-existence (haqiqiyya), (2) only in existence (mani'at al-jam'), (3) only in non-existence (mani'at al-khuluw). Study the definitions carefully.
Haarithiyya means they cannot be both true nor both false; mani'at al-jam' means they cannot be both true but may both be false; mani'at al-khuluw means they cannot be both false but may both be true.
4
Recognize how negation flips the type
Just as with connected conditionals, each type of disjoined conditional can be negated. The author notes that negating the incompatibility gives the negative form. For example, a negative real disjoined conditional would allow both truth and both falsity (i.e., it denies the exclusive relation).
Negation of a disjoined conditional tends to be less common in discourse, but it is important for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرطية: إما متصلة و هي التي حكم فيها بثبوت قضية على تقدير ثبوت أخرى، أو بسلب هذا الثبوت. و الأول هو الموجبة و الثاني هو السالبة. و إما منفصلة و هي التي حكم فيها بتباين قضية لأخرى: إما وجوداً و عدماً، و تسمى «حقيقية» أو وجوداً فقط و تسمى «مانعة الجمع»، أو عدماً فقط و تسمى «مانعة الخلق» أو بسلب هذا التباين و الأول موجبة و الثاني سالبة.
The conditional: either connected, which is the judgment affirming a proposition on the condition of affirming another, or negating this affirmation. The first is affirmative and the second is negative. Or disjoined, which is the judgment of the incompatibility of one proposition with another: either in existence and non-existence, and it is called 'real', or only in existence and it is called 'manifold of conjunction', or only in non-existence and it is called 'manifold of disjunction', or by negating this incompatibility, and the first is affirmative and the second is negative.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage defines the two main types of conditional propositions (shartiyya) in logic. The first is the connected conditional (muttasila), where judgment is made about the truth or falsity of a consequent (tali) given the truth of an antecedent (muqaddam). If the judgment affirms the connection, it is affirmative (mujiba); if it negates it, it is negative (saliba). The second type is the disjoined conditional (munfasila), where judgment is made about the incompatibility (tabayun) of two propositions. The incompatibility can be of three kinds: (1) complete incompatibility in both truth and falsity, called real (haqiqiyya); (2) incompatibility only in truth, meaning they cannot both be true but may both be false, called manifold of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'); (3) incompatibility only in falsity, meaning they cannot both be false but may both be true, called manifold of disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw). If the judgment negates any of these incompatibilities, it becomes a negative disjoined conditional.

Key Terms
شرطية
متصلة
منفصلة
حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Real (haqiqiyya)
B) Mani'at al-jam'
C) Mani'at al-khuluw
D) Negative disjoined

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are fundamentally of two types: connected (if-then) and disjoined (either-or), with disjunction further divided into three types based on the pattern of possible truth and falsity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Take the statement from fiqh: 'If the water is pure, then it is permissible to use it for wudu.' Express this as a conditional proposition and identify whether it is muttasila or munfasila, and whether it is affirmative or negative. Explain your reasoning.
?
Task 2: Consider the claim: 'The debtor must either repay the debt or have it forgiven.' Which subtype of disjoined conditional (haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', or mani'at al-khuluw) best represents this obligation? Justify your answer based on the definitions in this lesson.

Parts of Conditional Propositions: Muqaddam, Tali, Malzum, Lazim
Author names the parts of conditionals and describes their possible relationships in terms of shared terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent (muqaddam/malzum) and consequent (tali/lazim) in any conditional proposition and classify the relationship between them in terms of shared terms.

Core Idea
Antecedent and consequent are the building blocks of every conditional argument; knowing their relationship determines logical dependency.

In this passage, the author names the two essential parts of any conditional proposition: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tali). He then introduces a special terminology for affirmative connected conditionals expressing implication (luzum): the antecedent is called malzum and the consequent lazim. Most importantly, he classifies the possible relationships between the antecedent and consequent based on whether they share both, one, or none of their constituent terms. This classification is essential for evaluating the validity of conditional syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universal parts
Recognize that every conditional proposition, whether connected (mutasil) or disjunctive (munfasil), has a first part called muqaddam and a second called tali. In Arabic, 'الجزء الأول' and 'الثاني'.
This holds for all conditionals.
2
Note the special case of implication conditionals
When the conditional is affirmative, connected, and expresses necessary implication (luzum), the antecedent is called malzum and the consequent lazim. This emphasizes the causal or necessary connection.
The terms malzum and lazim are specific to this type.
3
Extract the three possible relations
The author lists three scenarios for the relationship between the antecedent and consequent: sharing both parts, sharing one part, or sharing no parts. For each, an example is given.
The relation is about the subject and predicate terms of the antecedent and consequent.
4
Match examples to each relation
For sharing both terms: 'استلزام القضية عكسها المستوي' (implication of a proposition by its simple converse). For sharing one term: 'استلزام ثبوت محمول لشيء ثبوت محمول آخر له' (implication of one predicate for a thing implying another predicate for the same thing). For no shared terms: 'استلزام القضية عكس نقيضها' (implication of the converse of its contradictory).
These examples illustrate the three categories.
5
Practice parsing
Given any conditional proposition, identify the antecedent and consequent, then check whether they share any subject or predicate terms.
The example from Usul al-Fiqh: 'If an analogy is valid, then its ruling applies.' Here, 'analogy is valid' and 'its ruling applies' share the term 'analogy' (subject), so they share one term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الجزء الأول من الشرطية مطلقاً يسمى «مقدماً» و الثاني «تالياً»، و من المتصلة الموجبة اللزومية «ملزوماً» و «لازماً». و المقدم و التالي: إما أن يتشاركا بطرفيها، كاستلزام القضية عكسها المستوي .. أو بأحد طرفيهما، كاستلزام ثبوت محمول لشيء ثبوت محمول آخر له، .. أو يتباينا فيهما، كاستلزام القضية عكس نقيضها.
And the first part of the conditional absolutely is called 'antecedent' and the second 'consequent', and in the affirmative connected conditional of implication, they are called 'implied' and 'implicant'. And the antecedent and consequent: either they share both their terms, as the implication of a proposition of its simple converse; or share one of their terms, as the implication of the affirmation of a predicate for something to the affirmation of another predicate for it; or they differ in both, as the implication of a proposition of the converse of its contradictory.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage identifies the parts of any conditional proposition. The first part is universally called the antecedent (muqaddam) and the second the consequent (tali). In the specific case of an affirmative connected conditional that expresses implication (luzum), the antecedent is called the malzum (that which implies) and the consequent the lazim (the implication). The passage then explains the possible relationships between the antecedent and consequent in terms of their constituent terms (for simple subject-predicate propositions inside the conditional). These are: (1) They share both terms, as when a proposition implies its own simple converse (e.g., 'If every A is B, then some B is A'). (2) They share only one term, as when the affirmation of one predicate for a subject implies the affirmation of another predicate for the same subject (e.g., 'If Zayd is walking, then Zayd is moving'). (3) They share no terms, as when a proposition implies the converse of its contradictory (e.g., 'If every A is B, then it is not the case that some A is not B'). These categories are crucial for analyzing the validity of conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
مقدم
تالي
ملزوم
لازم

A) Muqaddam
B) Malzum
C) Tali
D) Lazim

Key Takeaway
The antecedent and consequent of a conditional can be either the universal terms muqaddam/tali or the implication-specific malzum/lazim, and their relationship (shared terms) determines the logical connection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

They share the term 'forbidden' as predicate? But careful: The antecedent's predicate is 'forbidden' and consequent's predicate is 'forbidden' as well. So they share the predicate 'forbidden'. So they share one term.)

General and Specific Interpretations of Disjoined Conditionals
Author expands on mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw with broader and narrower interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general and specific interpretations of mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw, and explain their relationship to the real disjunction.

Core Idea
The same disjunction term can include or exclude the real type depending on interpretation.

In this passage, the author clarifies that the two types of disjoined conditionals—mani'at al-jam' (incompatibility of conjunction) and mani'at al-khuluw (incompatibility of disjunction)—each have a broader and a narrower interpretation. Understanding these interpretations is essential for correctly applying disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic disputation and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two base types
Recognize mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw as two distinct types of disjoined conditionals. The former prohibits both being true, the latter prohibits both being false.
2
Understand the general interpretation
For each type, note the general definition that does not impose extra conditions. For jam', it only denies both true, without addressing falsehood. For khuluw, it only denies both false, without addressing truth. This interpretation is broader than the real type.
3
Understand the specific interpretation
The specific interpretation adds an extra condition: for jam', both may be false; for khuluw, both may be true. This makes each incompatible with the real type.
4
Relate to the real type
The general interpretation includes the real type (haqiqiyya) because it allows the possibility of one true, one false. The specific interpretation excludes it by allowing both false (jam') or both true (khuluw).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو وجوداً فقط - أي يحكم بأنهما لا يصدقان معاً من غير التعرض إلى أنهما يكذبان أو لا يكذبان - و تسمى مانعة الجمع. و هي هذا التفسير أعم من الحقيقية. وقد فسروها بأخص من هذا، وهي أن يحكم فيها بأنهما لا تصدقان معاً لكن يجوز كذبهما وهي بهذا التفسير مباينة للحقيقية. أو عدماً فقط - أي يحكم بأنهما لا يكذبان معاً من غير التعرض إلى أنهما يصدقان أو لا يصدقان - و تسمى مانعة الخلق. و هي هذا التفسير أعم من الحقيقية. وقد فسروها بأخض من ذلك، و هي أن يحكم فيها بأنهما لا يكذبان معاً لكن يجوز صدقهما، وهي بهذا التفسير مباينة للحقيقية. و هذان التفسيران صرح عما قبل المصنف نصير الدين الطوسي: «كل نوع ينقسم ثلاثة أقسام: حقيقية و مانعة جمع و مانعة خلق. والأخيران قد يؤخذان بسيطين يشملان الحقيقية و قد يؤخذان مركبين يكونان قسيمين لها.
Or only in existence - i.e., it judges that they are not both true without addressing whether they are both false or not - and it is called 'manifold of conjunction'. And this interpretation is more general than the real. And they interpreted it as more specific than this, which is that it judges that they are not both true but it is permissible that they both be false, and by this interpretation it is incompatible with the real. Or only in non-existence - i.e., it judges that they are not both false without addressing whether they are both true or not - and it is called 'manifold of disjunction'. And this interpretation is more general than the real. And they interpreted it as more specific than that, which is that it judges that they are not both false but it is permissible that they both be true, and by this interpretation it is incompatible with the real. And these two interpretations were declared previously by the author Nasir al-Din al-Tusi: 'Every type is divided into three divisions: real, manifold of conjunction, and manifold of disjunction. And the latter two may be taken as simple, including the real, or as compound, being divisions of it.'
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage provides detailed clarifications on the two types of disjoined conditionals mentioned earlier: mani'at al-jam' (incompatibility of conjunction) and mani'at al-khuluw (incompatibility of disjunction). For each, there are two interpretations: a more general (a'amm) and a more specific (akhass) sense. For mani'at al-jam', the general interpretation is that the two propositions are simply not true together, without stipulating anything about their falsity; in this sense it is broader than the real (haqiqiyya) because it allows the possibility of both being false. The specific interpretation adds that they may both be false, thus it is distinct from the real (which requires at least one true). Similarly, for mani'at al-khuluw, the general interpretation is that they are not both false, without addressing truth; this is broader than the real. The specific interpretation adds that they may both be true, making it distinct from the real. The author then references Nasir al-Din al-Tusi, who states that the three types (real, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khuluw) can be taken as simple (i.e., each including the others in a certain sense) or as compound (i.e., as distinct subdivisions). This distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing disjunctive syllogisms.

مانعة الخلق
تفسير أعم
تفسير أخص

Key Takeaway
The general interpretation of mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw includes the real type, while the specific interpretation excludes it.

Two Types of Disjunctive Conditionals: Maniat al-Jam' and Maniat al-Khulu
Author defines exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions and their two interpretations (absolute and restricted).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khulu and identify their broader and narrower interpretations.

Core Idea
Maniat al-jam' prevents both true; maniat al-khulu prevents both false.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive conditionals are essential for constructing arguments that eliminate possibilities. The author here distinguishes two fundamental types: maniat al-jam', which prevents the conjunction of truth, and maniat al-khulu, which prevents the conjunction of falsity. Each type has a broader interpretation (allowing the opposite possibility) and a narrower interpretation (excluding it). Understanding these nuances is critical for correctly applying disjunctive syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of disjunctives
The passage defines maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khulu. Notice their Arabic names and what they prevent: maniat al-jam' prevents both parts from being true together; maniat al-khulu prevents both from being false together.
These are the two basic types of disjunctive conditionals.
2
Understand the broader interpretation
For maniat al-jam', the broader interpretation only says 'they are not both true', remaining silent on whether they can be false together. For maniat al-khulu, it says 'they are not both false', silent on truth together.
The broader interpretation is more general because it does not specify the opposite case.
3
Understand the narrower interpretation
The narrower interpretation adds a condition: for maniat al-jam', it explicitly allows both false; for maniat al-khulu, it explicitly allows both true.
This narrower interpretation is 'contradictory to the haqiqiyya' (mubayana li-l-haqiqiyya).
4
Compare with the haqiqiyya disjunction
The haqiqiyya (true) disjunction requires both exclusivity and exhaustivity—exactly one part true. The broader interpretations include the haqiqiyya as a special case, while the narrower interpretations exclude it because they allow the opposite possibility (both false for maniat al-jam', both true for maniat al-khulu).
Knowing this helps avoid misapplying disjunctions in syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو وجوداً فقط - أي يحكم بأتهما لا يصدقان معاً من غير التعرض إلى أنهما يكذبان أو لا يكذبان - و تستى مانعة الجمع» . و هي هذا التفسير أعم من الحقيقية. وقد فسروها بأخص من هذا، وهي أن يحكم فيها بأنهما لا تصدقان معاً لكن يجوز كذعهما وهي بهذا التفسير مباينة للحقيقية. أو عدماً فقط - أي يحكم بأتهما لا يكذبان معاً من غير التعرض إلى أنهما يصدقان أو لا يصدقان - و تستى مانعة الخلق». و هي هذا التفسير أعم من الحقيقية. وقد فسروها بأخض من ذلك، وهي أن يحكم فيها بأنهما لا يكذبان معاً لكن يجوز صدقهما، وهي بهذا التفسير مباينة للحقيقية.
Or existence only - i.e., it is judged that they are not both true together without addressing whether they are both false or not - and it is called 'exclusive disjunction' (maniat al-jam'). And this interpretation is more general than the true (haqiqiyya). And they interpreted it more specifically than this: that it is judged that they are not both true together but it is permissible that they be both false, and by this interpretation it is contradictory to the true. Or non-existence only - i.e., it is judged that they are not both false together without addressing whether they are both true or not - and it is called 'exhaustive disjunction' (maniat al-khulu). And this interpretation is more general than the true. And they interpreted it more specifically than that: that it is judged that they are not both false together but it is permissible that they be both true, and by this interpretation it is contradictory to the true.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage defines two types of disjunctive conditionals (munfasila). The first is maniat al-jam' (exclusive disjunction), which asserts that the two parts cannot be true together, but remains silent on whether they can be false together. This broader interpretation covers all cases where at most one is true. A narrower interpretation adds that they can both be false, which makes it the opposite of the true conjunction (haqiqiyya). The second is maniat al-khulu (exhaustive disjunction), which asserts that the two parts cannot be false together, leaving open whether they can be true together. The broader interpretation covers all cases where at least one is true. The narrower interpretation adds that they can both be true, making it opposite of the true disjunction. The author notes that these narrower interpretations are 'contradictory to the real' (mubayana li-l-haqiqiyya), meaning they are distinct from the 'true' (haqiqiyya) disjunction which requires both exclusivity and exhaustivity. This classification is crucial for understanding the logical behavior of disjunctions in syllogistic reasoning.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The two parts cannot both be true

D) The two parts are necessarily both true

Key Takeaway
The broader interpretation of maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khulu covers all cases of at most one true and at least one true respectively, while the narrower interpretations contradict the haqiqiyya disjunction.

Classifying Conditionals by Relation
Author enumerates possible compositions of conditionals and defines types based on causal or contradictory relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional is luzumiyya, inadiyya, or ittifaqiyya based on the relationship between its parts.

Core Idea
The logical force of a conditional depends on necessary implication or opposition, not mere occurrence.

Not all conditionals are logically equal. Some have a necessary connection between their parts, while others are purely accidental. The classical logicians classified conditionals into luzumiyya (implicative), inadiyya (adversative), and ittifaqiyya (accidental) based on whether the antecedent is a cause, effect, or unrelated to the consequent. This lesson will help you recognise these types and understand their logical significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether the conditional is conjunctive (muttasila, 'if...then...') or disjunctive (munfasila, 'either...or...'). This is the first step because the classification criteria differ: conjunctives require necessary implication, disjunctives require necessary opposition.
2
Check for necessary relation
For conjunctives, ask: Does the antecedent necessarily imply the consequent? For disjunctives, ask: Are the two parts truly contradictory (i.e., one must be true and the other false)? If yes, the relation is necessary; if not, it is accidental.
Necessary relation arises when the antecedent is a cause of the consequent (or its opposite) or an effect of it or of its cause.
3
Classify accordingly
If the conditional is conjunctive and has necessary implication, it is luzumiyya. If it is disjunctive and has necessary opposition, it is inadiyya. Otherwise (no necessary relation), it is ittifaqiyya regardless of type.
Ittifaqiyya means the connection is merely accidental, like 'If fire exists, the sky is blue.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل من المتصلة والمنفصلة إما أن يتركب من: ١. حمليتين ٢. أو متصلتين ٣. أو منفصلتين ٤. أو حملية ومتصلة ٥. أو حملية و منفصلة ٦. أو متصلة و منفصلة. و لما كان: ١. المقدم في المتصلة متميزاً عن التالي بالطبع، لأن نسبته إلى التالي بالاستصحاب و نسبة التالي إليه بالمصاحبة ٢. دون المنفصلة، إذ نسبة كل منها إلى الآخر بالمنافاة و الامتياز انّما يكون بالوضع، صارت الأقسام في المتصلة تسعة، و في المنفصلة ستة. الشرطية: [المبحث الثاني] اللزومية و العنادية و الاتفاقية. إن كانت بين طرفيها علاقة تقتضي اقتضاء المقدم للتالي أو عناده إياه، تستى: a. المتصلة «لزومية»، b. و المنفصلة «عنادية». وإلا فـ«اتفاقية». و تلك العلاقة إنما تتحقق إن لو كان المقدم: ١ علة للتالي أو المقابله، ٢. أو كان معلولاً له، ٣. أو معلولاً لعلته.
And each of the conjunctive and disjunctive is either composed of: 1. two categoricals, 2. or two conjunctives, 3. or two disjunctives, 4. or a categorical and a conjunctive, 5. or a categorical and a disjunctive, 6. or a conjunctive and a disjunctive. And since: 1. the antecedent in the conjunctive is distinguished from the consequent by nature, because its relation to the consequent is by occurrence (istishab) and the relation of the consequent to it is by accompaniment (musahaba); 2. unlike the disjunctive, because the relation of each to the other is by contradiction (munafat) and the distinction is only by convention (wad'), the divisions in the conjunctive become nine, and in the disjunctive six. Conditionals: [Second investigation] The implicative, adversative, and accidental. If there is a relation between its two parts that necessitates the antecedent's implication of the consequent or its opposition to it, it is called: a. the conjunctive 'implicative' (luzumiyya), b. and the disjunctive 'adversative' (inadiyya). Otherwise it is 'accidental' (ittifaqiyya). And that relation is realized only if the antecedent is: 1. a cause for the consequent or its opposite, 2. or an effect of it, 3. or an effect of its cause.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage first enumerates the possible compositions of conditional propositions: both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals can be composed from two categoricals (hamliyyatan), two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical and a conjunctive, a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conjunctive and a disjunctive. Then it explains why the number of possible combinations differs: in a conjunctive conditional (muttasila), the antecedent is naturally distinguished from the consequent because the antecedent is assumed constant (istishab) while the consequent is concomitant (musahaba). In a disjunctive, the two parts are related by opposition, so the distinction is conventional. Hence, the conjunctive has 9 divisions and the disjunctive 6. Next, the author introduces a classification based on the relationship between the parts: if there is a necessary relation such that the antecedent implies the consequent (for conjunctives) or opposes it (for disjunctives), then the conjunctive is called luzumiyya and the disjunctive inadiyya. Otherwise they are called ittifaqiyya (accidental). The necessary relation holds if the antecedent is a cause of the consequent (or its opposite), or an effect of it, or an effect of its cause. This classification is essential for determining the validity of syllogisms involving conditionals.

Key Terms
لزومية
عنادية
اتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A disjunctive conditional where there is a necessary relation of opposition between the two parts is called:
A) Ittifaqiyya
B) Luzumiyya
C) Inadiyya
D) Haqiqiyya

Key Takeaway
A conditional is classified as luzumiyya (conjunctive) or inadiyya (disjunctive) if the parts are necessarily connected by cause-effect; otherwise it is ittifaqiyya (accidental).
